Evidence vs Confession: Scope of Binding Effect
Maxim distinguishing between the scope of evidence and confession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim distinguishing bayyina and iqrār and apply it to determine who is bound by each type of proof.

Core Idea
Bayyina binds all; iqrār binds only the confessor.

This short but powerful maxim captures a fundamental distinction between two forms of legal proof. Recognizing which party is bound by a given proof is essential in judicial proceedings and everyday transactions. The maxim is a cornerstone of Hanafi procedural law.

Guided Path
1
Define each term
Recall that bayyina refers to legal evidence (typically testimony of two male witnesses), while iqrār refers to a person's admission against his own interest.
Bayyina is external proof; iqrār is self-implicating.
2
Understand the scope of binding effect
Bayyina is mulzima – binding on the judge, the parties, and all concerned. Iqrār is qāsira – limited to its maker; it cannot bind third parties or affect their rights.
The judge must accept valid bayyina; iqrār only creates liability for the confessor.
3
Apply to a concrete scenario
Consider a case: Zayd confesses that he owes Bakr 100. Later, two witnesses testify that Bakr owes 200 to someone else. The confessor (Zayd) is bound by his own confession; the witnesses' testimony (bayyina) binds the court to examine the claim against Bakr.
Iqrār does not transfer liability to others; bayyina may establish someone else's right.
4
Identify the practical consequence
The maxim prevents a person from inadvertently harming another's rights by confession, while ensuring that reliable external evidence carries full weight in court.
This protects due process and individual rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْبَيِّنَةُ حُجَّةٌ مُلْزِمَةٌ وَالْإِقْرَارُ حُجَّةٌ قَاصِرَةٌ
The evidence (proof) is a binding argument, and the confession is a limited (non-binding beyond self) argument.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This maxim distinguishes between two forms of proof: bayyina (evidence, such as testimony of witnesses) is binding on all parties and the judge, while iqrār (confession/admission) is binding only on the person who confesses and cannot affect the rights of others. For example, if X confesses that Y's property was stolen by Z, the confession does not make Z liable; only if X confesses his own liability does it bind him.

Key Terms
الْبَيِّنَةُ (al-bayyina): Legal evidence, usually testimony of two male witnesses or the like.
الْإِقْرَارُ (al-iqrār): A person's admission against his own interest.
مُلْزِمَةٌ (mulzima): Binding on all concerned, including the judge.
قَاصِرَةٌ (qāṣira): Limited in effect, not extending beyond the one who makes it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, what is the difference between bayyina and iqrār?
A) Bayyina is binding on all; iqrār is binding only on the confessor
B) Iqrār is stronger than bayyina
C) Both are equally binding
D) Bayyina is limited to the defendant

Key Takeaway
Bayyina (evidence) is binding on all parties and the judge; iqrār (confession) binds only the person who makes it.

The Maxim: Settlement of Unknown for Known is Permissible
Maxim on permissibility of compromise with an unknown item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'al-ṣulḥ ʿan al-majhūl ʿalā maʿlūm jāʾiz' and apply it to identify valid compromise agreements.

Core Idea
A known settlement can resolve an unknown claim: ṣulḥ is an exception to the prohibition of gharar.

This maxim from the Hanafi school addresses a common practical problem: how to settle a dispute when the exact amount of a claim is uncertain. While contracts with unknown elements (jahāla) are generally invalid, ṣulḥ (compromise) is permitted to end litigation. This lesson unpacks the maxim and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Recognise that the maxim is about ṣulḥ (a settlement contract). The ruling is 'jā'iz' (permissible and valid).
In Hanafi fiqh, 'jā'iz' often means both permissible and legally effective.
2
Understand the Key Terms
Al-ṣulḥ (compromise), al-majhūl (the unknown right), and al-maʿlūm (the known compensation). The unknown is the right being disputed; the known is what is given in exchange.
The term 'majhūl' here refers to the object of the claim, not the compensation.
3
Recognise the Exception (Istihsān)
Normally, gharar (uncertainty) invalidates contracts. Ṣulḥ is an exception based on istihsān to facilitate dispute resolution.
Istihsān is a Hanafi source of law; this maxim is a practical application.
4
Apply to a Hypothetical Case
If A claims unspecified damages from B, and they agree on $500, the ṣulḥ is valid. The unknown claim is settled for a known amount.
Both parties must consent; no coercion.
5
Note the Limits
This does not apply to sale contracts (bayʿ) or gifts (hiba). It only applies when there is a genuine dispute (nizāʿ).
If the claim is already known and undisputed, this maxim does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصُّلْحُ عَنِ الْمَجْهُولِ عَلَى مَعْلُومٍ جَائِزٌ
Settlement (compromise) regarding an unknown (right) upon a known (compensation) is permissible.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This maxim deals with ṣulḥ (amicable settlement) in disputes. If a person has a claim that is unspecified or unknown in amount (e.g., a debt of uncertain quantity), it is permissible to settle for a known sum. For example, if A claims unspecified damages from B, and they agree on a fixed amount, it is valid. Hanafis permit this to avoid litigation.

Key Terms
الصُّلْحُ (al-ṣulḥ): A contract of compromise to end a dispute.
الْمَجْهُولِ (al-majhūl): That which is unknown in quantity or specification.
مَعْلُومٍ (maʿlūm): Known compensation in a ṣulḥ contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a settlement (ṣulḥ), is it permissible to agree on a known compensation for an unknown right?
A) Yes, it is permissible
B) No, it is invalid due to uncertainty
C) Only if the unknown right is small
D) Only if both parties agree after seeing the unknown

Key Takeaway
Settlement of an unknown claim for a known compensation is valid and permissible in Hanafi law as an exception to the prohibition of gharar.

The Maxim: Evidence Equals Direct Sighting
Maxim equating proof via evidence to direct observation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim al-thābit bi al-bayyinah ka al-thābit muʿāyanatan and apply it to determine the legal effect of witness testimony in court.

Core Idea
In Islamic court, valid evidence gives the judge the same certainty as his own eyes.

This lesson introduces one of the most foundational maxims in Islamic judicial procedure: 'What is established by evidence is like what is established by direct sighting.' This principle underpins the authority of testimony and documentary proof in Hanafi fiqh. By the end, you will see how a judge can base a ruling on evidence as if he himself witnessed the event.

Guided Path
1
Memorise the Arabic maxim
Read the Arabic text aloud several times. Internalize the wording verbatim.
This maxim is a memorization anchor for qada and shahadat.
2
Identify key terms
Break down the maxim: al-thābit (what is established), bi al-bayyinah (by evidence), ka al-thābit (like what is established), muʿāyanatan (by direct sighting).
Bayyina here means valid legal proof, typically testimony of two reliable witnesses.
3
Understand the legal implication
The maxim creates a legal equivalence: evidence gives the judge the same level of certainty as his own direct observation. This allows him to rule on the basis of evidence without having witnessed the event himself.
This is a procedural rule, not about epistemic infallibility.
4
Apply to a judicial scenario
Consider a theft case where two reliable witnesses testify. Under this maxim, the judge treats the testimony as if he had seen the theft. He can then impose the hadd penalty (subject to other conditions).
The evidence must be valid according to shari'ah conditions.
5
Recognise limits
The maxim applies only when the evidence is valid. If the witnesses are discredited (e.g., found to be liars), the evidence loses its effect. The judge may also need to consider contradictory evidence.
The maxim does not mean evidence is infallible; it means it has the legal force of certainty for ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اَلثَّابِتُ بِالْبَيِّنَةِ كَالثَّابِتِ مُعَايَنَةً
What is established by evidence (bayyina) is like what is established by direct sighting.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This maxim holds that when a fact is proven in court through legal evidence (e.g., testimony of witnesses), it legally is as certain as if the judge himself had seen it. Thus, the judge can base his ruling on it. For example, if witnesses testify that a defendant stole, the judge treats it as if he saw the theft, and imposes the theft penalty (subject to other conditions).

Key Terms
الْبَيِّنَةِ (al-bayyina): Legal proof, typically valid testimony.
مُعَايَنَةً (mu'āyanatan): Direct observation or personal sighting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if valid evidence establishes a fact, the judge:
A) Must rule based on it as if he saw it.
B) Should still seek corroboration.
C) May disregard it if he has doubt.
D) Can only use it for civil cases.

Key Takeaway
Valid evidence legally equals direct observation for the purpose of judicial rulings.

Introduction to the Book of Adab al-Qadi
Chapter heading for the book of judicial ethics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the scope of the Book of Adab al-Qadi and identify the key areas of judicial ethics covered in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A judge's conduct is as crucial as the judgment itself.

This lesson introduces the chapter on judicial ethics (Adab al-Qadi) from Al-Hidayah. It sets the stage for understanding the qualifications, conduct, and decision-making procedures expected of a Muslim judge according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the chapter title
Read the Arabic phrase 'كتاب أدب القاضي' and its literal translation 'Book of the Etiquette of the Judge'. Understand that 'Adab' refers to proper conduct and manners.
2
Understand the scope
Identify the topics typically covered under Adab al-Qadi: qualifications of a judge, courtroom etiquette, handling of evidence, and procedures for delivering verdicts.
Hanafis have detailed works on qada' and adab.
3
Appreciate the importance
Recognize why a separate chapter on judicial ethics exists: to ensure justice is administered fairly and to uphold the integrity of the judiciary.
A judge's conduct directly impacts the trust in the legal system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب أدب القاضي
Book of the Etiquette of the Judge.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This heading introduces the section on the rules and manners (ādāb) that a judge should observe, including qualifications, conduct in court, and decision-making procedures.

Key Terms
أدب القاضي (adab al-qāḍī): The proper conduct and ethical guidelines for a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the subject of 'Kitāb Adab al-Qāḍī'?
A) Criminal punishment
B) Judicial ethics and procedure
C) Marriage law
D) Sales contracts

Key Takeaway
The Book of Adab al-Qadi establishes the ethical framework for a judge's conduct in the Hanafi legal tradition.

The Judge's Ruling Follows Testimony
Maxim on the derivation of judicial ruling from testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal maxim that a judge's ruling is derived from testimony and explain how it constrains judicial discretion.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling is not his own opinion; it is a legal consequence of the testimony he accepts.

This short but powerful maxim from the Hanafi school establishes a fundamental link between judicial rulings and the evidence presented in court. It emphatically reminds the qāḍī that his verdict is not a subjective decision but rather a legal inference from the shahāda he has verified. Understanding this principle deepens your appreciation of how Hanafi procedural law safeguards against arbitrary judgment.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Maxim
Recite the Arabic text: 'حُكْمُ الْقَضَاءِ يُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ حُكْمِ الشَّهَادَةِ' and its English translation until fluent.
Focus on the key words: ḥukm (ruling), qaḍā' (judgment), yustafādu (is derived), shahāda (testimony).
2
Understand the Literal Meaning
Break the sentence: 'Ḥukm al-qaḍā'' (the ruling of judgment) 'yustafādu' (is derived/copied) 'min ḥukm al-shahāda' (from the ruling of testimony).
The term 'yustafādu' implies a transfer: the judge's ruling is literally drawn from the testimony's ruling.
3
Identify the Two Key Terms
Identify 'ḥukm al-qaḍā'' as the final verdict and 'ḥukm al-shahāda' as the legal effect of the testimony (such as establishing a claim).
In Hanafi law, testimony has its own 'ruling' – e.g., it creates a presumption that must be followed.
4
Explain the Relationship
The judge's ruling does not originate from his own mind but is an inference from the testimony he has accepted as valid. The testimony's ruling becomes the judge's ruling.
This maxim prohibits the judge from ruling based on personal knowledge or extrajudicial information.
5
Apply with an Example
Give a concrete scenario: If witnesses testify that Zayd owes 1000 dirhams, the judge's ruling to pay derives directly from that testimony.
The judge must ensure the testimony meets conditions (two male witnesses, integrity, etc.) before deriving his ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حُكْمُ الْقَضَاءِ يُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ حُكْمِ الشَّهَادَةِ
The ruling of judgment is derived from the ruling of testimony.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This maxim indicates that a judge's verdict (ḥukm al-qaḍā') is based on the evidence (shahāda) presented in court. The judge does not rule arbitrarily; his ruling is a consequence of the testimony he accepts as valid. For example, if witnesses testify that the defendant owes money, the judge's ruling of payment derives from that testimony.

Key Terms
حُكْمُ الْقَضَاءِ (ḥukm al-qaḍā'): The final verdict or judgment issued by a qāḍī.
الشَّهَادَةِ (al-shahāda): Testimony given in court by witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the maxim 'ḥukm al-qaḍā' yustafādu min ḥukm al-shahāda' imply?
A) The judge can rule based on personal knowledge.
B) The judge's ruling is derived from testimony.
C) The judge must follow public opinion.
D) The judge's ruling is independent of evidence.

Key Takeaway
A judge's verdict in Hanafi law is a direct consequence of the testimony he accepts, not an independent decree.

Maxim 53: Judgment Against an Absent Person is Not Permissible
First of four maxims on judicial correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib lā yajūz', identify the ruling (haram/prohibited), and explain the Hanafi rationale regarding procedural fairness.

Core Idea
Judgment against an absent defendant is invalid unless properly represented.

The first maxim in this chapter establishes a fundamental procedural safeguard: no binding ruling can be issued against a person who is not present or properly represented. This protects the defendant's right to be heard and ensures fairness in the legal process.

Guided Path
1
Read the maxim in Arabic
Locate maxim (53) in the passage: 'اَلْقَضَاءُ عَلَى الْغَائِبِ لَا يَجُوزُ'. Note the key term 'al-ghā'ib' (the absent person).
This maxim is phrased as a absolute prohibition.
2
Translate and understand the meaning
Literally: 'Judgment against the absent is not permissible.' The ruling is that it is haram (prohibited) to pass a binding judgment without the defendant's presence or representation.
3
Identify the legal rationale
The Hanafi school emphasizes procedural fairness; the absent party might have a valid defense. A judgment in absentia could lead to injustice.
Exceptions exist when the absent person has a representative (wakeel) or the matter is urgent (e.g., risk of evidence loss).
4
Consider practical application
A judge cannot simply rule against someone who fails to appear without prior notice or representation. The court must ensure proper summons or appoint a representative.
This aligns with modern concepts of due process.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
The Shafi'i school allows judgment in absentia under certain conditions, such as proper notification. This is a key point of ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب کتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (۵۳) اَلْقَضَاءُ عَلَى الْغَائِبِ لَا يَجُوزُ (۵۴) الْكِتَابُ يَشْبَهُ الْكِتَابَ (۵۵) الْحُدُودُ وَالْقِصَاصُ مَبْنَاهُمَا عَلَى الْإِسْقَاطِ (۵۶) الْقَضَاءُ مَتَى لَاقَى فَصْلًا مُجْتَهَدًا فِيهِ يَنْفُذُ وَلَا يَرُدُّهُ غَيْرُهُ
Chapter of writing from judge to judge. (53) Judgment against an absent person is not permissible. (54) The writing resembles the writing. (55) Hadd penalties and qisas are based on dropping. (56) Judgment, when it meets a point of ijtihad, is executed and not reversed by another.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage lists four maxims from the chapter on judicial correspondence. The first maxim states that a judge cannot pass a binding ruling against an absent defendant without proper evidence or representation. The second maxim indicates that a written document from one judge to another holds evidentiary weight similar to oral testimony. The third maxim explains that hudud (fixed penalties) and qisas (retribution) are founded on the principle of dropping or waiving rights, meaning they can be nullified by forgiveness or doubt. The fourth maxim asserts that a judgment based on a valid ijtihad (independent reasoning) is enforceable and cannot be overturned by another judge merely on the basis of different ijtihad.

Key Terms
القضاء على الغائب (al-qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib): Judgment passed against an absent person without their presence or representation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first maxim (53), what is the ruling on passing a final judgment against an absent defendant who has no representative?
A) It is permissible if the evidence is strong.
B) It is not permissible.
C) It is permissible only if the defendant is a minor.
D) It is permissible only in criminal cases.

Key Takeaway
Judgment against an absent person without representation is haram (prohibited) in the Hanafi school, ensuring procedural fairness.

Maxim 54: The Writing Resembles the Writing
Second maxim on judicial correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-kitābu yushbihu al-kitāb', explain its meaning regarding judicial correspondence, and apply it to determine when a letter from one judge to another is admissible as evidence.

Core Idea
An official judicial letter has the same evidentiary value as oral testimony.

This maxim addresses the probative value of written communication between judges. In essence, a letter from one judge to another is treated like oral testimony, meaning the receiving judge can rely on it to execute a judgment without requiring the sending judge to appear in person.

Guided Path
1
Read the maxim in Arabic
Locate maxim (54): 'الْكِتَابُ يَشْبَهُ الْكِتَابَ'. The word 'kitāb' appears twice, meaning 'the writing resembles the writing'.
The repetition emphasises comparison.
2
Translate and understand the meaning
Literal: 'The writing resembles the writing.' Legal meaning: A written document from one judge to another is akin to oral testimony in terms of evidentiary authority.
This does not mean any writing; it specifically refers to official judicial correspondence.
3
Identify the legal implication
When a judge receives a letter from another judge detailing a judgment or evidence, he may accept it as sufficient proof to execute a sentence, similar to how he would accept oral testimony from a witness.
This facilitates cooperation between courts.
4
Consider practical application
If Judge A writes to Judge B that he has convicted X of theft, Judge B can rely on that letter to carry out the hadd penalty without re-trying the case.
But the letter must be verified as authentic (e.g., by seal or known handwriting).
5
Note limitations
The maxim applies only to official judicial correspondence, not to private letters or documents. The content must be clear and unambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب کتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (۵۳) اَلْقَضَاءُ عَلَى الْغَائِبِ لَا يَجُوزُ (۵۴) الْكِتَابُ يَشْبَهُ الْكِتَابَ (۵۵) الْحُدُودُ وَالْقِصَاصُ مَبْنَاهُمَا عَلَى الْإِسْقَاطِ (۵۶) الْقَضَاءُ مَتَى لَاقَى فَصْلًا مُجْتَهَدًا فِيهِ يَنْفُذُ وَلَا يَرُدُّهُ غَيْرُهُ
Chapter of writing from judge to judge. (53) Judgment against an absent person is not permissible. (54) The writing resembles the writing. (55) Hadd penalties and qisas are based on dropping. (56) Judgment, when it meets a point of ijtihad, is executed and not reversed by another.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage lists four maxims from the chapter on judicial correspondence. The first maxim states that a judge cannot pass a binding ruling against an absent defendant without proper evidence or representation. The second maxim indicates that a written document from one judge to another holds evidentiary weight similar to oral testimony. The third maxim explains that hudud (fixed penalties) and qisas (retribution) are founded on the principle of dropping or waiving rights, meaning they can be nullified by forgiveness or doubt. The fourth maxim asserts that a judgment based on a valid ijtihad (independent reasoning) is enforceable and cannot be overturned by another judge merely on the basis of different ijtihad.

Key Terms
الكتاب يشبه الكتاب (al-kitābu yushbihu al-kitāb): A written document from one judge to another is analogous to oral testimony in evidentiary value

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second maxim (54), what is the legal status of an official letter from one judge to another?
A) It has no evidentiary value unless witnesses are also produced.
B) It is equivalent to oral testimony and can be acted upon.
C) It is equivalent to circumstantial evidence only.
D) It is only admissible if the sending judge appears personally.

Key Takeaway
An official judicial letter is treated as credible evidence similar to oral testimony, facilitating inter-jurisdictional cooperation.

Maxim 55: Hudud and Qisas are Based on Dropping
Third maxim on judicial correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-ḥudūdu wal-qiṣāṣu mabnāhumā 'alā al-isqāṭ' and explain that fixed penalties and retaliation are strict regimes that are often dropped or waived in cases of doubt or forgiveness.

Core Idea
Hudud and qisas are not mandatory; they are waived by doubt or forgiveness.

This maxim highlights a key feature of Hanafi criminal law: hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation) are founded on the principle of 'isqat' (dropping). This means they are designed to be avoided whenever possible, either because of doubt (shubha) or because the victim or their family forgives.

Guided Path
1
Read the maxim in Arabic
Locate maxim (55): 'الْحُدُودُ وَالْقِصَاصُ مَبْنَاهُمَا عَلَى الْإِسْقَاطِ'. The key term is 'al-isqāṭ' (the dropping).
2
Translate and understand the meaning
Literal: 'Hudud and qisas are built upon dropping.' Legal meaning: The application of hudud and qisas is strict, and they are to be dropped (not executed) in the presence of any doubt or upon forgiveness.
This does not mean they are optional; they are highly restricted.
3
Identify the legal implication
If there is any uncertainty (shubha) about the commission of the act or the conditions, the penalty is not applied. Similarly, the victim's family can pardon qisas.
This is based on the Prophetic tradition: 'Avert hudud by doubts.'
4
Consider practical application
In a theft case, if there is doubt about the value of the stolen item or whether the thief acted under necessity, the hadd (amputation) is not applied.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
The principle is widely accepted, but the scope of 'doubt' may vary. Malikis are also strict, while Shafi'is may require less doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب کتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (۵۳) اَلْقَضَاءُ عَلَى الْغَائِبِ لَا يَجُوزُ (۵۴) الْكِتَابُ يَشْبَهُ الْكِتَابَ (۵۵) الْحُدُودُ وَالْقِصَاصُ مَبْنَاهُمَا عَلَى الْإِسْقَاطِ (۵۶) الْقَضَاءُ مَتَى لَاقَى فَصْلًا مُجْتَهَدًا فِيهِ يَنْفُذُ وَلَا يَرُدُّهُ غَيْرُهُ
Chapter of writing from judge to judge. (53) Judgment against an absent person is not permissible. (54) The writing resembles the writing. (55) Hadd penalties and qisas are based on dropping. (56) Judgment, when it meets a point of ijtihad, is executed and not reversed by another.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage lists four maxims from the chapter on judicial correspondence. The first maxim states that a judge cannot pass a binding ruling against an absent defendant without proper evidence or representation. The second maxim indicates that a written document from one judge to another holds evidentiary weight similar to oral testimony. The third maxim explains that hudud (fixed penalties) and qisas (retribution) are founded on the principle of dropping or waiving rights, meaning they can be nullified by forgiveness or doubt. The fourth maxim asserts that a judgment based on a valid ijtihad (independent reasoning) is enforceable and cannot be overturned by another judge merely on the basis of different ijtihad.

Key Terms
الحدود والقصاص مبناهما على الإسقاط (al-ḥudūdu wal-qiṣāṣu mabnāhumā 'alā al-isqāṭ): The fixed penalties (hudud) and retaliation (qisas) are founded on the principle of dropping, i.e., they are waived in cases of doubt or forgiveness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third maxim (55), what is the basis of hudud and qisas?
A) Implementation without exception.
B) Dropping (isqat) – they are to be avoided when doubt exists.
C) Punishment proportional to harm.
D) Public deterrence.

Key Takeaway
Hudud and qisas are averted by any credible doubt, reflecting the Hanafi commitment to mercy and caution in applying severe penalties.

Maxim 56: Ijtihad-Based Judgment Stands and Cannot Be Reversed
Fourth maxim on judicial correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-qaḍā'u matā lāqā faṣlan mujtahadan fīhi yanfudhu wa lā yarudduhu ghayruhu', explain that a judgment based on valid ijtihad is enforceable and cannot be overturned by another judge merely because of a different ijtihad.

Core Idea
A valid ijtihad-based judgment is final and not subject to reversal by another judge's ijtihad.

This maxim addresses the finality of judicial decisions in matters where there is legitimate scope for ijtihad. Once a judge passes a ruling based on his own ijtihad, that ruling is binding and cannot be overturned by another judge simply because the second judge has a different interpretation. This ensures stability and respect for judicial independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the maxim in Arabic
Locate maxim (56): 'الْقَضَاءُ مَتَى لَاقَى فَصْلًا مُجْتَهَدًا فِيهِ يَنْفُذُ وَلَا يَرُدُّهُ غَيْرُهُ'. Break it into parts.
2
Translate and understand the meaning
Literal: 'Judgment, when it meets a point of ijtihad in it, it is executed and not rejected by another.' Legal meaning: If a judge delivers a verdict on a matter that is subject to ijtihad (i.e., not ruled by clear text), that verdict is final and cannot be reversed by another judge based on differing ijtihad.
This applies to rulings that are within the judge's jurisdiction and based on sound reasoning.
3
Identify the legal implication
Once a valid judgment is rendered, it is enforceable. Another judge cannot nullify or modify it merely because he disagrees with the reasoning, unless the original judgment was clearly contrary to a definitive text (nass) or illogical.
This promotes certainty and prevents endless appeals.
4
Consider practical application
If a judge in Kufa decides a case using his ijtihad and writes to a judge in Basra for execution, the Basra judge must execute it even if he holds a different opinion, provided the original judgment was valid.
5
Note limitations
The maxim does not apply if the judgment contradicts a clear text (Quran or Sunnah) or is procedurally invalid. Also, if the first judge's ijtihad is obviously flawed (e.g., ignoring consensus), it may be reversed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter of writing from judge to judge. (53) Judgment against an absent person is not permissible. (54) The writing resembles the writing. (55) Hadd penalties and qisas are based on dropping. (56) Judgment, when it meets a point of ijtihad, is executed and not reversed by another.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage lists four maxims from the chapter on judicial correspondence. The first maxim states that a judge cannot pass a binding ruling against an absent defendant without proper evidence or representation. The second maxim indicates that a written document from one judge to another holds evidentiary weight similar to oral testimony. The third maxim explains that hudud (fixed penalties) and qisas (retribution) are founded on the principle of dropping or waiving rights, meaning they can be nullified by forgiveness or doubt. The fourth maxim asserts that a judgment based on a valid ijtihad (independent reasoning) is enforceable and cannot be overturned by another judge merely on the basis of different ijtihad.

Key Terms
القضاء متى لاقى فصلا مجتهدا فيه ينفذ ولا يرده غيره (al-qaḍā'u matā lāqā faṣlan mujtahadan fīhi yanfudhu wa lā yarudduhu ghayruhu): A judgment that addresses a point open to ijtihad is enforceable and cannot be reversed by another judge based on different ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A ruling made on a valid ijtihad is final and cannot be overturned by another judge's differing ijtihad, ensuring legal stability and respect for judicial independence.

Four Key Maxims of Arbitration from Usul al-Hidayah
List of four maxims on arbitration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four maxims of arbitration and explain their legal significance in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The apparent is not proof; guardianship is deputyship; no liability for public officials; no oath on the judge.

This passage from the Usul al-Hidayah presents four key maxims from the chapter of arbitration (Bab al-Tahkim). These maxims address the limits of outward possession, the nature of guardianship, the immunity of judges and rulers, and the prohibition of oaths on judges. Understanding these principles is essential for grasping the Hanafi framework of judicial procedure and public law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first maxim
The first maxim states: 'الظاهر لا يصلح حجة للاستحقاق' (The apparent is not valid as proof for entitlement). This means that mere outward appearance, such as possession of an item, is not sufficient evidence to establish legal ownership in a dispute. Actual proof (like witnesses or documentation) is required.
This is a foundational principle in Hanafi evidence law.
2
Identify the second maxim
The second maxim states: 'الوصاية خلافة' (Guardianship is a deputyship). This means that a guardian (e.g., a father or executor) acts as a deputy for the ward, so the guardian's actions are legally attributed to the ward within the scope of authority.
This defines the legal nature of guardianship as a form of representation.
3
Identify the third maxim
The third maxim states: 'الضمان لا يلحق الإمام والقاضي وأمينه' (Liability does not attach to the imam, judge, and his trustee). This means that public officials are not personally liable for damages resulting from actions performed in their official capacity, provided they act within their authority and rightfully.
This immunity ensures that officials can perform their duties without fear of personal liability.
4
Identify the fourth maxim
The fourth maxim states: 'لا يمين على القاضي' (No oath upon the judge). This means that a judge cannot be compelled to take an oath regarding his judgment. His decision is based solely on evidence and his ijtihad.
This preserves the judge's impartiality and authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحكيم (۵۷) الظَّاهِرُ لَا يَصْلُحُ حُجَّةً لِلِاسْتِحْقَاقِ (۵۸) الْوَصَايَةُ خِلَافَةٌ (۵۹) اَلضَّمَانُ لَا يَلْحَقُ الْإِمَامَ وَالْقَاضِي وَأَمِيْنَهُ (٢٠) لَا يَمِيْنَ عَلَى الْقَاضِي
Chapter of arbitration. (57) The apparent is not valid as proof for entitlement. (58) Guardianship is a deputyship. (59) Liability does not attach to the imam, judge, and his trustee. (60) No oath upon the judge.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents four maxims from the chapter on arbitration. The first maxim means that if someone appears to own something (like possessing it), that alone is not sufficient proof of legal ownership — actual evidence is needed. The second maxim states that a guardian (like a father or executor) acts as a deputy for the person under guardianship, so their actions are like the actions of the ward. The third maxim protects religious and judicial authorities from personal liability for actions performed in their official capacity, provided they act rightfully. The fourth maxim indicates that a judge cannot be compelled to take an oath regarding his judgment; his decision is based on evidence.

Key Terms
الوصاية خلافة (al-wiṣāyatu khilāfatun): Guardianship is a form of deputyship where the guardian stands in place of the ward
الضمان لا يلحق الإمام والقاضي (al-ḍamānu lā yalḥaqu al-imāma wa al-qāḍī): Liability for damages does not attach to the imam (ruler) or judge for actions done within their authority

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because apparent possession is always deceptive.
B) Because the apparent (ẓāhir) does not serve as proof for legal entitlement (istiḥqāq).
C) Because possession requires two witnesses.
D) Because only a judge can determine ownership.

Key Takeaway
The four maxims together define key principles of evidence, authority, liability, and procedure in Hanafi judicial practice.

The Obligation Regarding the Unknown Does Not Materialize
States a maxim concerning legal claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim and apply it to identify invalid legal claims due to uncertainty.

Core Idea
A legal claim cannot impose an obligation if the claimed item is unknown.

In the Hanafi judicial system, the validity of a claim (da'wa) depends on the clarity of its subject matter. This maxim establishes a fundamental rule: you cannot force someone to pay or deliver something that is not specified. Understanding this rule is essential for judges and litigants alike.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim's subject
The maxim deals with legal claims (da'wa) brought before a judge. Its context is the chapter on claims in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the key term: majhul
Majhul means unknown or unspecified. For a claim to be valid, the thing claimed must be known (ma'lum) – e.g., a specific amount of money, a particular item, or a definite right.
Uncertainty in the subject matter invalidates the claim.
3
State the consequence
If the claim concerns an unknown matter, the obligation (ilzam) does not materialize (la yatahaqqaq). The judge cannot impose any duty on the defendant.
4
Apply to scenarios
Evaluate sample claims: 'He owes me something' – invalid; 'He owes me 100 dirhams' – valid. The maxim ensures legal obligations are based on definite facts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى (83) الْإِلْزَامُ فِي الْمَجْهُولِ لَا يَتَحَقَّقُ
Book of Claims. (83) Obligation regarding the unknown does not materialize.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
In the chapter on claims (da'wa), this maxim states that a legal claim cannot impose an obligation if the subject matter is unknown (majhul). For a claim to be valid, the thing claimed must be specified and known.

Key Terms
الدَّعْوَى (al-da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit before a judge.
الْمَجْهُول (al-majhul): The unknown or unspecified; the opposite of ma'lum (known).
الْإِلْزَام (al-ilzam): The imposition of an obligation or duty by a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this maxim, can a legal obligation be established for an unknown subject?
A) Yes
B) No
C) Only if both parties agree
D) Only in criminal cases

Key Takeaway
A legal claim must specify its subject; otherwise, no obligation can arise.

Condition for Valid Legal Claim: Maximum Specification
A condition for valid claim: maximum specification required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the validity of a legal claim and apply it to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A claim is valid only if the claimant specifies the subject matter to the fullest extent possible.

This lesson introduces a fundamental condition for the validity of a legal claim (da'wa) in Hanafi qada. The maxim requires that the claimant describe the claimed object or right with the maximum possible detail, eliminating uncertainty (gharar). This principle ensures clarity in litigation and prevents frivolous or ambiguous claims.

2
Understand the condition

The word 'شرط' indicates a binding condition.
3
Apply to a scenario
Given a hypothetical claim, determine whether the specification meets the condition by checking if details are provided to the utmost extent.
For example, a claim for debt must specify amount, currency, cause, and date.

The Maxim: The Possessor's Word is Taken in Claims
A maxim of judicial procedure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that the possessor's word is believed in property disputes and identify the exceptional condition where this presumption is rebutted.

Core Idea
The possessor's word is default; the claimant must prove otherwise.

In Islamic judicial procedure, when a dispute arises over ownership of an item, the person who currently holds physical possession (yad) is given the benefit of the doubt. This maxim from the Hanafi school establishes a presumption in favor of the possessor, but it is not an absolute rule—it can be overcome by evidence. Understanding this principle is essential for any student of Hanafi fiqh, especially in matters of qada (judiciary).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Context
Recognize that this maxim applies in claims (da'awa) over property. The 'word' (qawl) refers to whose statement is accepted without proof as a default.
This is a procedural rule for judges.
2
Understand 'Possessor'
Sahib al-yad is the person who has actual physical control or possession of the item. The maxim gives them the evidentiary advantage.
Possession is a strong indicator of ownership in Hanafi law.
3
Apply the Presumption
If the claimant sues the possessor, the judge will initially accept the possessor's word (e.g., 'This is my property') unless the claimant provides evidence to the contrary.
The burden of proof is on the claimant.
4
Note the Exception
This presumption is rebuttable. If the claimant brings clear evidence (bayyinah), the judge decides in favor of the claimant. Also, if the possession itself is contested (e.g., theft), the maxim may not apply.
Acquiring possession through wrongdoing voids the presumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(92) اَلْقَوْلُ فِي الدَّعَاوِي لِصَاحِبِ الْيَدِ
(92) The word in claims is for the owner of possession.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This maxim means that in disputes over property, the person who has actual possession (yad) of the item is favored; their word is taken as the default, and the claimant must provide evidence to overcome this presumption.

Key Terms
صَاحِبِ الْيَدِ (sahib al-yad): The person who has physical possession or control of an item.
اَلْقَوْلُ (al-qawl): The statement or word that is accepted as true without proof.
الدَّعَاوِي (al-da'awi): Plural of da'wa: claims or lawsuits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi court, two parties dispute ownership of a book. The book is in the hand of the defendant. Who must provide evidence of ownership?
A) The defendant (possessor)
B) The claimant
C) Both equally
D) The judge

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-qawl li-sahib al-yad' establishes a rebuttable presumption in favor of the possessor in property disputes, placing the burden of proof on the claimant.

براءة الذمة: The Default is Innocence of Obligations
Introduces a foundational legal presumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim براءة الذمة and apply it to determine the burden of proof in a financial claim.

Core Idea
A person is presumed free from liability until proven otherwise.

This maxim is a foundational legal presumption in Islamic law. It establishes that the default state of a person is freedom from any obligation or debt. It shifts the burden of proof to the claimant, ensuring that no one is held liable without evidence.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
Commit the Arabic phrase to memory: الْأَصْلُ بَرَاءَةُ الدِّمَمِ. Understand its literal meaning: 'The default is the innocence of persons (from obligations).'
2
Identify the legal consequence
The maxim creates a presumption of non-liability. If someone claims another owes them money, the claimant must bring proof; the defendant does not have to prove innocence.
This applies to all types of obligations: debts, torts, contract claims.
3
Distinguish from substantive innocence
This is a procedural presumption, not a guarantee of actual innocence. It can be overcome by evidence. It does not assert that the person is morally innocent, only that they are legally presumed not liable.
Contrast with the principle 'al-asl baqa ma kana ala ma kana' (presumption of continuity).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(100) الْأَصْلُ بَرَاءَةُ الدِّمَمِ
(100) The default is the innocence of persons (from obligations).
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This maxim expresses the legal presumption that a person is free from liability or debt. If someone claims that another owes them something, the burden of proof is on the claimant; the defendant is presumed innocent until proven otherwise.

Key Terms
بَرَاءَةُ الدِّمَمِ (bara'at al-dhimam): Innocence of obligations; the default state of being free from liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The defendant

Key Takeaway

Whose Statement is Accepted: The Maxim of Apparent Action
Maxim on whose word is believed in case of conflicting claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim and apply it to a dispute to determine which party's claim is given preference.

Core Idea
The one whose outward possession or action is more evident has their word accepted in a dispute.

In legal disputes, who does the judge believe? This maxim provides a key evidentiary principle: preference is given to the party whose manifest behavior (taṣarruf)—such as possession or control—matches their claim. It is a rule of evidence used by Hanafi judges in cases of conflicting claims, especially in sales and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
Start by noting who is disputing and what each party claims. For example, a buyer and seller disagree on the price at which a sale occurred.
2
Determine whose taṣarruf is more apparent
Look at the outward action or possession of each party. The one who has the stronger apparent control (e.g., possession of the item, exercise of authority) has a more apparent taṣarruf.
Taṣarruf can be possession, use, or any visible disposition of the property.
3
State that this party's qawl is accepted
According to the maxim, the statement (qawl) of the party with more apparent taṣarruf is taken as the basis. Their claim is deemed credible unless the other party provides contrary proof.
This does not mean the other party's claim is rejected outright; it means the burden of proof shifts.
4
Apply the maxim to a scenario
Practice by applying the maxim to a concrete case: if the buyer has the goods, his word on the price is accepted; if the seller has not yet delivered, his word is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ مَنْ كَانَ أَظْهَرَ تَصَرُّفًا
The statement is the statement of the one who was more apparent in action.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This maxim is used when there is a dispute about a transaction, e.g., buyer and seller disagree on price. The principle is that the word of the person whose outward action (taṣarruf) is more evident is given preference. For example, in a sale, if the buyer claims a lower price while the seller claims a higher price, and the merchandise is in the buyer's possession, the buyer's statement is accepted because his possession is more apparent. Conversely, if the seller has the good, his word is preferred. This is based on the Hanafi legal principle of ẓāhir al-taṣarruf.

Key Terms
قول (qawl): Statement or claim of a party in a dispute.
تصرف (taṣarruf): Action or disposition, e.g., possession or exercise of control over property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over a sale, whose statement is given preference according to this maxim?
A) The seller always
B) The buyer always
C) The one with more apparent possession
D) The judge

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-qawl qawl man kāna aẓhara taṣarrufan' establishes that in disputes, the party with more visible possession or control has their statement accepted initially.

Qada — Judiciary
beginner
6 min
Word of the Possessor in Quantity Disputes
Maxim on credibility of possessor in amount received

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on the credibility of the possessor in disputes over the amount of property received and apply it to distinguish it from disputes over entitlement.

Core Idea
In quantity disputes, the possessor's word is taken; the onus is on the claimant to prove otherwise.

This lesson examines a key judicial maxim from Hanafi fiqh: when two parties disagree about the amount of property that was actually handed over, the person who received it (the qābiḍ) is believed with an oath. This applies whether the receiver is a trustee (amīn) or a guarantor (ḍamīn). The maxim is a rule of evidence that shifts the burden of proof to the claimant, reflecting the principle that possession is prima facie evidence of trustworthiness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
Understand that the dispute is about the quantity (miqdār) of property received, not about ownership or entitlement. The parties are the giver (often the plaintiff) and the receiver (qābiḍ).
The maxim does not apply if the dispute is about whether anything was given at all.
2
State the ruling (the maxim)
Memorize the Arabic maxim: al-qawl qawl al-qābiḍ fi miqdār al-maqbūḍ. The statement of the possessor regarding the amount is taken as true, after he takes an oath.
This holds whether the qābiḍ is a guarantor (ḍamīn) or a trustee (amīn).
3
Explain the legal reasoning
The possessor is entrusted with the property; the original owner entrusted it to him. Hence, the possessor is considered trustworthy until proven otherwise. The onus of proof lies on the claimant (the one alleging a larger amount).
This is based on the principle that the trustee is to be trusted, and the guarantor is also trusted because he took possession with consent.
4
Apply to an example
Example: A lends B $100 (B is a trustee). Later A claims he lent $200, but B says it was $100. According to the maxim, B's statement is accepted with oath. A must provide evidence for the extra $100.
If B were a guarantor (e.g., he guaranteed a debt and then received payment from the debtor), similar rule applies.
5
Distinguish from other disputes
Note that the maxim does not apply to disputes about entitlement (e.g., who owns the property) or whether a contract existed. It only applies when the fact of receipt is undisputed and only the amount is contested.
This is a crucial limitation to avoid misapplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْقَابِضِ فِى مِقْدَارِ الْمَقْبُوضِ ضَمِينًا أَوْ أَمِينًا
The statement is the statement of the taker in the amount of what was taken, whether as a guarantor or a trustee.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim deals with disputes about the amount of property received. If the person who took possession (qābiḍ) disagrees with the owner about the quantity, the qābiḍ's statement is accepted with oath, regardless of whether he is a guarantor (ḍamīn) or a trustee (amīn). For example, if a borrower (trustee) claims he borrowed $100 but the lender claims $200, the borrower's word is preferred. This is because the onus of proof lies on the claimant, and the possessor is considered trustworthy.

Key Terms
قابض (qābiḍ): The person who has taken possession (qabḍ) of the property.
ضَمِين (ḍamīn): Guarantor – one who undertakes to pay a debt if the debtor defaults.
أَمِين (amīn): Trustee – one who is entrusted with property without liability for loss except by negligence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim, whose statement is given weight regarding the amount of property received?
A) The owner's
B) The giver's
C) The possessor's
D) A neutral witness

Key Takeaway
In disputes over the quantity of property received, Hanafi law accepts the word of the person in possession, whether trustee or guarantor, with an oath, placing the burden of proof on the claimant.

Qada — Judiciary

Imprisonment for Suspicion Established by Partial Testimony
Commentary expanding on a ruling from al-Hidayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why imprisonment for suspicion in hadd and qisas cases is established by partial testimony, and differentiate this from property cases.

Core Idea
Imprisonment for suspicion in hudud and qisas requires only partial testimony—two masked witnesses or one just witness.

This passage from Usool al-Hidayah presents a key Hanafi principle: in serious crimes, imprisonment may be ordered based on suspicion, and suspicion is proven by one of the two components of testimony. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this leniency and how it contrasts with property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal ruling
State the Hanafi position: in hadd and qisas, a defendant can be imprisoned on suspicion if two masked witnesses or one just witness testify.
The ruling applies to the guarantor (kafil) in the context of kafalah, but the principle extends to any defendant.
2
Understand 'shatr al-shahada'
Grasp the two halves: number (at least two witnesses, even if not fully just) and uprightness (a single just witness). Partial testimony means satisfying exactly one of these halves.
Complete testimony requires both halves: two just witnesses.
3
Apply the principle to the two scenarios
Two masked witnesses (masturan) provide number; one just witness provides uprightness. Either suffices for suspicion-based imprisonment.
A masked witness is one whose corruption is not known—legally acceptable for suspicion but not for establishing the claim itself.
4
Contrast with property cases
In property disputes, imprisonment is the ultimate punishment (ghayat 'uquba) and so requires complete testimony (two just witnesses). Partial testimony is insufficient.
The rationale: suspicion is weaker than a proven claim, so a lower evidentiary threshold is acceptable.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school generally requires complete testimony even for suspicion-based detention. Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
In Hanafi, a judge has more discretion to remand on partial evidence; in Shafi'i, he must wait for full proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فَإِنْ شَهِدَ عَلَيْهِ) أَيْ عَلَى الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ (مَسْتُورَانِ) أَيْ غَيْرُ مَعْلُومٍ فَسَادُهُمَا فِي حَدٌ أَوْ قَوَدٍ حُبِسَ وَكَذَا يُحْبَسُ إِنْ شَهِدَ عَدْلٌ وَاحِدٌ) يَعْرِفُهُ الْقَاضِي بِالْعَدَالَةِ لأَنَّ الْحَبْسَ هُنَا لِلسُّهُمَةِ وَالتُهُمَةٌ تَثْبُتُ بِإِحْدَى شَطْرَى الشَّهَادَةِ، وَهُوَ الْعَدَدُ فِي المستور أو العَدَالَةُ فِى الْوَاحِدِ بِخِلَافِ الْحَبْسِ فِي الْأَمْوَالِ لِأَنَّهُ غَايَةُ عُقُوبَةٍ فِيهَا فَلَا يَثْبُتُ إِلَّا بِحُجَّةٍ كَامِلَةٍ. (مجمع الأنهر : كتاب الكفالة، ج ۲ ص ۱۲۹) قَالَ (وَلَا يُحْبَسُ فِيهَا حَتَّى يَشْهَدَ شَاهِدَانِ إِلَخُ لَا يَحْبِسُ الْحَاكِمُ فِي الْحُدُودِ
وَفِي بَعْضِ النُّسَخِ فِيهِمَا: أَي فِي حَدٌ الْقَذْفِ وَالْقِصَاصِ حَتَّى يَشْهَدَ شَاهِدَانِ مَسْتُورَانِ أَوْ شَاهِدٌ عَدْلٌ يَعْرِفُهُ : أَيْ يَعْرِفُ الْحَاكِمُ كَوْنَهُ عَدْلًا، لأَنَّ الْحَبْسَ هَاهُنَا لِلسُّهُمَةِ: أَى لِتُهُمَةِ الْفَسَادِ لَا لِإِثْبَاتِ الْمُدَّعَى لِأَنَّهُ يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى حُجَّةٍ كَامِلَةٍ وَالتَّهْمَةُ تَنْبُتُ بِأَحَدٍ شَطْرَى الشَّهَادَةِ إِمَّا الْعَدَدُ أَوْ الْعَدَالَةُ. (العناية: كتاب الكفالة، ج۷ ص ۱۷۹،۱۷۸) الهداية كتاب الكفالة، ج ۳ ص ۱۲۲
Translation: Suspicion (tuhma) is established by one of the two parts of testimony, whether it be number or uprightness. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that to prove an accusation against someone, one component of the two parts of testimony—number or uprightness—is sufficient: either two witnesses, or one just witness. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, states that in hadd and qisas cases, a guarantor will not be imprisoned until two masked (mastur) persons or one just person known to the judge testify, because the imprisonment of the guarantor is due to suspicion, and suspicion is established by one component of the two parts of testimony. Thus if two masked witnesses testify, or one just male witness testifies, then imprisonment is established because it is due to suspicion, and suspicion in these crimes is established by both parts of testimony. Unlike matters related to property, where unless complete testimony (shahadat al-kamilah) exists, it is not established; for that, complete testimony i.e. two just witnesses is necessary, and it is not established by defective testimony. In property cases, imprisonment is the ultimate punishment, so its establishment requires complete testimony.

(If two masked persons testify against him,) meaning against the defendant, (in a hadd or qisas case, he is imprisoned. Likewise, he is imprisoned if one just witness testifies) whom the judge knows to be just, because imprisonment here is for suspicion (suhma), and suspicion is established by one half of testimony: number in the case of the masked, or uprightness in the case of the single witness. Unlike imprisonment in property cases, because it is the ultimate punishment there, so it is not established except by complete proof. (Majma' al-Anhur: Kitab al-Kafalah, vol. 2, p. 129) He said: (He is not imprisoned therein until two witnesses testify, etc.) The judge does not imprison in hudud...

And in some copies: i.e., in hadd al-qadhf and qisas, until two masked witnesses or one just witness known to him testify—i.e., the judge knows him to be just—because imprisonment here is for suspicion: i.e., for suspicion of corruption, not for proving the claim because that requires complete proof, and suspicion is established by one of the two halves of testimony: either number or uprightness. (al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Kafalah, vol. 7, pp. 178,179) al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Kafalah, vol. 3, p. 122
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage from Usool al-Hidayah states a legal principle and its application in the context of kafalah (suretyship) within hadd and qisas cases. The passage is a commentary (sharh) that expands on the matn (core text) of al-Hidayah. The core ruling is that for imprisonment based on suspicion (tuhma) in criminal cases, a complete testimony (two just witnesses) is not necessary; it suffices to have either two masked witnesses (masturan: whose uprightness is not known but whose corruption is not known either) or a single just witness known to the judge. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the imprisonment here is for suspicion, not for proving the claim itself. Suspicion is established by one of the two 'halves' of testimony: number (at least two witnesses, even if not fully just) or uprightness (of a single witness). The passage contrasts this with imprisonment in property cases, which is the ultimate punishment and therefore requires complete testimony (two just witnesses). The author also notes that some manuscripts specify this applies to hadd al-qadhf (slander) and qisas, further clarifying the scope. The overall legal weight is from the Zahir al-Riwayah works (al-Hidayah, al-'Inayah, Majma' al-Anhur) and is relied upon in the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
شطر الشهادة (shatr al-shahada): One of the two components of testimony: either number (at least two witnesses) or uprightness (adala of a single witness).
مستور (mastur): A person whose corruption is not known but whose uprightness is also not known; i.e., not known to be unjust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a qisas case, what testimony suffices to imprison the defendant on suspicion according to the Hanafi school?
A) Two just witnesses only
B) Two masked witnesses or one just witness
C) One masked witness only
D) Any two witnesses

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the threshold for imprisonment based on suspicion is lower than for proving the claim: partial testimony suffices in hadd and qisas cases.

Qada — Judiciary

Judicial Ruling Derived from Testimony
Stating a legal maxim: qada depends on shahadah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that qada is derived from shahadah and explain why this principle is foundational to Hanafi judicial procedure.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling is grounded in testimony, not personal opinion.

This short but powerful maxim establishes a cornerstone of Hanafi judicial theory: the authority of a judge (qada) is not independent but is built upon the evidence presented through testimony (shahadah). In other words, the ruling of the court is a direct consequence of the proofs submitted by witnesses. Understanding this principle helps clarify why testimony rules are so rigorously defined in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim
Read the Arabic maxim: 'حُكْمُ الْقَضَاءِ يُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ حُكْمِ الشَّهَادَةِ'. Note that 'يُسْتَفَادُ' means 'is derived' or 'is benefited'.
The verb 'yustafad' indicates a causal relationship.
2
Translate and Paraphrase
Translate: 'The ruling of judiciary is derived from the ruling of testimony'. Paraphrase: A judge's decision is based on the evidence given by witnesses.
This implies that without proper testimony, the judge cannot issue a valid ruling.
3
Connect to the Broader Principle
The commentary then quotes from hawalah and kafalah to illustrate a meta-rule: 'al-ahkam al-shar'iyyah 'ala wifaq al-ma'ani al-lughawiyyah' – Shariah rulings conform to linguistic meanings. This reinforces that qada follows the literal meaning of shahadah (testimony).
The link between qada and shahadah is thus both terminological and substantive.
4
Apply to a Simple Scenario
Imagine a judge hearing a property dispute. Without witness testimony, the judge cannot rule. The ruling is 'derived' from the testimony of credible witnesses.
This distinguishes qada from other forms of decision-making (e.g., arbitration by agreement).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب أدب القاضي
(۵۲) حُكْمُ الْقَضَاءِ يُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ حُكْمِ الشَّهَادَةِ.
ترجمہ: قضاء کا حکم شہادت کے حکم سے مستفاد ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ مسئلہ قضاء یہ مسئلہ شہادت پر مبنی ہے یعنی جو إن الحوالة للنقل والدين متى انتقل من ذمة لا يبقى فيها أما الكفالة فللضم
والأحكام الشرعية على وفاق المعاني اللغوية.
(الجوهرة النيرة : كتاب الحوالة، ج ۱ ص ۳۱۷)
ولنا أن الحوالة النقل لغة أى معناها من حيث اللغة بدل كما ......... (أما الكفالة فللضم لأنها مشتقة من الكفل وهو ضم الشيئ إلى الشيئ، وضم الشيئ إلى الشيئ لا يوجب فراغ الأول إلا أن يكون بشرط براءة الأصيل فتصير حوالة، لأنها فى معنى الحوالة والأحكام الشرعية على وفاق المعاني اللغوية) يعنى أن العمل بمعانى اللغات واجب في الأحكام الشرعية.

Kitab Adab al-Qadi. (52) The ruling of judiciary is derived from the ruling of testimony. Translation: The ruling of qada is derived from the ruling of shahadah. Commentary: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that the issue of qada is based on the issue of shahadah, meaning ... 'Indeed, hawalah is for transfer, and when the debt is transferred from a liability, it does not remain in it, while kafalah is for joining, and the shariah rulings are in accordance with linguistic meanings.' (Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah: Kitab al-Hawalah, vol. 1 p. 317) 'And we hold that hawalah is transfer linguistically, i.e., its meaning from the language is ... (as for kafalah, it is for joining, because it is derived from kifl, which is joining one thing to another, and joining one thing to another does not necessitate the freeing of the first unless with the condition of freeing the principal; then it becomes hawalah, because it is in the meaning of hawalah. And the shariah rulings are in accordance with linguistic meanings.)' (Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Hawalah, vol. 8 pp. 488, 287) Al-Hidayah: Kitab Adab al-Qadi, vol. 3 p. 139.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a short legal maxim: rulings concerning judicial decisions (qada) are derived from rulings concerning testimony (shahadah). The commentary then digresses by quoting from earlier texts on hawalah and kafalah, likely to illustrate the broader principle that shariah rulings follow linguistic meanings, which is also applicable to qada. However, the direct connection between qada and shahadah is not further explained. The quotes from al-Jawharah and al-Binayah repeat the previous lesson's content, suggesting that the compiler is reminding the reader of the overarching principle. This entry serves as a memorization anchor for the student, linking qada to the well-established rule that testimony is the basis of proof in court.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Judicial decision or judgment issued by a qadi (judge).
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony given by witnesses in a legal proceeding, serving as evidence for the judge.
يستفاد (yustafad): Is derived or is benefited; indicates the source of the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling is not an independent act; it is a consequence of testimony presented in court.

Qada — Judiciary
beginner
6 min
The Ruling of Qada' is Derived from Shahada
Establishes that witness qualifications apply to judges

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that a judge's qualifications mirror those of a witness, and apply it to determine whether a given person qualifies for judgeship.

Core Idea
A judge must have the same qualifications as a witness because both involve wilāyah (authority to enforce one's statement).

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi legal principle: the ruling on judgeship (qada) is derived from the ruling on testimony (shahada). The author of Al-Hidayah explains that both roles belong to the category of wilayah – the authority to enforce one's statement upon others. Since a witness must meet specific conditions (Islam, freedom, sanity, etc.), a judge must meet the same conditions. This principle simplifies the eligibility criteria for judges and ensures consistency across the judicial system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Read the phrase: 'حُكْمُ الْقَضَاءِ يُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ حُكْمِ الشَّهَادَةِ'. Understand that the ruling of qada is derived from the ruling of shahada.
2
Understand the common element: wilayah
Recognize that both a witness (shahid) and a judge (qadi) exercise wilayah: they impose their statements or decisions on others. This shared concept forms the basis of the analogy.
Wilayah is defined as 'تنفيذ القول على الغير' – enforcing one's statement upon others.
3
List the conditions required for a witness
According to Hanafi fiqh, a witness must be Muslim, free, sane, and possess other qualities. These are derived from standard works of shahada.
The passage specifically mentions Islam, freedom (hurriyya), and intellect ('aql).
4
Apply the conditions to a judge
Since qada is derived from shahada, the same conditions apply to a judge. Additionally, the passage from Al-Lubab states that the judge must be a mujtahid.
Thus, a judge must be Muslim, free, sane, and a mujtahid.
5
Confirm the legal consequence
The appointment of a judge (wilayat al-qadi) is not valid unless the person appointed possesses these conditions. Also, the judge should be appointed by others, not self-nominated.
The passive participle 'al-muwalla' indicates the judge is appointed by another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب أدب القاضي
(۵۲) حُكْمُ الْقَضَاءِ يُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ حُكْمِ الشَّهَادَةِ.
ترجمہ: قضاء کا حکم شہادت کے حکم سے مستفاد ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ مسئلہ قضاء یہ مسئلہ شہادت پر مبنی ہے یعنی جو شرائط اور احکام شہادت کے لئے ہیں وہی قضاء کے لئے بھی ہیں ، بالفاظ دیگر جو گواہ بنے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہو وہ قاضی بن سکتا ہے، یعنی گواہ کے لئے جو شرائط ہیں وہی قضاء کے لیے بھی شرائط ہیں۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا ر ہے ہیں کہ کسی کو قاضی بنانا درست نہیں ہے جب تک متونی یعنی جس کو منصب قضاء سپرد کی جاتی ہے اس میں شہادت کی شرائط نہ ہوں ، اس لیے کہ قضاء کا حکم شہادت کے حکم سے مستفاد ہے ،شہادت اور قضاء دونوں کا تعلق ولایت کے باب سے ہے، اور ولایت کہتے ہیں تنـفـيـذ الـقـول عـلـى الغيـر يعنى دوسرے پر کوئی بات نافذ کرنا ، جس طرح شاہد شہادت کے ذریعے دوسرے پر اپنا قول نافذ کرتا ہے، اس طرح قاضی بھی قضاء کے ذریعے دوسرے پر اپنا فیصلہ نافذ کرتا ہے، اس حوالے سے دونوں میں یکسانیت ہے اور شاہد کے لیے شرائط شہادت شرط ہیں تو قضاء کے لیے بھی یہ چیزیں شرط ہوں گی، اس لیے جو شہادت کا اہل ہوگا وہ قضاء کا بھی اہل ہوگا۔ خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ عہد قضاء اسی کے سپرد کیا جائے گا جس میں شہادت کی شرائط ہیں ، اس لیے کہ قضاء شہادت سے مستفاد ہے۔
(شرائط الشهادة) لأن حكم القضاء يستقى من حكم الشهادة، لأن كل واحد منهما من باب الولاية، فكل من كان أهلا للشهادة يكون أهلا للقضاء، وما يشترط لأهلية الشهادة يشترط لأهلية القضاء . اللباب في شرح الكتاب: كتاب أدب القاضي، ج ۴ ص ۷۷)
وَلَا تَصِحُ وِلَايَةُ الْقَاضِي إِلَخْ لَا تَصِحُ وِلَايَةُ الْقَاضِي حَتَّى يَجْتَمِعَ فِي الْمُوَلَّى بِلَفْظِ اسْمِ الْمَفْعُولِ، وَاخْتَارَهُ عَلَى الْمُتَوَلَّى بِلَفْظِ اسْمِ الْفَاعِلِ إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى أَنَّ الْقَاضِيَ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَكُونَ قَاضِيًا بِتَوْلِيَةِ غَيْرِهِ لَا بِطَلَبِهِ التَّوْلِيَةِ شَرَائِطُ الشَّهَادَةِ مِنْ الإسلام والحُرِّيَّةِ وَالعَقلِ والتنوع، وَيَكُونُ أَى الْمُوَلَّى مِنْ أَهْلِ الاجْتِهَادِ. أَمَّا
Book of Manners of the Judge (52) The ruling of qada' is derived from the ruling of shahada. Translation: The ruling of qada' is derived from the ruling of shahada. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that the matter of qada' is based on the matter of shahada; that is, whatever conditions and rulings apply to shahada also apply to qada'. In other words, whoever has the ability to be a witness can be a judge, meaning the conditions for a witness are the same conditions for qada'. The author of Al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is stating that it is not valid to appoint someone as a judge unless the person being appointed (mawallaa) possesses the conditions of shahada, because the ruling of qada' is derived from the ruling of shahada. Both shahada and qada' belong to the category of wilayah (authority), and wilayah means enforcing one's statement upon others. Just as a witness enforces his statement upon others through testimony, similarly a judge enforces his decision upon others through qada'. In this respect, both are similar. Since conditions of shahada are required for a witness, these same things will be conditions for qada'. Therefore, whoever is qualified for shahada will also be qualified for qada'. In summary, the office of qada' will only be entrusted to someone who possesses the conditions of shahada, because qada' is derived from shahada. (Conditions of shahada) Because the ruling of qada' is drawn from the ruling of shahada, because each of them belongs to the category of wilayah. So whoever is qualified for shahada is qualified for qada', and what is required for eligibility for shahada is required for eligibility for qada'. (Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Book of Adab al-Qadi, vol. 4, p. 77) 'And the jurisdiction of the judge is not valid unless... The jurisdiction of the judge is not valid until the person appointed (using the passive participle form) combines the conditions of shahada: Islam, freedom, intellect, and [something]. And the person appointed should be from the people of ijtihad.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage establishes a key legal principle in Hanafi fiqh: the qualifications for a judge are the same as those for a witness. The author explains that both positions involve wilayah (authority to enforce one's statement on others). The rule is derived from the principle that the ruling of qada' (judgeship) is derived from shahada (testimony). Therefore, any person who is eligible to be a witness (free, Muslim, sane, etc.) is also eligible to be a judge. The text from Al-Lubab further adds that the judge must be a mujtahid. The passage also notes that the judge should be appointed by others (using the passive participle 'mawallaa') rather than seeking the position himself.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qaḍāʾ): The office of the judge; the authority to issue binding legal decisions.
الشهادة (al-shahādah): Testimony given in court; the act of bearing witness.
الولاية (al-wilāyah): Authority or jurisdiction to enforce one's statement on others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle stated in this passage, what is the relationship between a judge and a witness?
A) The judge must be more qualified than the witness.
B) The judge must have the same qualifications as the witness.
C) The witness must have the same qualifications as the judge, but not vice versa.
D) There is no relationship between the two roles.

Key Takeaway
The conditions for a judge are the same as for a witness because both roles involve wilāyah, and additionally the judge must be a mujtahid.

Conditions for a Judge: Derived from Witness Qualifications
Explains that judge's conditions are same as witness's conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions required for a Hanafi judge and explain the qiyas (analogy) that links them to the conditions for a witness.

Core Idea
A judge must have the same qualifications as a witness: Islam, freedom, sanity, uprightness, and ijtihad.

This passage establishes the qualifications for a judge (qadi) in Hanafi fiqh. It argues that since both judgeship (qada) and testimony (shahada) involve the exercise of authority (wilaya) over others, the same personal conditions apply. Understanding this qiyas helps you see the systematic reasoning of the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The ruling is that a judge must possess certain conditions for his appointment to be valid. List them from the text.
Text says: Islam, freedom, intellect, uprightness, and ijtihad.
2
Identify the Proof (Dalil)
The author uses qiyas (analogy). The underlying cause ('illa) is that both qada and shahada are forms of wilayah (authority to enforce one's statement).
Wilaya is the common element.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Because the 'illa is the same (wilaya), whatever is required for shahada is also required for qada. This is a clear analogy (qiyas jaliy).
No istihsan override is mentioned.
4
List the Conditions
From the text: Islam, freedom (hurriyya), sanity ('aql), uprightness ('adala), and ijtihad (being a Mujtahid).
Ijtihad is specifically mentioned as required.
5
Note the Cross-Madhhab Nuance
While all schools agree on most conditions, the requirement of ijtihad for the judge is emphasized here; other schools may be more lenient in non-essential cases.
This is a point of ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Conditions of testimony: because the ruling of qada is derived from the ruling of testimony, because each of them belongs to the category of wilayah. So whoever is qualified for testimony is qualified for qada, and what is required for qualification for testimony is required for qualification for qada. (Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Kitab Adab al-Qadi, vol. 4 p. 77) And the appointment of a judge is not valid unless the appointed person combines the conditions of testimony: Islam, freedom, intellect, and uprightness, and the appointed person is a Mujtahid.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof establishing the conditions for a judge (qadi). The author states the principle that the conditions for qada are derived from the conditions for shahada (testimony). The proof is that both qada and shahada fall under the same category of wilayah (authority to enforce one's statement on others). Since a witness must be Muslim, free, sane, upright ('adl), and a Mujtahid (in some contexts), the same conditions apply to a judge. The passage also emphasizes that a judge must be appointed by someone else (tawliya) and should not seek the position. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position, transmitted in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
قضاء (qaḍā’): Judgeship; the office of a judge who resolves disputes and issues binding verdicts.
شهادة (shahāda): Testimony given in court by a qualified witness.
ولاية (wilāya): Authority or jurisdiction to enforce one's decision on others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Judgment Against an Absent Defendant is Invalid
States the prohibition of passing judgment in the defendant's absence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on judgment against an absent defendant and explain the rational proof behind it.

Core Idea
Judgment without the defendant is void — the dispute is not properly before the court.

This lesson examines a fundamental principle of Hanafi qada: the invalidity of passing judgment when the defendant is absent. The text from Al-Hidayah and its commentaries establishes that the presence of both parties is a prerequisite for a valid judicial process. We will unpack the rational proof and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
What exactly is forbidden? The ruling states: القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز — judgment against the absent is not permissible. This applies to all cases where the defendant is not present in court.
The ruling is absolute; no exception is mentioned in this passage.
2
Understand the rational proof
Why is it not permissible? The proof is rational: the purpose of qada (judgment) is to settle a dispute (niza') between the plaintiff and defendant. Without the defendant, the dispute cannot be properly heard.
The commentary explains that evidence is only heard against a present opponent because the dispute is not actualized otherwise.
3
Distinguish from evidence hearing
Why is evidence only heard in the presence of both parties? Because the ruling of qada is derived from the ruling of testimony (shahada), which requires both parties to be present for cross-examination.
This connection is made in Al-Inayah: the conditions of qada are taken from shahada.
4
Note the cross-madhhab status
This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school. The Maliki school, for example, permits judgment in absentia under certain conditions, such as when the defendant has been properly notified.
The text says 'at our school' (indanā), indicating it is a point of ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْقَضَاءُ عَلَى الْغَائِبِ لَا يَجُوزُ . (الهداية كتاب أدب القاضي، باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي، ج ۳ ص ۱۴۶) والبينات لا تسمع إلا على خصم حاضر، لأن القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز عندنا. (شرح مختصر الطحاوي للجصاص : كتاب المفقود، ج۸ ص ۴۳۱) الأَوَّلُ: يَعْنِي اشْتِرَاطَ شَرَائِطِ الشَّهَادَةِ فَإِنَّ حُكْمَ الْقَضَاءِ يُسْتَقَى أَيْ يُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ حُكم الشَّهَادَةِ. (العناية: كتاب الحوالة، ج- ص ۲۵۲)
Judgment against the absent is not permissible. (Al-Hidayah, Kitab Adab al-Qadi, Bab Kitab al-Qadi ila al-Qadi, vol. 3 p. 146) And evidence is only heard against a present opponent, because judgment against the absent is not permissible according to us. (Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi li al-Jassas: Kitab al-Mafqud, vol. 8 p. 431) The first: meaning the stipulation of the conditions of testimony, for the ruling of qada is derived from the ruling of testimony. (Al-Inayah: Kitab al-Hawalah, p. 252)
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the invalidity of judgment against an absent defendant (qada ala al-gha'ib). The Hanafi position is clear: a judge cannot pass a verdict unless the defendant is present. The proof is rational: the purpose of qada is to settle a dispute between the plaintiff and defendant, which requires the defendant's presence to either admit or deny the claim. Without the defendant, the dispute is not properly before the court. The passage also reiterates the earlier principle that the conditions of qada are derived from shahada. This ruling is found in the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi position.

Key Terms
القضاء على الغائب (al-qaḍā’ ‘alā al-ghā’ib): Passing a judicial verdict against a defendant who is not present in the court.
بينات (bayyināt): Evidence, especially witness testimony, presented in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage
