A) Because the Quran explicitly forbids it.
B) Because the purpose of qada is to settle a dispute, which requires both parties to be present.
C) Because the judge might be biased without hearing the defendant.
D) Because the plaintiff must also be absent.

Key Takeaway
Judgment against an absent defendant is invalid because the judicial process requires the presence of both parties to settle the dispute.

6 min
Ruling Against an Absent Defendant is Not Permissible
Stating the prohibition of judging absent defendants and its rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on judgment against an absent defendant and explain the legal reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Judgment against an absent defendant is prohibited because the dispute requires both parties' presence.

This lesson examines a fundamental principle of Hanafi judicial procedure: a judge cannot pass a verdict in the absence of the defendant. Even if the plaintiff presents strong evidence, the defendant must be present to admit or deny the claim. The passage from al-Hidayah explains the reasoning behind this rule and its application in the context of judicial correspondence. Understanding this principle is essential for grasping the importance of due process in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Locate the sentence 'الْقَضَاءُ عَلَى الْغَائِبِ لَا يَجُوزُ' – judgment against the absent is not permissible. This is the core hukm.
2
Understand the rationale
The commentary explains that the purpose of litigation is to resolve a dispute, and a dispute only exists when both parties are present. Without the defendant, it is unknown whether they would admit or deny the claim.
The Arabic phrase 'پتہ نہیں کہ وہ اقرار کرتا ہے یا انکار' captures this uncertainty.
3
Apply to the chapter context
The author relates this principle to Kitab al-Qadi ila al-Qadi: if a judge receives witness testimony via letter and the defendant is absent, he cannot decide the case.
This shows the principle's scope extends to judicial correspondence.
4
Identify the condition for valid judgment
A judge may only decide when both litigants are present and the plaintiff presents valid evidence (hujja shar'iyya).
The passage states: 'قاضی اس وقت فیصلہ کر سکتا ہے جب کہ خصمین حاضر ہوں'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول ہدایہ ثالث
باب کتاب القاضي إلى القاضي
(۵۳) الْقَضَاءُ عَلَى الْغَائِبِ لَا يَجُوزُ .
ترجمہ: غائب کے خلاف فیصلہ کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔
۹۱
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ قاضی کے لئے صرف مدعی کے بینہ سن کر مدعی علیہ غائب کے خلاف فیصلہ کرنا جائز نہیں ہے جب تک کہ مدعی علیہ مجلس قضاء میں حاضر نہ ہو، کیونکہ قضاء کا مقصد مدعی اور مدعی علیہ کے درمیان نزاع کو ختم کرنا ہے اور نزاع اس متحقق ہو گا جب مدعی علیہ موجود ہو، اب اس کی عدم موجودگی میں پتہ نہیں کہ وہ اقرار کرتا ہے یا انکار، لہذا قضاء علی الغائب جائز نہیں ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا ر ہے ہیں کہ کتاب القاضی الی القاضی میں اگر گواہوں نے مکتوب الیہ قاضی کے پاس گواہی دے دی اور مدعی علیہ حاضر نہ ہو تو قاضی فیصلہ نہیں کرے گا، کیونکہ اگر خصم کی عدم موجودگی میں قاضی فیصلہ کر لے تو یہ قضاء علی الغائب ہے اور قضاء علی الغائب جائز نہیں ہے۔ قاضی اس وقت فیصلہ کر سکتا ہے جب کہ خصمین حاضر ہوں ، جب مدعی مدعی علیہ پر گواہ پیش کرے گا تو حجت شرعی پائی جائے گی، اور حجت شرعی پائے جانے کے بعد قاضی فیصلہ کرنے کا مکلف ہو جاتا ہے۔
Principles of Hidayah, third volume. Chapter: The Letter of the Judge to the Judge. (53) Judgment against the absent is not permissible. Translation: It is not permissible to pass a judgment against an absent person. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that it is not permissible for a judge to pass a judgment against an absent defendant merely by hearing the evidence of the plaintiff unless the defendant is present in the court, because the purpose of judgment is to resolve the dispute between the plaintiff and the defendant, and the dispute is only realized when the defendant is present. In his absence, it is not known whether he would admit or deny, therefore judgment against the absent is not permissible. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the aforementioned principle, is explaining that in the chapter of the letter of the judge to the judge, if witnesses give testimony before the judge to whom the letter is written and the defendant is not present, the judge will not decide, because if the judge decides in the absence of the opponent, it is judgment against the absent, and judgment against the absent is not permissible. The judge can decide only when both parties are present. When the plaintiff produces witnesses against the defendant, a valid legal proof (hujja shar'iyya) is established, and after the establishment of such proof, the judge becomes obligated to pass judgment.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental principle of Hanafi qada: judgment cannot be passed against an absent defendant (qada' ala al-gha'ib). The ruling is that a judge cannot decide a case solely based on the plaintiff's evidence if the defendant is not present. The reason is that the purpose of litigation is to resolve a dispute, and a dispute only exists when both parties are present. Without the defendant, it is unknown whether they would admit (iqrar) or deny (inkar) the claim. Therefore, the judge must wait for the defendant's presence. In the context of Kitab al-Qadi ila al-Qadi (the chapter on correspondence between judges), if a letter is sent from one judge to another with witness testimonies, the receiving judge cannot pass judgment if the defendant is absent. This would be qada' ala al-gha'ib, which is impermissible. Only when both litigants are present, and the plaintiff presents evidence, does a valid legal proof exist, obligating the judge to decide. This principle ensures fairness and due process.

Key Terms
قضاء على الغائب (qada' ala al-gha'ib): Passing a judicial verdict against a person who is not present in the court and has not been given an opportunity to defend themselves.
بينة (bayyina): Legal evidence, typically testimony of witnesses, that establishes a claim in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is judgment against the absent (qada' ala al-gha'ib) not permissible in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the plaintiff's evidence is not reliable.
B) Because the dispute is not verified without the defendant's presence to admit or deny.
C) Because the judge lacks jurisdiction over an absent person.
D) Because the defendant must always be present at the first hearing.

Key Takeaway
Judgment against an absent defendant is prohibited in Hanafi fiqh because the dispute is not verified without the defendant's presence.

Verification of a Judge's Letter: Two Witnesses Required
Explaining that a judge's letter requires witness verification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement for accepting a judge's letter and apply it by identifying the minimum number and gender of witnesses required.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is accepted only with two witnesses or one man and two women to prevent forgery.

This lesson explains the procedural safeguard in Hanafi judiciary for written communications between judges. Because letters can be forged, the receiving judge must verify the sender's identity through live witnesses before acting on the letter. This rule balances efficiency with evidentiary integrity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Recognize the scenario: a judge sends a written letter (or document) to another judge containing a judgment or testimony that requires implementation.
This is called Kitab al-Qadi ila al-Qadi.
2
State the ruling
The receiving judge must not accept the letter as binding until it is verified by witnesses. Mere presentation or a seal is insufficient.
The hukm is wajib: verification is obligatory before execution.
3
Determine the condition
The verification requires two just male witnesses, or one male and two female witnesses, who testify that the letter is from the sender and that they recognise his handwriting and seal.
This constitutes a hujja tamma (complete proof).
4
Understand the rationale
The principle 'al-kitabu yushbihu al-kitab' (one letter resembles another) means that without witness testimony, forgery cannot be ruled out.
Seals can be copied; handwriting can be imitated.
5
Recognise the legal effect
Once verified, the letter becomes binding on the defendant because it carries the authority of the sender-judge. Without complete proof (hujja tamma), no obligation can be imposed.
The letter itself is not a proof; it is a means of conveying the proof.

Source Text

اصول ہدایہ ثالث
(۵۴) الْكِتَابُ يَشْبَهُ الْكِتَابَ .
ترجمہ: ایک خط دوسرے خط کے مشابہ ہوتا ہے۔
۹۲
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ایک خط دوسرے خط کے مشابہ ہوتا ہے، لہذا صرف خط پر اعتماد نہیں کیا جائے گا جب تک دو گواہ گواہی نہ دیں کہ یہ فلاں کا خط ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر کوئی قاضی کسی قاضی کے پاس اپنا خط یا تحریر بھیجے تو مکتوب الیہ قاضی کو چاہیے کہ وہ اس خط کو تب قبول کرے جب دو عادل مرد یا ایک مرد اور دو عورتیں اس کی گواہی دیں کہ یہ فلاں قاضی کا خط ہے، اور ہم اس کی تحریر اور مہر سے واقف ہیں، اس لیے کہ ایک خط دوسرے خط کے مشابہ ہوتا ہے، لہذا حجت تامہ کے بغیر کوئی تحریر ثابت نہیں ہوگی، کیونکہ خط مدعی علیہ پر قاضی کے فیصلے کو لازم کرنے والا ہوتا ہے اور حجت تامہ کے بغیر کسی چیز کو لازم نہیں کیا جاسکتا۔
Principles of Hidayah, third volume. (54) A letter resembles another letter. Translation: One letter resembles another letter. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that one letter resembles another letter, therefore mere reliance on a letter is not allowed unless two witnesses testify that this is so-and-so's letter. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the aforementioned principle, is explaining that if a judge sends his letter or writing to another judge, the judge to whom it is sent should accept that letter only when two just men or one man and two women testify that this is so-and-so's letter and that they are familiar with his writing and seal, because one letter resembles another letter. Therefore, without a complete proof (hujja tamma), no writing will be established, because the letter obliges the judge's decision upon the defendant, and something cannot be made obligatory without a complete proof.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage establishes the procedural rule for accepting written correspondence between judges (Kitab al-Qadi ila al-Qadi). The principle 'al-kitabu yushbihu al-kitab' means that one piece of writing can resemble another, so forgery is possible. Therefore, a letter from a judge cannot be accepted merely because it is presented; it must be verified by witnesses. The receiving judge must require two just male witnesses, or one male and two female witnesses, to testify that the letter is indeed from the sender-judge and that they recognize his handwriting and seal. This is because the letter carries legal weight — it becomes binding on the defendant (the person against whom the letter's contents are directed). Without a complete proof (hujja tamma), such a binding cannot be imposed. This ensures the authenticity and integrity of judicial communication. The principle guards against fraud and upholds the standard of evidence required in judicial matters.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi): A written communication from one judge to another, containing a judgment or testimony to be executed in the jurisdiction of the receiving judge.
حجة تامة (hujja tamma): A complete or full proof that is legally sufficient to establish a claim or fact, in this context the testimony of two witnesses or one man and two women.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One just male witness.
B) Two just men or one man and two women.
C) Only the seal of the judge is sufficient.
D) Two women are enough.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter is accepted only upon witness verification (two men or one man and two women) because one letter can resemble another, requiring complete proof before imposing a legal obligation.

A Letter Resembles a Letter: Acceptance Requires Full Proof
Explains the principle that a letter is like another letter, requiring witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the acceptance of a judge's letter and explain why witness testimony is required.

Core Idea
A judge's letter alone is insufficient; it must be verified by two witnesses because letters can be forged.

In the Hanafi school, written correspondence between judges is not automatically accepted. The principle that 'a letter resembles a letter' means that handwriting can be imitated, so the authenticity of a judge's letter must be proven through admissible testimony. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal subject and ruling
The subject is a judge's letter (kitab al-qadi) sent to another judge. The ruling: it is not accepted (la yuqbal) unless with the testimony of two men or one man and two women.
2
Understand the underlying principle
The principle is 'al-kitabu yushbihu al-kitab' – one letter resembles another. This implies that written documents alone are not reliable because forgery is possible.
3
Connect the principle to the ruling
Because letters can be forged, complete proof (hujja tamma) is required. The letter becomes binding on the defendant, so certainty is needed to enforce it.
4
Note the condition for enforcement
Even with a verified letter, if there is no opponent or representative present, the judge cannot rule, because judgment on the absent is impermissible.

The book resembles the book. Translation: One letter resembles another letter. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that one letter resembles another letter, therefore one will not rely on the letter alone until two witnesses testify that it is so-and-so's letter. The author of Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is stating that if a judge sends his letter or writing to another judge, the recipient judge should accept that letter only when two upright men or one man and two women testify that this is the letter of such-and-such judge, and they are familiar with his writing and seal, because one letter resembles another letter, hence no writing is proven without complete proof (hujjah tamah), since the letter makes the judge's verdict binding on the defendant, and nothing can be made binding without complete proof. Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the acceptance of a judge's letter to another judge. The principle stated is that one letter resembles another (al-kitabu yushbihu al-kitab), meaning that a written document alone is not reliable proof of its authorship because handwriting can be imitated. Therefore, for a letter to be accepted as valid evidence, it must be corroborated by full testimony (hujjah tamah): either two upright men or one man and two women who can attest that the letter indeed belongs to the sender and that they recognize his handwriting and seal. The underlying reason is that the judge's letter carries a legal ruling that becomes binding on the defendant, and nothing can be made binding without a complete proof. The passage also clarifies that if there is no adversary or representative present, the recipient judge cannot rule based solely on the letter, as judgment against an absent person is not permissible. This ruling is drawn from the Hidayah and other Hanafi works, emphasizing the need for caution in accepting written correspondence in judicial matters.

Key Terms
الكتاب (al-kitāb): A written document, specifically a judge's letter to another judge.
شهادة (shahādah): The testimony of witnesses required to verify the authenticity of a judge's letter.
حجة تامة (ḥujjat tāmah): Complete proof, consisting of admissible testimony that establishes the fact beyond doubt.

Enforceability of a Qadi's Judgment in Matters of Ijtihad
Stating finality of judgment in ijtihad matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a qadi's judgment in a matter of ijtihad is final and binding, and explain why a second qadi cannot overturn it.

Core Idea
A qadi's ruling in an ijtihad matter is final due to the preponderance gained from its connection to judgment.

In the Hanafi judicial system, once a qualified judge issues a ruling on a matter where scholars differ, that ruling becomes binding. Even if another judge holds a differing opinion, he must enforce the first ruling. This lesson explains the rationale based on the equality of ijtihad and the preponderance gained through connection with judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Rule
State the rule: a qadi's judgment in a matter of ijtihad is executed and cannot be rejected by another.
The rule applies only to matters open to ijtihad, not to definitive texts.
2
Understand the Condition
Identify what 'matter of ijtihad' means: an issue where qualified scholars may differ due to the absence of a clear text or consensus.
If the ruling contradicts a definitive text, the judgment is invalid and can be overturned.
3
Analyze the Rationale
The reason given is that both the first and second ijtihad are equal in probability (zann). Since they are equal, the first gains preponderance because it is connected with the actual act of judgment (qada).
This connection makes the first ruling binding, not because it is more correct, but because it has been enacted.
4
Apply to a Scenario
Consider a case where a qadi rules that a certain sale is valid based on a valid ijtihad. Later, another qadi who holds a different ijtihad rules the same sale invalid. The second ruling is ineffective; the first ruling stands.
This ensures stability in judicial decisions and prevents endless revision.
5
Compare with Other Schools
Note that the Shafi'i school permits a later qadi to overturn a previous judgment if he believes it to be erroneous, but Hanafis restrict this to maintain finality.
The difference stems from how each school weighs the likelihood of error and the need for legal certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْقَضَاءُ مَتَى لَاقَى فَصْلًا مُجْتَهَدًا فِيْهِ يَنْفُذُ وَلَا يَرُدُّهُ غَيْرُهُ.
الهداية: كتاب أدب القاضي: باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي، ج ۳ ص ۴۹ لأن القضاء متى لاقى محلا مجتهداً فيه ينفذ ولا يرده غيره، لأن الاجتهاد الثاني كالاجتهاد الأول لتساويهما فى الظن، وقد ترجح الأول باتصال القضاء به، فلا
Judgment, when it meets a matter of ijtihad, is executed and no one else can reject it. Al-Hidayah: Kitab Adab al-Qadi, Chapter of Kitab al-Qadi ila al-Qadi, vol. 3 p. 49: Because judgment, when it meets a place of ijtihad, is executed and no one else can reject it, because the second ijtihad is like the first ijtihad, both equal in probability, and the first is preponderant due to its connection with judgment, so...
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a key rule in Hanafi judicial procedure: when a qadi issues a ruling on a matter that is subject to ijtihad (i.e., a disputed issue among scholars), that ruling becomes enforceable and cannot be overturned by another judge. The reason is that both judges' ijtihad is equally probable (zann), but the first judge's ruling gains preponderance (tarjih) because it is connected with the actual act of judgment (qada). Thus, the first ruling stands, and the second judge must enforce it even if his own ijtihad differs.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): The act of a judge issuing a binding judgment in a legal dispute.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning by a qualified scholar to derive a ruling from primary sources.
ترجيح (tarjih): Preponderance: giving weight to one evidence or ruling over another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the second qadi's ijtihad is invalid.
D) Because the matter is not open to ijtihad.

Key Takeaway
A qadi's judgment in a matter of ijtihad is final and binding because the first ijtihad gains preponderance through its connection with the judgment, preventing instability.

Principle of Judicial Rulings in Ijtihadi Matters
Explaining the principle of judicial rulings in ijtihādī matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that a judge's ruling in an ijtihādī matter is binding and irreversible, and explain the reasoning based on the equality of ijtihād and the attachment of the judgment.

Core Idea
A judge's ijtihād becomes binding once attached to a ruling; a second judge cannot overturn it with an equal or weaker ijtihād.

This lesson introduces a foundational maxim of Hanafi judicial procedure: once a judge renders a decision on a matter where scholars legitimately differ, that decision is final and cannot be undone by another judge. Understanding this principle is crucial for maintaining stability in the Islamic legal system and respecting the authority of the judiciary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The maxim states that when a judgment deals with a matter subject to ijtihād, it is enforced and cannot be rejected by another judge.
Focus on the phrase 'فصلا مجتهدا فيه'.
2
Understand the rationale
Both the first and second ijtihād are equal in terms of probability (ẓann). However, the first ijtihād has been actualized by the judge's ruling, giving it precedence.
The text says: 'الاجتهاد الثاني كالاجتهاد الأول لتساويهما فى الظن، وقد ترجح الأول باتصال القضاء به'.
3
Recognize the implication
A later judge cannot overturn the ruling with a different ijtihād because that would be overturning a stronger (due to attachment) with a weaker (without attachment).
The maxim is 'لا ينقض بما هو دونه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْقَضَاءُ مَتَى لَاقَى فَصْلًا مُجْتَهَدًا فِيْهِ يَنْفُذُ وَلَا يَرُدُّهُ غَيْرُهُ. لأن القضاء متى لاقى محلا مجتهدا فيه ينفذ ولا يرده غيره، لأن الاجتهاد الثاني كالاجتهاد الأول لتساويهما فى الظن، وقد ترجح الأول باتصال القضاء به، فلا ينقض بما هو دونه. (اللباب في شرح الكتاب: كتاب أدب القاضي، ج ٤ ص ٨٧) (والأصل) أي في تنفيذ القاضى ما رفع إليه إذا لم يكن مخالفا للأدلة المذكورة أن القضاء متى لاقى فصلا مجتهدا فيه وفي بعض النسخ: محلا مجتهدا فيه ينفذ ولا يرده غيره، لأن الاجتهاد الثاني كاجتهاد الأول في أن كلا منهما يحتمل الخطأ وقد يرجح الاجتهاد الأول باتصال القضاء به، فلا ينقض بما هو دونه) درجة، وهو لم يتصل القضاء به. (البناية: كتاب أدب القاضي، باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي، ج ٩ ص ٤٩)
The judgment, when it meets a matter subject to ijtihad, is enforced and no other [judge] can reject it. Because the judgment, when it meets a place subject to ijtihad, is enforced and no other can reject it, because the second ijtihad is like the first ijtihad in that both are equal in probability, and the first has been given precedence by the connection of the judgment to it, so it cannot be overturned by something lesser than it.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This is a legal maxim from the chapter on judgeship. The principle states that when a judge passes a ruling on a matter that is open to ijtihād (i.e., there are valid differing opinions among scholars), that ruling is enforceable and cannot be overturned by another judge. The reasoning is that both ijtihāds are equal in terms of probability of error, but the first ijtihād gains precedence because it has been attached to the judge's actual ruling. Therefore, a subsequent judge cannot reverse it with an inferior ijtihād. The text quotes from al-Lubāb and al-Bināyah to reinforce this point.

Key Terms
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): The judgment or ruling issued by a judge.
فصل مجتهد فيه (faṣl mujtahad fīhi): A matter that is subject to ijtihād, meaning there are valid scholarly disagreements.
ينقض بما هو دونه (yanquḍu bimā huwa dūnahu): To overturn a ruling with something of lesser authority or weight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because ijtihād is equal.
B) No, because the first ruling is already enforced.
C) No, because the first judge's ijtihād is considered superior due to its attachment to the ruling.
D) Yes, if the second judge is more knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
A ruling on an ijtihādī matter is binding and irreversible by a later judge because the first ijtihād gains precedence through its attachment to the judgment.

10 min
The Apparent State Cannot Prove Entitlement
Explaining a legal maxim with an inheritance case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-dhāhir lā yaṣluḥu ḥujjatan lil-istiḥqāq' to distinguish between using a present state as positive proof (muthbita) versus defensive proof (dāfi'a).

Core Idea
A person's current state cannot retroactively prove a right; it can only defend an existing claim.

This lesson unpacks one of the foundational procedural maxims in Hanafi fiqh: that the apparent, observable state of affairs (al-dhāhir) does not carry enough weight to establish a legal entitlement. Instead, it serves only to repel a claim. We will see how this principle is applied in a classic inheritance dispute between a Christian widow and the Muslim heirs, and why the Ḥanafī school restricts the use of istiṣḥāb (presumption of continuity) to defensive purposes only.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
State the maxim 'al-dhāhir lā yaṣluḥu ḥujjatan lil-istiḥqāq' and its basic meaning: the present apparent state cannot serve as positive proof for claiming a right.
Keep in mind the distinction between hujja muthbita (positive proof) and hujja dāfi'a (defensive proof).
2
Analyze the fact pattern
Consider the case: a Muslim man dies leaving a Christian wife. After his death, the wife appears as a Muslim and claims she converted before his death, thus she is entitled to inherit. The heirs deny this, claiming she converted after death.
The wife's present Islam is the 'zāhir' she relies on.
3
Apply the maxim
According to the maxim, the wife's current state (Islam) cannot be used to prove that she was Muslim at the time of death. Therefore, the heirs' statement (that she converted later) is accepted, because they are defending the status quo (her Christianity at death).
The maxim prevents the wife from using istiṣḥāb as positive proof.
4
Understand the role of istiṣḥāb
Istiṣḥāb (presumption of continuity) can only be used defensively in the Hanafi school. The heirs can use istiṣḥāb to say 'she was Christian before, so she remains Christian until proven otherwise' — but the wife cannot use it to say 'she is Muslim now, so she was Muslim before'.
This is a key difference from the Shafi'i school, which allows istiṣḥāb for both positive and defensive purposes.
5
Derive the ruling
The qadi must rule in favor of the heirs. The wife's claim is rejected because it would require making the apparent state a positive proof, which is invalid under this maxim.
The ruling is consistent with the principle that 'al-ṣaḥīḥ lā yanquli al-ḥukm' (the sound does not transfer the ruling) — but here the maxim directly prohibits using the apparent state for istiḥqāq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحكيم
(۵۷) الظَّاهِرُ لَا يَصْلُحُ حُجَّةً لِلِاسْتِحْقَاقِ.
ترجمہ: ظاہر حال استحقاق کے لئے حجت بنے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ظاہر حال حجت مثبتہ یعنی اس کے ذریعے کسی شے کا استحقاق ثابت نہیں ہوسکتا، البتہ محبت دافعہ بن سکتا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر مسلمان آدمی مر گیا اور اس کی بیوی جو کہ نصرانیہ ہے اس کے مرنے کے بعد اس کے ورثہ کے پاس مسلمان ہو کر آتی ہے اور کہتی ہے کہ میں نے اپنے شوہر کی وفات سے قبل اسلام لا یا تھا یعنی شوہر کی موت کے وقت میں مسلمان تھی، لہذا مجھے وراثت ملنی چاہیے، اور ورثہ نے کہا نہیں آپ ان کی وفات کے بعد مسلمان ہوئی ہو، تو اس صورت میں ورثہ کے قول کا اعتبار ہوگا، کیونکہ عورت اپنے ظاہری حالت (اسلام) سے وراثت کے استحقاق کا دعوی کرتی ہے، اور ظاہر حال استحقاق کے لئے حجت نہیں بن سکتا، لہذا عورت کی بھی ظاہری حالت وراثت کے استحقاق کے لئے ينقض بما هو دونه. اللباب في شرح الكتاب: كتاب أدب القاضي، ج ۴ ص ۸۷) (والأصل) أي في تنفيذ القاضى ما رفع إليه إذا لم يكن مخالفا للأدلة المذكورة أن القضاء متى لاقى فصلا مجتهدا فيه وفى بعض النسخ: محلا مجتهدا فيه ينفذ ولا يرده غيره، لأن الاجتهاد الثاني كاجتهاد الأول في أن كلا منهما يحتمل الخطأ وقد يرجح الاجتهاد الأول باتصال القضاء به، فلا ينقض بما هو دونه) درجة، وهـو لـم يتصل القضاء به. (البناية: كتاب أدب القاضي، باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي، ج ۹ ص ۴۹) الهداية كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج ۳ ص ۱۵۵
حجت نہیں بن سکتی، یہاں استصحاب حال کو حکم بنا کر یہ نہیں کہا جائے گا کہ یہ عورت فی الحال مسلمان ہے، لہذا ماضی میں بھی مسلمان رہی ہوگی، کیونکہ ایسا کرنے سے وہ عورت مستحق میراث قرار پائے گی اور استصحاب حال کا حجت مثبتہ ہونا لا زم آئے گا، حالانکہ ہمارے یہاں استصحاب حال حجبت دافعہ ہے، لہذاوہ حجبت مثبتہ نہیں بنے گا اور نہ ہی استصحاب حال کو حکم بنا کر اس عورت کے لیے میراث کے استحقاق کا فیصلہ کیا جائے گا۔ البتہ میت کے ورثاء چونکہ اس عورت کو میراث سے دفع کر رہے ہیں ، اس لیے یہاں استصحاب حال ان کے لیے حجت ہوگا ، اور یہ کہا جائے گا کہ یہ عورت ماضی میں نصرانی تھی ، اس لیے شوہر کی موت کے وقت بھی نصرانی ہی ہے، لہذا اختلاف دین کی وجہ سے میراث کی مستحق نہیں ہے۔ وَكَذَا لَوْ مَاتَ مُسْلِمٌ فَقَالَتْ زَوْجَتُهُ النَّصْرَانِيَّةُ أَسْلَمْتُ قَبْلَ مَوْتِهِ) وَلِي اسْتِحْقَاقُ الْمِيرَاثِ وَقَالَ الْوَارِثُ بَلْ أَسْلَمْتِ بَعْدَهُ) وَلَيْسَ لَكَ الْمِيرَاتُ، يَعْنِي يَكُونُ الْقَوْلُ لِلْوَارِثِ أَيْضًا، وَلَا يُحَكِّمُ الْحَالُ لأَنَّ الظَّاهِرَ لَا يَصْلُحُ حُجَّةً لِلاسْتِحْقَاقِ وَهِيَ مُحْتَاجَةٌ إِلَيْهِ، أَمَّا الْوَرَثَةُ فَهُمُ الدَّافِعُونَ وَيَشْهَدُ لَهُمْ ظَاهِرُ الْحُدُوثِ أَيْضًا كَمَا فِي الْهِدَايَةِ، وَالتَّعْبِيرُ بِالاسْتِصْحَابِ أَحْسَنُ مِنْ التَّعْبِيرِ بِالظَّاهِرِ فَإِنَّ مَا ثَبَتَ بِهِ الاسْتِحْقَاقُ كَثِيرًا مَا يَكُونُ ظَاهِرًا كَإِخْبَارِ الْآحَادِ كَثِيرًا مَا يُوجِبُ اسْتِحْقَاقًا كَمَا فِي الْفَتْح مجمع الأنهر : كتاب القضاء، فصل في القضاء بالمواريث، ج ۲ ص ۱۷۹)
وَلَوْ مَاتَ الْمُسْلِمُ وَلَهُ امْرَأَةٌ نَصْرَانِيَّةٌ فَجَاءَتْ مُسْلِمَةٌ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ وَقَالَتْ أَسْلَمْتُ قَبْلَ مَوْتِهِ وَقَالَتِ الْوَرَثَةُ أَسْلَمْتِ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْوَرَثَةِ أَيْضًا وَلَا يُحَكُمُ الْحَالُ)، لأنَّ تَحْكِيمَهُ يُؤَدِّى إِلَى جَعْلِهِ حُجَّةً لِلِاسْتِحْقَاقِ الَّذِي هِيَ مُحْتَاجَةٌ إِلَيْهِ وَهُوَ لَا يَصْلُحُ لِذَلِكَ، وَبِهَذَا الْقَدْرِ يَتِمُّ الدَّلِيلُ. (العناية: كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج۷ ص ۳۴۰)
The apparent state does not serve as a proof for entitlement. Translation: The apparent state does not have the capacity to be a proof for entitlement. Explanation: This principle means that the apparent state (zahir hal) is a positive proof (hujja muthbita) but cannot establish entitlement to anything; rather, it can only act as a defensive proof (hujja dafi'a). The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle and then explains that if a Muslim man dies and his Christian wife comes after his death as a Muslim and claims she converted before his death, so she should inherit, while the heirs claim she converted after his death, then the word of the heirs is accepted. This is because the woman uses her apparent state (Islam) to claim entitlement, but the apparent state cannot be a proof for entitlement. It cannot be said by istishab (presumption of continuity) that since she is now Muslim, she was Muslim before, because that would make istishab a positive proof, whereas according to us, istishab is only a defensive proof. So the heirs can use istishab to defend against her claim. The Arabic text from al-Hidayah: 'And similarly if a Muslim dies and his Christian wife says, "I accepted Islam before his death" and she claims inheritance, and the heir says "Rather you accepted after his death" and you have no inheritance, meaning the statement is that of the heir, and the current state is not judged because the apparent does not serve as a proof for entitlement, and she needs it. As for the heirs, they are the defenders and the apparent of occurrence (zahir al-huduth) also supports them.' And in al-Inayah: 'And if a Muslim dies and he has a Christian wife, then she comes as a Muslim after his death and says "I accepted before his death" and the heirs say "you accepted after his death", then the statement is that of the heirs, and the current state is not judged because judging by it would lead to making it a proof for entitlement which she needs, and it is not valid for that.'
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage from Usool-e-Hidayah expounds a foundational legal maxim (qawida) of the Hanafi school: that a present apparent state (zahir) cannot be used to establish a right retrospectively, but only to defend an existing right. The author gives the concrete example of a Christian widow who converts to Islam and claims she converted before her husband's death to inherit. The ruling: the heirs' statement is taken, not the wife's apparent Islam now. The commentary explains why: the wife's claim would require using istishab (presumption of continuity based on her present state) as a positive proof to establish entitlement, but Hanafi usul restricts istishab to being only a defensive proof (hujja dafi'a). The heirs, on the other hand, are defending the status quo (that she was Christian at the time of death) and can use istishab to support their position. The passage also notes that the 'apparent of occurrence' (zahir al-huduth) – i.e., the fact that her conversion is recent – also supports the heirs. The text concludes with a reference to al-Inayah confirming the principle. This maxim is a key distinction between Hanafi and Shafi'i schools, as the latter allows istishab for positive proof as well.

Key Terms
ظَاهِر (dhāhir): The apparent or manifest state of affairs that is readily observable, such as someone's current religious affiliation.
حُجَّة (hujjah): A proof or argument that establishes a legal claim or defense. The text distinguishes between hujja muthbita (positive proof to establish a right) and hujja dafi'a (defensive proof to repel a claim).
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Entitlement to a right, here specifically inheritance (mirath).
استصحاب حال (istiṣḥāb al-ḥāl): Presumption of continuity; the legal principle that a state of affairs is presumed to continue until evidence of change arises. In this passage, it is used to defend the status quo.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a Christian woman claims inheritance after her Muslim husband's death, saying she converted before his death, while the heirs say she converted after, whose statement is accepted according to the Hanafi maxim?
A) The wife's statement, because she is currently Muslim
B) The heirs' statement, because the apparent state cannot establish entitlement
C) Both statements are given equal weight
D) The case is decided by drawing lots

Key Takeaway
The present apparent state (zāhir) can only be used to defend a right, never to establish one; this is a core procedural maxim in Hanafi fiqh that prevents reliance on mere appearance for claiming entitlements.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
No Liability for Imam, Judge, and His Trustee
Stating a principle exempting public officials from liability due to public need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that exempts the Imam, judge, and his trustee from liability (daman) and apply it to determine liability in cases of property loss in official capacity.

Core Idea
Public officials are not personally liable for property lost in their official capacity — otherwise no one would accept the office.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle from Usul al-Hidayah: that public officials such as the Imam, judge, and the judge's appointed trustee are exempt from liability for property lost or destroyed in the course of their duties. The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) driven by public need. Understanding this principle clarifies why the Hanafi school treats public officials differently from private individuals and highlights the role of maslahah in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the principle: liability (daman) does not attach to the Imam, judge, and his trustee.
This is the core ruling.
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the proof: istihsan based on public need (hajah). The text gives the rationale: holding them liable would deter qualified people from accepting these offices.
The dalil is not a text but a rational necessity.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the rationale connects to the ruling: if liability were imposed, no one would accept the position, harming Muslims' rights. Therefore, exemption is necessary.
This is a maslahah-based argument.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Recognize that the Shafi'i school holds judges liable if negligent. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf on the scope of exemption.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
5
Apply to the example
Work through the example: a deceased debtor's slave is sold by the judge/trustee; the price is lost, and the slave turns out to belong to another. No liability because the seller acted in official capacity.
The example clarifies the principle.

Source Text

اصول ہدایہ ثالث

(۵۹) اَلضَّمَانُ لَا يَلْحَقُ الْإِمَامَ وَالْقَاضِي وَأَمِيْنَهُ.
ترجمہ: امام، قاضی اور اس کے امین پر ضمان لاحق نہیں ہوتا۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ امام وقت، قاضی اور قاضی جس کو امین بنائے ، ان حضرات سے اگر کوئی شے تلف ہو جائے تو ان پر ضمان نہیں آئے گا، کیونکہ اگران کو ضامن بنایا جائے تو پھر ان عہدوں کو قبول کرنے کے لئے کوئی تیار نہیں ہوگا ، جس کے نتیجہ میں مسلمانوں کے حقوق ضائع ہوں گے۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکور و اصول ذکر کر کے یہ اقدار ہے ہیں کہ اگر کوئی شخص مر گیا اور اس پر لوگوں کے قرضے تھے اور اس میت نے مثلاً غلام چھوڑا تھا، پھر قرض خواہوں کے قرضے کی ادائیگی کے لئے قاضی نے یا قاضی کے امین نے اس غلام کو بیچ دیا اور مشتری سے ثمن وصول کر لیا لیکن پھر وہ مشن ضائع ہو گیا اور مینی یعنی غلام کسی دوسرے کا متفق نکل گیا تو بیچنے والے پر شمن کا ضمان واجب نہیں ہوگا ، خواہ وہ قاضی ہو یا اس کا امین ہو، اس لیے کہ قاضی کا امین قاضی کے قائم مقام ہوتا ہے اور قاضی امیر المسلمین کے قائم مقام ہوتا ہے اور ان میں سے کسی پر کوئی مضمان نہیں ہوتا، کیونکہ اگر ہم ان میں سے کسی پر مضمان واجب کریں تو کوئی شخص الْوَكَالَةِ قَبْلَ عِلْمِ الْوَكِيلِ بِهَا. (المبسوط للسرخسي: كتاب الوكالة، باب توكيل الزوح بالطلاق والخلع، ج ۱۹ ص ۱۲۷) قَالَ وَمَنْ أَوْصَى إِلَيْهِ وَلَمْ يَعْلَمُ إِلَخُ وَمَنْ أَوْصَى إِلَيْهِ وَلَمْ يَعْلَمُ بِالْوِصَايَةِ حَتَّى بَاعَ شَيْئًا مِنَ الشَّرِكَةِ فَهُوَ وَصِيٌّ وَبَيْعُهُ جَائِةٌ، وَإِذَا وُكِّلَ وَلَمْ يَعْلَمُ بِالْوَكَالَةِ حَتَّى بَاعَ لَمْ يَجُزُ بَيْعُهُ.. وَوَجْهُ الْفَرْقِ بَيْنَهُمَا عَلَى ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَةِ أَنَّ الْوِصَايَةَ خِلَافَةٌ لَا نِيَابَةٌ لِأَنَّهَا مُضَافَةٌ إِلَى زَمَان بُطَلان النِّيَابَةِ، وَالْخِلَافَةُ لَا تَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الْعِلْمِ فِي التَّصَرُّف. (العناية: كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج۷ ص ۳۵۴)
الهداية كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج ۳ ص ۱۵۸
Principles of Hidayah, third volume, page 98. (59) Liability does not attach to the Imam, the judge, and his trustee. Translation: Liability does not apply to the Imam, the judge, and his trustee. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that if any item is destroyed with these individuals (the Imam of the time, the judge, and the one whom the judge appoints as trustee), then liability will not be imposed on them. Because if they were made liable, then no one would be willing to accept these positions, resulting in the loss of rights of Muslims. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, states that if a person dies and people have debts against him, and the deceased left, for example, a slave, then for the repayment of the debts, the judge or the judge's trustee sells that slave and receives the price from the buyer, but then that price is lost, and the slave turns out to belong to someone else (i.e., was not owned by the deceased), then liability for the price will not be obligatory on the seller, whether he is the judge or his trustee. This is because the judge's trustee stands in the place of the judge, and the judge stands in the place of the Commander of the Faithful, and none of them are subject to liability. Because if we impose liability on any of them, then no one...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents another principle from Usul al-Hidayah: 'Liability does not attach to the Imam, the judge, and his trustee.' The author explains that public officials are exempted from liability (daman) for property lost or destroyed in their official capacity. The reason is practical: if they were held liable, no qualified person would accept these positions, and the rights of the community would be harmed. An example is given: a deceased debtor leaves a slave; the judge or his trustee sells the slave to pay debts, but the sale price is lost and the slave turns out to have been owned by someone else. The seller (judge or trustee) is not liable for the price. This is because the trustee is the deputy of the judge, and the judge is the deputy of the Imam, and they are all exempt from liability due to public need (hajah). The ruling is based on istihsan. The Hanafi position is that no daman is imposed on these officials, and this is the mufta bihi view.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or destruction of property.
الإمام (al-imam): The supreme leader of the Muslim community; the caliph.
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge appointed by the Imam to adjudicate disputes.
الأمين (al-amin): A trustee appointed by the judge to handle property or execute tasks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle, why are the Imam, judge, and his trustee not liable for property lost in their official capacity?
A) Because they are above the law.
B) Because holding them liable would deter people from accepting these positions, harming public interest.
C) Because they never make mistakes.
D) Because the property is always insured.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle exempts public officials from liability for property lost in official capacity to protect public interest, justified by istihsan.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
Immunity of Imam, Qadi, and Amin from Liability
Ruling and proof for immunity of top public officials.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the Imam, Qadi, and his Amin are not liable for property lost in their custody and explain the istihsan-based rationale involving public interest.

Core Idea
To protect public welfare, the Imam, Qadi, and his deputy are exempt from personal liability for lost property.

This lesson explores a unique Hanafi principle that exempts the highest public officials — the Imam, the Qadi, and his appointed Amin — from financial liability for property that is lost or destroyed in their official capacity. The ruling is not based on a direct text but on istihsan (juristic preference) grounded in public interest (maslaha). Understanding this rule reveals how Islamic law balances individual rights with the smooth functioning of governance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the general rule: the Imam, Qadi, and Amin are not liable for property lost in their hands. Note that this is an exception to the general principle that a person is liable for destroying others' property.
The ruling is specifically for property in their official custody, not for personal acts.
2
Understand the proof (dalil)
The proof is istihsan: the rationale that imposing liability would deter individuals from accepting these public offices, leading to neglect of Muslim interests. This consideration overrides the general rule of liability.
The dalil is not a textual source but a rational argument based on public welfare.
3
Examine the illustrative example
The author from Al-Hidayah provides a case: a deceased person with debts leaves a slave; the Qadi sells the slave to pay creditors, the price is received but then lost, and the slave turns out to belong to someone else. The Qadi is not liable for the lost price to the buyer.
The example shows immunity even when the sale is invalid due to ownership dispute.
4
Trace the chain of deputyship
The Amin is a deputy of the Qadi, and the Qadi is a deputy of the Imam. Hence the immunity flows from the Imam down to the Amin. This chain ensures that the principle applies uniformly.
The hierarchical connection emphasizes that immunity is necessary for the entire judicial system.
5
Relate to the broader objective
The ultimate goal is protection of the rights of the Muslim community (hifz huquq al-ibad). By removing personal financial risk, the system encourages qualified individuals to serve.
This is a classic application of istihsan overriding qiyas (analogy) for public benefit.

اَلضَّمَانُ لَا يَلْحَقُ الْإِمَامَ وَالْقَاضِي وَأَمِيْنَهُ.
ترجمہ: امام، قاضی اور اس کے امین پر ضمان لاحق نہیں ہوتا۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ امام وقت، قاضی اور قاضی جس کو امین بنائے، ان حضرات سے اگر کوئی شے تلف ہو جائے تو ان پر ضمان نہیں آئے گا، کیونکہ اگران کو ضامن بنایا جائے تو پھر ان عہدوں کو قبول کرنے کے لئے کوئی تیار نہیں ہوگا، جس کے نتیجہ میں مسلمانوں کے حقوق ضائع ہوں گے۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ اضافہ ہے ہیں کہ اگر کوئی شخص مر گیا اور اس پر لوگوں کے قرضے تھے اور اس میت نے مثلاً غلام چھوڑا تھا، پھر قرض خواہوں کے قرضے کی ادائیگی کے لئے قاضی نے یا قاضی کے امین نے اس غلام کو بیچ دیا اور مشتری سے ثمن وصول کر لیا لیکن پھر وہ ثمن ضائع ہو گیا اور وہی یعنی غلام کسی دوسرے کا مستحق نکل گیا تو بیچنے والے پر ثمن کا ضمان واجب نہیں ہوگا، خواہ وہ قاضی ہو یا اس کا امین ہو، اس لیے کہ قاضی کا امین قاضی کے قائم مقام ہوتا ہے اور قاضی امیر المسلمین کے قائم مقام ہوتا ہے اور ان میں سے کسی پر کوئی ضمان نہیں ہوتا، کیونکہ اگر ہم ان میں سے کسی پر ضمان واجب کریں تو کوئی شخص الْوَكَالَةِ قَبْلَ عِلْمِ الْوَكِيلِ بِهَا. (المبسوط للسرخسي: كتاب الوكالة، باب توكيل الزوج بالطلاق والخلع، ج ۱۹ ص ۱۲۷) قَالَ وَمَنْ أَوْصَى إِلَيْهِ وَلَمْ يَعْلَمُ بِالْوِصَايَةِ حَتَّى بَاعَ شَيْئًا مِنَ الشَّرِكَةِ فَهُوَ وَصِيٌّ وَبَيْعُهُ جَائِزٌ، وَإِذَا وُكِّلَ وَلَمْ يَعْلَمُ بِالْوَكَالَةِ حَتَّى بَاعَ لَمْ يَجُزْ بَيْعُهُ.. وَوَجْهُ الْفَرْقِ بَيْنَهُمَا عَلَى ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَةِ أَنَّ الْوِصَايَةَ خِلَافَةٌ لَا نِيَابَةٌ لِأَنَّهَا مُضَافَةٌ إِلَى زَمَان بُطْلَان النِّيَابَةِ، وَالْخِلَافَةُ لَا تَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الْعِلْمِ فِي التَّصَرُّفِ. (العناية: كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج۷ ص ۳۵۴)
الهداية كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج ۳ ص ۱۵۸
بھی منصب قضاء یا عہدہ امارت کو قبول نہیں کرے گا اور لوگوں کے حقوق ضائع ہو جائیں گے، اس لیے شریعت نے حقوق العباد کی حفاظت وصیانت کے پیش نظر امام، قاضی اور اس کے امین کو ضمان سے بری کر دیا ہے۔
Translation: Liability does not attach to the Imam, the Qadi, and his Amin.
Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that if any item is destroyed in the hands of the Imam of the time, the Qadi, or the one whom the Qadi appoints as Amin, they will not be held liable, because if they were made liable, then no one would be ready to accept these positions, resulting in the loss of the rights of the Muslims.
The author of Al-Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, adds that if a person died and people had loans upon him, and the deceased had left, for example, a slave, then for the repayment of the creditors' loans, the Qadi or the Qadi's Amin sold that slave and received the price from the buyer, but then that price was lost and the slave turned out to be owned by someone else, then the seller (whether the Qadi or his Amin) is not liable for the price, because the Qadi's Amin stands in place of the Qadi, and the Qadi stands in place of the Leader of the Muslims, and none of them is liable, because if we impose liability on any of them, then no one... (Al-Mabsut, etc.)
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage introduces a key Hanafi legal principle: the Imam, the Qadi (judge), and his appointed Amin (trustee) are exempt from financial liability (daman) for property that is lost or destroyed while in their custody. The reasoning is purely practical and based on public interest (maslaha): if these officials were held personally liable, no one would accept such positions, and the rights of the Muslim community would be neglected. The author then illustrates the principle with a concrete example: a deceased person has debts, and the Qadi sells the deceased's slave to pay creditors. The proceeds are received by the Qadi but then lost. Subsequently, the slave is found to be owned by someone else, so the sale is invalid. However, the Qadi (or his Amin) is not liable for the lost price to the buyer. This is because the Amin acts as a deputy for the Qadi, who in turn is a deputy for the Imam (the supreme leader). Just as the Imam is immune from liability to ensure public welfare, so too are the Qadi and his Amin. The passage also includes a comparative discussion from Al-Mabsut distinguishing between the validity of a sale by an executor (wasi) who is unaware of his appointment versus an agent (wakil) unaware of his agency, showing that the executor's sale is valid because executorship is a khilafah (succession) not mere niyabah (agency), and succession does not require knowledge.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Financial liability or responsibility to compensate for loss or damage
امام (imam): The supreme leader of the Muslim community (Caliph) who oversees public affairs
قاضی (qadi): Judge appointed to adjudicate disputes and administer justice
امین (amin): A trusted deputy appointed by the Qadi to assist in judicial or administrative tasks

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are the Imam, Qadi, and his Amin not liable for property lost in their custody according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because they are infallible
B) To encourage people to accept public office without fear of personal loss
C) Because they are considered owners of the property
D) Because the property is not their responsibility

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts top public officials from liability to preserve the public interest, based on istihsan.

Qada — Judiciary
beginner
6 min
Qadi is Not Required to Swear an Oath
States a fiqh ruling with rational proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a Qadi is exempt from swearing an oath when denying a claim, and explain the rational proof based on the analogy to the Imam and the principle of public welfare.

Core Idea
A Qadi's word is accepted without oath — requiring it would deter qualified judges and harm public welfare.

In Hanafi fiqh, the Qadi (judge) occupies a special position. When a claim is made against him and he denies it, he is not required to swear an oath (yamin) to support his denial. His statement is taken as credible by virtue of his office. The ruling is based on a rational proof (dalil aqli): the Qadi is analogous to the Imam (Caliph), who is never liable for losses incurred in the course of duty. If oaths were required, qualified individuals would avoid the judiciary, harming the public interest. This lesson examines the principle, its proof, and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine the exact legal ruling: the Qadi is not required to swear an oath when he is the defendant in a case and denies the claim. His word is accepted without an oath.
This is a procedural exemption, not a substantive right.
2
State the rationale (hikmah)
The ruling is based on public welfare (maslahah). If oaths were required, it would discourage qualified and upright individuals from accepting the position of Qadi, for fear of being subjected to oaths and potential liability.
The same rationale applies to the Qadi's immunity from liability in certain transactions.
3
Trace the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
The Qadi is compared to the Imam (Caliph). Just as the Imam is not required to swear an oath and is not liable for public property losses, the Qadi holds the same status for his jurisdiction. The Amin (deputy) stands in the place of the Qadi.
This qiyas is explicitly mentioned in the source texts (Al-Binaya, Tabyin al-Haqa'iq).
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require the Qadi to swear an oath in some cases. The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi (operative view) and is supported by the need to protect the judiciary's prestige.
The difference stems from different weights given to general principles vs. public welfare.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا يَمِينَ عَلَى الْقَاضِي.
ترجمہ: قاضی پر قسم نہیں ہوتی۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر قاضی کے ساتھ کسی کا اختلاف ہو جائے اور قاضی منکر ہو تب بھی قاضی پر یمین نہیں ہوگی، اور قاضی کی بات معتبر ہوگی، کیونکہ ظاہر یہ وَلَوْ بَاعَ الْقَاضِي أَوْ أَمِينُهُ عَبْدًا لِلْغُرَمَاءِ وَأَخَذَ الْمَالَ فَضَاعَ وَاسْتَحَقَّ الْعَبْدَ لَمْ يَضْمَنْ أَى ضَاعَ ثَمَنُ الْعَبْدِ فِي يَدِ الْبَائِعِ وَهُوَ الْقَاضِي أَوْ أَمِينُهُ لَمْ يَضْمَنْ الْقَاضِي وَلَا أَمِينُهُ الثَّمَنَ لِلْمُشْتَرِي، لِأَنَّ أَمِينَ الْقَاضِي قَائِمٌ مَقَامَ الْقَاضِي وَالْقَاضِي قَائِمٌ مَقَامَ الْخَلِيفَةِ وَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمْ لَا يَلْزَمُهُ الضَّمَانُ، لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَزِمَهُ الضَّمَانُ لَتَقَاعَدُوا عَنْ قَبُولِ هَذِهِ الْأَمَانَةِ كَيْ لَا يَلْزَمَهُمُ الضَّمَانُ وَتَعَطَّلَتْ مَصَالِحُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ. (تبيين الحقائق كتاب القضاء، باب مسائل شتى، ج ٤ ص ٢٠٤) (قال) أي محمد رَحِمَهُ اللهُ في الجامع الصغير (وإذا باع القاضي أو أمينه عبدا للغرماء وأخذ المال أي الثمن (فضاع) أي الثمن و استحق العبد لم يضمن أي القاضي أو أمينه لأن أمين القاضي قائم مقام القاضي، والقاضي قائم مقام الإمام) والإمام لا يضمن، فكذا القاضي أو أمينه وهو معنى قوله (وكل واحد منهم أي من الإمام والقاضي وأمينه (لا يلحقه ضمانا كيلا يتقاعد الناس) أي كل واحد منهم عن قبول هذه الأمة خوفا من الضمان فتضيع الحقوق) أي حقوق المسلمين. (البناية: كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج ٩ ص ٩٣، ٩٤)
الهداية كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج ٣ ص ١٥٩
No oath upon the Qadi.
Translation: No oath is required from the Qadi.
Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that if someone has a dispute with the Qadi and the Qadi denies it, then even then no oath will be required from the Qadi, and the Qadi's statement will be considered credible, because it is apparent...
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage introduces the principle that a Qadi (judge) is not required to take an oath (yamin) when he is a defendant in a case and denies the claim against him. The credibility of the Qadi's statement is accepted without swearing. The rationale is the same as for the immunity from liability: to protect the dignity of the office and to avoid deterring qualified individuals from accepting the position. The text then provides a supporting example already seen in the previous lesson: if a Qadi or his Amin sells a slave and the price is lost, they are not liable. This example is quoted from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq and Al-Binaya, both authoritative Hanafi commentaries. The discussion in the passage also references the ruling from Al-Jami' al-Saghir by Imam Muhammad that the Qadi and his Amin are not liable, and ties it to the larger principle that the Imam (Caliph) is not liable. The principle is thus well-established in the Hanafi school as part of the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
یمین (yamin): An oath sworn by a party in a dispute to affirm the truth of a claim or denial
قاضی (qadi): Judge who hears and decides cases
امین (amin): Deputy of the Qadi who assists in his duties

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Qadi must swear an oath
B) No oath is required from the Qadi; his word is accepted
C) The Qadi must provide two witnesses
D) The case is dismissed automatically

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts the Qadi from swearing an oath when denying a claim, relying on the analogy to the Imam and the need to protect the judiciary's prestige.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
No Oath Upon the Qadi
Explaining the principle that no oath is imposed on the qadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a qadi is not required to take an oath when his past judgment is challenged, and explain the istihsan-based reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
The qadi’s word is accepted without oath because he is an amin, not a litigant.

Imagine a person claims a former qadi wrongfully took his money during his tenure. The qadi denies it. Should the qadi be forced to swear an oath? The Hanafi school says no. This lesson explores the principle that a qadi is exempt from oaths in such disputes, based on the qadi’s status as a trustee (amin) and the public interest in preserving the judiciary. We will examine the ruling, its proof from istihsan, and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the core ruling: The qadi is not required to take an oath when a dispute arises regarding acts performed during his tenure. His statement is accepted as credible.
The ruling is procedural, not classifying an act as obligatory or prohibited.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is istihsan (juristic preference). The text argues that if the qadi were liable or required to swear an oath, people would avoid the office, leading to neglect of Muslims' rights. This overrides any general qiyas that might otherwise require an oath.
Istihsan is a prominent Hanafi tool for departing from strict analogy to achieve a more equitable result.
3
Analyze the reasoning chain
The qadi is an amin (trustee) standing in place of the caliph/imam. An amin is not liable for losses without negligence, and an oath is not required from an amin because he is not a litigant. The apparent (zahir) circumstances support the qadi’s justice.
The qadi’s agent also shares this immunity, as he stands in the qadi’s place.
4
Apply to a practical scenario
Consider a former qadi accused of unjustly taking money. The new qadi asks the claimant for evidence (bayyinah). If no evidence is produced, the qadi’s statement is accepted without oath. This preserves the finality of judgments and the integrity of the judiciary.
If evidence of injustice is produced, the claimant’s right is restored; the qadi is not protected in cases of proven wrongdoing.
5
Contrast with other positions
Some other schools might require the qadi to swear an oath if he is a defendant, treating him like any other litigant. The Hanafi school rejects this due to the qadi’s special status and the public interest.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi view prioritizes the smooth functioning of the judiciary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر قاضی کے ساتھ کسی کا اختلاف ہو جائے اور قاضی منکر ہوتب بھی قاضی پر یمین نہیں ہوگی ، اور قاضی کی بات معتبر ہوگی، کیونکہ ظاہر یہ وَلَوْ بَاعَ الْقَاضِي أَوْ أَمِينُهُ عَبْدًا لِلْغُرَمَاءِ وَأَخَذَ الْمَالَ فَضَاعَ وَاسْتَحَقَّ الْعَبْدَ لَمُ يَضْمَنُ أَى ضَاعَ ثَمَنُ الْعَبْدِ فِي يَدِ الْبَائِعِ وَهُوَ الْقَاضِي أَوْ أَمِينُهُ لَمْ يَضْمَنُ الْقَاضِي وَلَا أَمِينُهُ الثَّمَنَ لِلْمُشْتَرِى، لأنَّ أمِينَ الْقَاضِي قَائِمٌ مَقَامَ الْقَاضِي وَالْقَاضِي قَائِمٌ مَقَامَ الْخَلِيفَةِ وَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمْ لَا يَلْزَمُهُ الضَّمَانُ، لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَزِمَهُ الضَّمَانُ لَتَقَاعَدُوا عَنْ قَبُولِ هَذِهِ الْأَمَانَةِ كَيْ لَا يَلْزَمَهُمُ الضَّمَانُ وَتَعَطَّلَتْ مَصَالِحُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ. (تبيين الحقائق كتاب القضاء، باب مسائل شتی، ج ۴ ص ۲۰۴) (قال) أى محمد رَحِمَهُ اللهُ فى الجامع الصغير (وإذا باع القاضي أو أمينه عبدا للغرماء وأخذ المال أى الثمن (فضاع) أى الثمن و استحق العبد لم يضمن أى القاضي أو أمينه لأن أمين القاضي قائم مقام القاضى، والقاضي قائم مقام الإمام) والإمام لايضمن، فكذا القاضى أو أمينه وهو معنى قوله (وكل واحد منهم أى من الإمام والقاضي وأمينه (لا يلحقه ضمانا كيلا يتقاعد الناس) أى كل واحد منهم عن قبول هذه الأمة خوفا من الضمان فتضيع الحقوق) أى حقوق المسلمين. ہے کہ منصب قضاء پر ہوتے ہوئے قاضی کسی کے بارے میں ناحق فیصلہ نہیں کرتا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر قاضی کے معزول ہونے کے بعد ایک شخص قاضی کے پاس آیا اور کہنے لگا کہ آپ نے زمانہ قضاء میں میرے خلاف ناحق فیصلہ کیا تھا، قاضی نے کہا میں نے حق کے مطابق فیصلہ کیا تھا، اس شخص نے کہا کہ آپ نے ناحق فیصلہ کر کے مجھ سے ظلما اتنی رقم لے کر فلاں کو دی ،لہذا مجھے وہ رقم واپس کرو ۔ اسی طرح ایک مقطوع الیہ شخص نے قاضی سے کہا کہ تم نے ناحق میرا ہاتھ کٹوایا ہے، قاضی نے اُس شخص سے کہا: میں نے ایک صحیح معاملے میں تمہارے ہاتھ کاٹنے کا فیصلہ کیا تھا، اور اس شخص نے کہا تم نے ظلما میرا ہاتھ کٹو ایا ہے، اس لیے مجھے تاوان دو۔ اب جب یہ مسئلے نئے قاضی کی عدالت میں جائیں گے تو وہ ماخوذ منہ المال اور مقطوع الید سے بینہ طلب کرے گا، چنانچہ اگر وہ قاضی کے فیصلے کو ظالمانہ ثابت کر دیں گے تو انہیں ان کا حق دلوایا جائے گا، لیکن اگر وہ بینہ نہ پیش کر سکیں تو اس صورت میں قاضی کا قول معتبر ہوگا، یہ اس وقت جب یہ دونوں اقرار کریں کہ قاضی نے یہ فیصلہ زمانہ قضاء میں کیا ہے، تو اب ظاہر حل قاضی کے حق میں شاہد ہوگا، کیونکہ قاضی بظاہر ظلم پر مبنی فیصلہ نہیں کرتا ، اب قاضی پر قسم نہیں آئے گی کیونکہ قاضی امین ہوتا ہے خصم نہیں ہوتا ہے، اور امین پر تم نہیں آتی ہے، اگر اس طرح خصم بنا کر قاضی پر تم کو لازم کر دیا جائے تو آئندہ لوگ قضاء کے عہدے سے انکار کریں گے اور اس میں لوگوں کے حقوق کا ضیاع ہے۔ نیز اگر قاضی کو خصم بنائیں گے تو اس کا فیصلہ نافذ نہیں ہوگا۔ فَعَلَى هَذَا إِذَا قَالَ لِرَجُلٍ أَخَذْتُ مِنْكَ أَلْفًا وَدَفَعْتِهَا إِلَى فُلَانِ قَضَيْتَ بِهَا عَلَيْكَ وَقَالَ لِآخَرَ قَضَيْت بِقَطْعِ يَدِى فِي حَقِّ فَقَالَ الْمَأْخُوذُ مِنْهُ الْمَالُ وَالْمَقْطُوعُ يَدُهُ فَعَلْت ذَلِكَ فِي حَالِ قَضَائِكَ ظُلْمًا فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْقَاضِي لِأَنَّهُمَا لَمَّا تَوَافَقَا أَنَّهُ فَعَلَ ذَلِكَ فِي قَضَائِهِ كَانَ الظَّاهِرُ شَاهِدًا لَهُ إِذِ الْقَاضِي لَا يَقْضِى بِالْجَوْرِ ظَاهِرًا وَالْقَوْلُ لِمَنْ يَشْهَدُ لَهُ الظَّاهِرُ، لِأَنَّهُ ثَبَتَ فِعْلُهُ فِي قَضَائِهِ
(20) No oath upon the qadi. Translation: The qadi is not required to take an oath. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that if someone has a dispute with the qadi and the qadi denies it, still no oath is required from the qadi, and the qadi's statement is considered credible, because the apparent [evidence] supports him. And if the qadi or his agent sold a slave belonging to creditors, took the money, and it was lost, and the slave was claimed [by someone else], they would not be liable, i.e., the price of the slave was lost in the hand of the seller who is the qadi or his agent; neither the qadi nor his agent is liable for the price to the buyer, because the qadi's agent stands in the place of the qadi, and the qadi stands in the place of the caliph, and each one of them is not liable for damages; because if they were liable for damages, they would refrain from accepting this trust so that they would not incur liability, and the interests of the Muslims would be disrupted. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Qada, Bab Masa'il Shatta, vol. 4, p. 204) (He said) i.e., Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) in al-Jami' al-Saghir: And if the qadi or his agent sold a slave belonging to creditors, took the money (i.e., the price), and it was lost (i.e., the price), and the slave was claimed, he would not be liable, i.e., the qadi or his agent. Because the qadi's agent stands in the place of the qadi, and the qadi stands in the place of the Imam, and the Imam is not liable. Likewise the qadi or his agent. And this is the meaning of his statement: Each one of them (i.e., the Imam, the qadi, and his agent) is not subject to liability, so that people do not refrain from accepting this office for fear of liability, and thus rights (i.e., the rights of Muslims) are lost. (al-Binaya, Kitab Adab al-Qadi, Bab al-Tahkim, vol. 9, pp. 93-94) Al-Hidayah, Kitab Adab al-Qadi, Bab al-Tahkim, vol. 3, p. 159. [Urdu continuation:] It is that while holding the office of qadi, the qadi does not make an unjust decision against anyone. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning the aforementioned principle, explains that if after the qadi's removal from office, a person comes to the qadi and says, 'You made an unjust decision against me during your tenure,' the qadi says, 'I decided according to the truth,' and the person says, 'You unjustly took such an amount from me and gave it to so-and-so, so return that money to me.' Similarly, a person whose hand was cut off says to the qadi, 'You had my hand cut off unjustly,' and the qadi says, 'I decided to cut off your hand in a valid case,' and the person says, 'You got my hand cut off oppressively, so give me compensation.' Now when these cases go to a new qadi's court, he will require evidence (bayyinah) from the one from whom money was taken and the one whose hand was cut. If they prove the qadi's decision was unjust, their right will be restored. But if they cannot produce evidence, then in that case the qadi's statement will be accepted, provided both admit that the qadi made that decision during his tenure. Now the apparent [situation] will bear witness in favor of the qadi, because the qadi apparently does not give a decision based on injustice. Now an oath will not be required from the qadi, because the qadi is a trustee (amin), not a litigant (khasm), and an oath is not required from a trustee. If by making him a litigant, an oath is imposed on the qadi, then in the future people will refuse to accept the office of qadi, and this would lead to the loss of people's rights. Also, if we make the qadi a litigant, his decisions will not be enforced. Therefore, if he says to a man, 'I took one thousand from you and gave it to so-and-so as a judgment against you,' and says to another, 'I ordered the amputation of my hand in a rightful case,' and the one from whom the money was taken and the one whose hand was cut say, 'You did that during your tenure as qadi oppressively,' then the statement of the qadi is accepted, because when they agree that he did that during his tenure as qadi, the apparent [evidence] bears witness for him, since the qadi does not apparently decide unjustly. The statement is accepted from the one whom the apparent [evidence] supports, because his action during his tenure as qadi is established.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage addresses the principle that a qadi is not required to take an oath (yamin) when a dispute arises regarding his past judgments. The author begins by stating the principle: 'No oath upon the qadi.' The Urdu commentary explains that if someone disputes with a qadi and the qadi denies the allegation, the qadi does not have to swear an oath; his word is taken as credible because the apparent circumstances (zahir) support him. The qadi is presumed to act justly. The passage then cites a related ruling from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: if a qadi or his agent sells a slave of the creditors, takes the price, and the price is lost while the slave is later found to belong to someone else, neither the qadi nor his agent is liable for the price to the buyer. The reason is that the agent stands in place of the qadi, the qadi stands in place of the caliph (Imam), and the Imam is not liable for such losses. If liability were imposed, people would avoid accepting these positions of trust, leading to neglect of Muslims' interests. This is a clear example of istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent harm (daran) and preserve public benefit (maslahah). The author then presents a practical scenario: after a qadi is removed from office, a person claims the qadi unjustly took money or ordered an amputation during his tenure. The new qadi will require evidence (bayyinah) from the claimant. If they prove injustice, the right is restored. If they cannot, the qadi's statement is accepted because both parties agree the act occurred during his tenure, and the qadi is presumed not to have acted unjustly. No oath is required from the qadi because he is an amin (trustee), not a khasm (litigant). Imposing an oath would deter people from becoming qadis, harming public welfare. The passage concludes with a direct quote: 'The statement is of the qadi because the apparent (zahir) bears witness for him.' This reinforces the rule that the qadi is not subjected to an oath in such disputes.

Key Terms
الْقَاضِي (al-qadi): The judge appointed by the Muslim ruler to adjudicate disputes and enforce Islamic law.
الْيَمِين (al-yamin): The oath sworn by a party in a legal proceeding to establish a claim or defense.
الضَّمَان (al-daman): Legal liability or responsibility to compensate for loss or damage.
الْأَمِين (al-amin): A trustee; one who is entrusted with property or authority without liability for loss not caused by negligence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a qadi's past judgment is challenged after his removal, and the claimant cannot produce evidence, what is the Hanafi ruling regarding the qadi's statement?
A) The qadi must take an oath to affirm his statement.
B) The qadi's statement is accepted without oath because he is an amin and the apparent circumstances support him.
C) The qadi must pay damages if the claimant insists.
D) The matter is dismissed entirely without consideration.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts the qadi from oath in disputes over his past judgments, based on his status as amin and the public interest in preserving the judiciary.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Ruling on a Former Qadi's Word When Accused of Injustice
Explaining the ruling when a former qadi is accused of unjust judgement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a former qadi is accused of an unjust judgment, explain why his word is taken as authoritative, and identify why no oath is required.

Core Idea
The qadi's word stands because he is a trustee (amin), not a litigant (khasm).

In this passage from the Hidayah, we explore a practical scenario: after a qadi's removal, a claimant accuses him of an unjust judgment. The new qadi must decide whose word to accept. The resolution hinges on two key distinctions: the presumption that the qadi does not judge unjustly (zahir), and the qadi's status as an amin rather than a khasm. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Islamic procedural law protects the integrity of the judiciary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Two claimants come forward after the qadi's removal: one claims the qadi wrongfully took money from him and gave it to another; the other claims the qadi wrongfully ordered his hand to be cut. Both admit the qadi performed these acts during his tenure.
The admission that the acts occurred 'fi qada'ihi' (during his judging) is crucial.
2
Determine the procedural starting point
The new qadi first demands evidence (bayyinah) from the claimants to prove the injustice. If they succeed, their rights will be restored. If not, the ruling shifts to the former qadi's favor.
Bayyinah is the standard proof in qada.
3
Apply the default rule when evidence is lacking
When claimants cannot produce proof, the former qadi's word (qawl) is taken as authoritative. The text states: 'al-qawl qawl al-qadi' (the statement is the statement of the qadi).
This applies only when both parties agree the act occurred during the qadi's term.
4
Analyze the legal basis: the presumption of justice
The principle 'al-qadi la yaqdi bil-jawr zahiran' (the qadi does not outwardly judge unjustly) creates a presumption (zahir) in favor of the qadi. Since the claimants admit the act occurred while he was qadi, the apparent condition (zahir al-hal) witnesses for him.
This is an istihsan based on public policy (sadd al-dhari'ah).
5
Understand the exemption from oath
The qadi is not required to take an oath because he is an amin (trustee), not a khasm (litigant). Oaths are obligatory only on litigants. If the qadi were treated as a khasm, his judgment would not be enforceable and people would avoid the office of qada.
The distinction between amin and khasm is fundamental in Hanafi procedural law.

Source Text

اصول بدایہ ثالث

ہے کہ منصب قضاء پر ہوتے ہوئے قاضی کسی کے بارے میں ناحق فیصلہ نہیں کرتا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر قاضی کے معزول ہونے کے بعد ایک شخص قاضی کے پاس آیا اور کہنے لگا کہ آپ نے زمانہ قضاء میں میرے خلاف ناحق فیصلہ کیا تھا، قاضی نے کہا میں نے حق کے مطابق فیصلہ کیا تھا، اس شخص نے کہا کہ آپ نے ناحق فیصلہ کر کے مجھ سے ظلما اتنی رقم لے کر فلاں کو دی ،لہذا مجھے وہ رقم واپس کرو ۔ اسی طرح ایک مقطوع الیہ شخص نے قاضی سے کہا کہ تم نے ناحق میرا ہاتھ کٹوایا ہے، قاضی نے اُس شخص سے کہا: میں نے ایک صحیح معاملے میں تمہارے ہاتھ کاٹنے کا فیصلہ کیا تھا، اور اس شخص نے کہا تم نے ظلما میرا ہاتھ کٹو ایا ہے، اس لیے مجھے تاوان دو۔ اب جب یہ مسئلے نئے قاضی کی عدالت میں جائیں گے تو وہ ماخوذ منہ المال اور مقطوع الید سے بینہ طلب کرے گا، چنانچہ اگر وہ قاضی کے فیصلے کو ظالمانہ ثابت کر دیں گے تو انہیں ان کا حق دلوایا جائے گا، لیکن اگر وہ بینہ نہ پیش کر سکیں تو اس صورت میں قاضی کا قول معتبر ہوگا، یہ اس وقت جب یہ دونوں اقرار کریں کہ قاضی نے یہ فیصلہ زمانہ قضاء میں کیا ہے، تو اب ظاہر حل قاضی کے حق میں شاہد ہوگا، کیونکہ قاضی بظاہر ظلم پر مبنی فیصلہ نہیں کرتا ، اب قاضی پر قسم نہیں آئے گی کیونکہ قاضی امین ہوتا ہے خصم نہیں ہوتا ہے، اور امین پر تم نہیں آتی ہے، اگر اس طرح خصم بنا کر قاضی پر تم کو لازم کر دیا جائے تو آئندہ لوگ قضاء کے عہدے سے انکار کریں گے اور اس میں لوگوں کے حقوق کا ضیاع ہے۔ نیز اگر قاضی کو خصم بنائیں گے تو اس کا فیصلہ نافذ نہیں ہوگا۔ فَعَلَى هَذَا إِذَا قَالَ لِرَجُلٍ أَخَذْتُ مِنْكَ أَلْفًا وَدَفَعْتِهَا إِلَى فُلَانِ قَضَيْتَ بِهَا عَلَيْكَ وَقَالَ لِآخَرَ قَضَيْت بِقَطْعِ يَدِى فِي حَقِّ [؟] فَقَالَ الْمَأْخُوذُ مِنْهُ الْمَالُ وَالْمَقْطُوعُ يَدُهُ فَعَلْت ذَلِكَ فِي حَالِ قَضَائِكَ ظُلْمًا فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْقَاضِي لِأَنَّهُمَا لَمَّا تَوَافَقَا أَنَّهُ فَعَلَ ذَلِكَ فِي قَضَائِهِ كَانَ الظَّاهِرُ شَاهِدًا لَهُ إِذِ الْقَاضِي لَا يَقْضِى بِالْجَوْرِ ظَاهِرًا وَالْقَوْلُ لِمَنْ يَشْهَدُ لَهُ الظَّاهِرُ، لِأَنَّهُ ثَبَتَ فِعْلُهُ فِي قَضَائِهِ
فَلا يَمِينَ عَلَى الْقَاضِي لِأَنَّ الْيَمِينَ تَجِبُ عَلَى الْخَصْمِ وَالْقَاضِي لَيْسَ بِخَصْمٍ وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ أَمِينٌ، وَلَوْ صَارَ خَصْمًا لَمَا نَفَذَ قَضَاؤُهُ.
(تبيين الحقائق: كتاب القضاء، باب مسائل شتی، ج۴ ص ۲۰۵)
Principles of Hidayah, third. That the qadi, while holding the position of judgment, does not pass an unjust verdict against anyone. The author of Hidayah, mentioning the aforementioned principle, explains that if after the qadi is removed, a person comes to him and says, 'You passed an unjust verdict against me during your tenure;' the qadi said, 'I judged according to right.' That person said, 'By passing an unjust verdict, you took such amount from me oppressively and gave it to so-and-so; therefore, return that money to me.' Similarly, a person whose hand was cut off said to the qadi, 'You had my hand cut off unjustly.' The qadi told that person, 'I gave a ruling in a correct matter to cut off your hand.' And that person said, 'You had my hand cut off oppressively, so pay me compensation.' Now when these matters go to the court of a new qadi, he will demand evidence from the one from whom the money was taken and the one whose hand was cut off. So if they prove the qadi's verdict to be oppressive, they will be given their right; but if they cannot produce evidence, then in that case the qadi's statement is authoritative. This is when both admit that the qadi issued that verdict during his tenure; then the apparent condition will be a witness for the qadi, because the qadi, outwardly, does not pass an unjust verdict. Now the qadi will not be subject to an oath, because the qadi is a trustee, not a litigant; and an oath is not administered to a trustee. If by making him a litigant the oath is made necessary for the qadi, then in the future people would refuse the office of qada, and this would lead to the loss of people's rights. Also, if the qadi is made a litigant, his judgment would not be enforceable. So upon this, if he says to a man, 'I took from you one thousand and paid it to so-and-so, you passed a verdict for it against you;' and to another he says, 'You gave a ruling to cut off my hand for a right [?];' then the one from whom the money was taken and the one whose hand was cut off say, 'You did that oppressively during your judging.' Then the statement of the qadi is authoritative because when they both agree that he did that during his judging, the apparent condition is a witness for him, since the qadi does not outwardly judge unjustly. And the statement is for one for whom the apparent condition is a witness, because his act is established during his judging. So no oath is upon the qadi because the oath is obligatory upon the litigant, and the qadi is not a litigant; rather, he is a trustee. And if he became a litigant, his judgment would not be enforceable. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: Kitab al-Qada, Bab Masail Shatti, vol. 4, p. 205)
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a ruling from al-Hidayah regarding the credibility of a former qadi's word when he is accused of an unjust judgment after his term. The commentary explains a specific scenario: after a qadi is removed from office, two persons come forward with claims. One claims that the qadi wrongfully took money from him and gave it to another. The other claims that the qadi wrongfully ordered his hand to be cut off. Both admit that the qadi made these rulings during his tenure. The new qadi handling the case will ask the claimants to bring evidence (bayyinah) proving the injustice. If they fail to do so, the former qadi's word is taken as authoritative. The reasoning is that the apparent condition (zahir al-hal) supports the qadi, because a qadi does not (outwardly) pass unjust judgments. The passage then addresses the question of whether the qadi must take an oath. It rules that no oath is required, because the qadi is an amin (trustee), not a khasm (litigant). If an oath were imposed, people would avoid accepting the role of qadi, and rights would be lost. The passage concludes with quotes from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq reinforcing that the qadi's statement is accepted and no oath is upon him.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Judgment, the office of a judge; in this passage, refers to the period during which the qadi held the position.
ظاهر (zahir): The apparent condition or outward state; in this context, the presumption that the qadi's actions were just based on his public role.
یمین (yamin): Oath; in this passage, the oath that would be required of a litigant, but the qadi is exempted because he is not a litigant.
امین (amin): Trustee; here, the qadi is considered a trustee (amin) over the rights of people, not a litigant.
خصم (khasm): Litigant or adversary; the qadi is not a khasm and thus does not bear the burden of oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the qadi not required to take an oath when accused of an unjust judgment?
A) Because the qadi is a litigant in this case.
B) Because the qadi is an amin (trustee), not a khasm (litigant), and an oath is only required for litigants.
C) Because the claimant has already taken an oath.
D) Because the qadi's judgment is final and cannot be challenged.

Key Takeaway
When a former qadi is accused of injustice after his tenure, his word is taken as authoritative and he is not required to take an oath because he is a trustee (amin), not a litigant (khasm).
