The Principle of Qada Being Based on Hujjah and the Dispute on Tazkiyah
Introducing the principle and explaining the intra-Hanafi difference on tazkiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn on tazkiyah (witness verification) and explain the contextual basis for their difference.

Core Idea
Judgment is based on proof; witness verification may be presumed from outward uprightness in a righteous era.

This lesson explores a foundational principle of Islamic judiciary: 'Adjudication is based on proof' (القضاء مبني على الحجة). The author of al-Hidayah uses this principle to frame an important intra-Hanafi difference on whether a judge must always investigate witnesses' character (tazkiyah) or may rely on their apparent uprightness. You will learn both positions and how the era's moral climate influences the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Principle
Identify the core maxim: al-qada mabnahu 'ala al-hujjah (judgment is based on proof). Understand that hujjah here primarily refers to the testimony of upright witnesses.
This principle is axiomatic in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Identify the Dispute
Recognize the ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn on the necessity of tazkiyah (investigation of witnesses' 'adalah) in all cases.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, not cross-madhhab.
3
Analyze Each Position
Examine the Sahibayn's view that tazkiyah is always required because hujjah requires certain knowledge of 'adalah, which only comes through investigation. Then examine Imam Abu Hanifah's view that outward appearance (zahir al-hal) suffices in an upright era.
Note the differing interpretations of what constitutes hujjah.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Conclude that the practical difference depends on the era: in a time of prevalent righteousness, a qadi may rely on appearance; otherwise, verification is necessary.
The text does not give a clear tarjih, but later practice often requires tazkiyah.

(العناية: كتاب أدب القاضي، باب التحكيم، ج- ص ٣٦١)
الهداية كتاب الشهادة، ج ۳ ص ۱۶۳
Principles of Hidayah, third. Book of Testimony. (61) Adjudication is based on proof. Translation: The foundation of qada is on proof. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that the qadi can give a decision only when there is proof, i.e., the basis of the decision is proof, and by proof is meant upright witnesses when they testify. The author of Hidayah, mentioning the aforementioned principle, explains that according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), in all rights and all transactions and cases, just as in hudud and qisas, it is necessary that the qadi conduct both secret and open investigation of the witnesses (tazkiyah), whether the defendant impugns the witnesses or not, because the foundation of qada is on proof, and proof is the name of the testimony of upright witnesses, and the uprightness of witnesses is known only when the qadi investigates them; therefore, in any case, inquiry about witnesses will be made. According to Imam Abu Hanifah, his opinion is that the apparent condition of witnesses will be relied upon. This difference is due to the era and time; the Imam's time was the best of centuries, where truthfulness and righteousness predominated in people. This [؟] and there is no oath on the qadi because imposing it upon him leads to the suspension of people's affairs by preventing entry into qada. (Al-'Inayah: Kitab Adab al-Qadi, Bab al-Tahkim, vol. ? p. 361) Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Shahadat, vol. 3 p. 163
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage introduces a fundamental principle of Islamic judiciary: 'Adjudication is based on proof' (القضاء مبني على الحجة). The commentary explains that this principle means a qadi can only decide when there is a valid hujjah (proof), which primarily consists of testimony from upright witnesses ('udul). The author then uses this principle to explain an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the necessity of tazkiyah (verification of witnesses' uprightness). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the qadi must investigate the witnesses' character both secretly and openly in all cases, regardless of whether the defendant challenges them, because hujjah requires 'adalah (uprightness), and 'adalah is not known without tazkiyah. Imam Abu Hanifah, on the other hand, holds that the qadi may rely on the witnesses' apparent condition (zahir al-hal) without formal investigation. The commentary notes that this difference stems from the era: Imam Abu Hanifah lived in the best generation (khayr al-qurun) where people were generally truthful and righteous, so outward appearance sufficed. The passage ends with a partial repetition of the ruling about no oath on the qadi, sourced from al-'Inayah, and references al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
حجت (hujjah): Proof or evidence; in this context, specifically the testimony of upright witnesses that forms the basis of judicial decisions.
تزکیہ (tazkiyah): Investigation or verification of a witness's uprightness ('adalah); both secret and open inquiry by the qadi.
شہادت (shahadah): Testimony given by witnesses; the primary means of proof in cases.
عادل ('adil): Upright, just; a witness must be 'adil for his testimony to be accepted as hujjah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Imam Abu Hanifah hold that tazkiyah (witness verification) is not always necessary, while the Sahibayn required it in all cases?
A) Imam Abu Hanifah believed that the qadi's knowledge is sufficient without investigation.
B) Imam Abu Hanifah considered that in his time, people were generally upright, so outward appearance sufficed.
C) The Sahibayn followed a different school of thought.
D) Imam Abu Hanifah did not accept the principle 'al-qada mabnahu 'ala al-hujjah'.

Key Takeaway
The requirement for witness verification (tazkiyah) in Islamic judicial procedure is contextual: in a righteous era, outward appearance may suffice; otherwise, formal investigation is necessary—the Sahibayn's cautious view is followed in most contemporary practice.

The Basis of Judgment: Investigating Witnesses
Intra-Hanafi dispute on witness investigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that judgment is based on proof, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on witness investigation, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to contemporary judicial practice.

Core Idea
Judgment rests on proof; ensuring witnesses are just is essential, and the duty to investigate changes with the moral state of society.

This passage establishes the foundational principle that judicial decisions rest on proof (hujja), specifically the testimony of just witnesses. However, the Hanafi school debated whether the judge must proactively investigate the character of witnesses or may rely on their apparent justice. We will examine the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, the reasoning behind each view, and how the later school resolved the conflict in light of changing times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The passage opens with 'Judgment is based on proof.' Recognize that this is the foundational axiom from which all rulings on witness investigation derive.
The proof (hujja) here is specifically the testimony of just witnesses.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa held that the judge may rely on the apparent justice (zahir al-adala) of a Muslim witness and only needs to investigate if the opposing party impugns the witness. In hudud and qisas, investigation is required regardless.
This was based on the presumption of integrity in his era.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) held that the judge must investigate witnesses secretly and openly in all types of cases (all rights). This is to safeguard the judgment from being invalidated if witnesses are later found to be slaves, disbelievers, or corrupt.
They argued that since judgment is built on proof, and proof is testimony of just witnesses, verifying justice is indispensable.
4
Analyze the reasoning behind each view
Examine each side's dalil. Abu Hanifa relied on the general trustworthiness of Muslims in his time. The Sahibayn emphasized the logical necessity of verifying justice to preserve the validity of the judgment, especially given the possibility of hidden defects.
The difference is not in principle but in its application under varying social conditions.
5
Understand the resolution: difference of era
The later Hanafi scholars reconciled the dispute by attributing it to the change of era. Abu Hanifa would have agreed with the Sahibayn had he lived in a time of widespread corruption. Therefore, the fatwa today is on the view of the two companions.
The text explicitly states: 'And it is said: This is a difference of era and time, and the fatwa is on their statement in this time.'

Source Text

قَوْلُهُ وَسَأَلَ عَنْ الشُّهُودِ سِرًّا وَعَلَنَا فِي سَائِرِ الْحُقُوقِ أَى وَسَأَلَ الْقَاضِي عَنْهُمُ فِي السِّرِّ وَالْعَلَانِيَةِ وَهُوَ قَوْلُ أَبِى يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ ، لأَنَّ الْقَضَاءَ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى الْحُجَّةِ وَهِيَ شَهَادَةُ الْعُدُولِ فَيَتَعَرَّفْ عَنْ الْعَدَالَةِ وَفِيهِ صَوْنُ قَضَائِهِ عَنِ الْبُطْلَانِ وَقَالَ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ يَقْتَصِرُ الْحَاكِمُ عَلَى ظَاهِرِ الْعَدَالَةِ فِي الْمُسْلِمِ وَلَا يَسْأَلُ حَتَّى بِطَعْنِ الْخَصْمِ ... وَالْحَاصِلُ أَنَّهُ إِنْ طَعَنَ الْخَصْمُ سَأَلَ عَنْهُمْ فِي الْكُلِّ وَإِلَّا سَأَلَ فِي الْحُدُودِ وَالْقِصَاصِ وَفِي غَيْرِهَا مَحَلُّ الِاخْتِلَافِ وَقِيلَ هَذَا اخْتِلَافُ عَصْرٍ وَزَمَانٍ وَالْفَتْوَى عَلَى قَوْلِهِمَا فِى هَذَا الزَّمَانِ. (البحر الرائق: كتاب الشهادات، ج۷ ص ۶۳) وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ : لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَسْأَلَ عَنْهُمْ فِي السِّرِّ وَالْعَلَانِيَةِ فِي جَمِيعِ الْحُقُوقِ، لِأَنَّ مَبْنَى الْقَضَاءِ عَلَى الْحُجَّةِ وَهِيَ شَهَادَةُ الْعُدُولِ فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ التَّعَرُّفِ عَنْ الْعَدَالَةِ، وَفِي السُّؤَالِ صَوْنُ الْقَضَاءِ عَنْ الْبُطْلَانِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ ظُهُورِ الشُّهُودِ عَبِيدًا أَوْ كُفَّارًا وَقِيلَ هَذَا الاخْتِلافُ اخْتِلَافُ عَصْرٍ وَزَمَانٍ)، لِأَنَّ أَبَا حَنِيفَةَ أَجَابَ فِي زَمَانِهِ وَكَانَ الْغَالِبُ مِنْهُمُ عُدُولًا وَهُمَا أَجَابَا فِي زَمَانِهِمَا وَقَدْ تَغَيَّرَ النَّاسُ وَكَثُرَ الْفَسَادُ، وَلَوْ شَاهَدَ ذَلِكَ أَبُو حَنِيفَةَ لَقَالَ بِقَوْلِهِمَا، وَلِهَذَا قَالَ وَالْفَتْوَى عَلَى قَوْلِهِمَا فِي هَذَا الزَّمَانِ). (العناية كتاب الشهادة، ج۷ ص ۳۷۸، ۳۷۹)
(61) Judgment is based on proof. His statement: 'And he asked about the witnesses secretly and openly in all rights' meaning the judge asks about them in secret and open, and this is the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, because judgment is built upon proof which is the testimony of just witnesses, so he ascertains justice and in it is preservation of his judgment from invalidity. Abu Hanifa said: The judge confines himself to the apparent justice of a Muslim and does not ask unless the opponent impugns... The summary: If the opponent impugns, he asks about them in all cases; otherwise he asks in hudud and qisas, and in other than them it is the place of disagreement. And it is said: This is a difference of era and time, and the fatwa is on their statement in this time. (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: It is necessary to ask about them secretly and openly in all rights, because the basis of judgment is on proof which is the testimony of just witnesses, so ascertaining justice is necessary, and in asking is preservation of judgment from invalidity on the supposition that the witnesses appear to be slaves or disbelievers. And it is said this difference is a difference of era and time, because Abu Hanifa gave his answer in his time when the majority were just, and they gave their answer in their time when people had changed and corruption increased, and had Abu Hanifa witnessed that he would have said according to their statement, and therefore it is said: the fatwa is on their statement in this time.) (al-`Inayah)
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the necessity of investigating witnesses' character. The core principle is that judgment (qada) is founded on proof (hujja), which is the testimony of just witnesses. The dispute arises about whether the judge must secretly and openly inquire into the witnesses' justice in all rights or only when the opponent impugns them. Abu Hanifa held that the judge can rely on the apparent justice of a Muslim and only investigate if the opponent challenges. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) required investigation in all cases to safeguard the judgment from being invalidated if witnesses turn out to be slaves or disbelievers. The text explains that this disagreement is due to the change of era: in Abu Hanifa's time, righteousness was prevalent, but by the time of the companions, corruption had become widespread. It is explicitly stated that if Abu Hanifa had witnessed the later corruption, he would have agreed with the companions. Therefore, the fatwa today is on the view of the two companions. The passage also cites sources: al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and al-`Inayah, indicating it is from later Hanafi works.

Key Terms
الْقَضَاء (al-qada'): Judgment or judicial decision by a qadi.
الْحُجَّة (al-hujja): Proof; in this context, the testimony of just witnesses.
تَزْكِيَة (tazkiya): Investigation into the character and justice of witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position, when must a judge investigate the character of witnesses?
A) Only in hudud and qisas cases
B) Only when the opponent impugns them
C) In all rights, secretly and openly
D) Never, because apparent justice suffices

Key Takeaway
Judgment is based on proof; to ensure it, the judge must investigate witnesses in all rights, a duty that the Hanafi school now considers wajib in light of changing social conditions.

The Imposition of a Claim Regarding an Unknown Thing is Not Valid
Principle on the necessity of specifying the object of a claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that a claim must be known in type and quantity, and apply it to determine the validity of a claim.

Core Idea
A claim that is unknown in type or quantity cannot impose liability.

This lesson introduces a fundamental rule in Hanafi fiqh on the validity of legal claims. The claimant must clearly specify what they are claiming; otherwise, the claim is rejected. This principle is from Al-Hidayah's Kitab al-Da'wa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making a claim (da'wa) against someone. The ruling is that claiming an unknown thing (majhul) is invalid (ghayr sahih) and not accepted in court.
The passage clearly states: 'الإلزام في المجهول لا يتحقق'.
2
Determine the condition
For a claim to be valid, the claimant must specify the type (jins) and quantity (miqdar) of the thing claimed. Without these, the claim is unknown and cannot impose liability.
Examples: 'one thousand rupees' or 'one kilo of flour'.
3
Understand the rationale
An unknown claim is an unjust imposition on the defendant because they cannot defend against a vague accusation. The law protects the defendant from such uncertainty.
The text says: 'because claiming an unknown thing is an imposition on another'.
4
Apply the rule to a sample scenario
Suppose a claimant says: 'You owe me money.' This is invalid. But if they say: 'You owe me 500 dirhams,' it is valid because type (dirhams) and quantity (500) are specified.
Apply to real court cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۳) الْإِلْزَامُ فِى الْمَجُهُولِ لَا يَتَحَقَّقُ . •
ترجمہ: مجہول چیز میں الزام تحقیق نہیں ہوتا۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ مجہول چیز کا دعوی کر کے کسی پر کوئی چیز لازم کرنا درست نہیں ہے، جب تک اس مدعی کے لئے مقدار اور جنس بیان نہ کرے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ مدعی کا دعوی مجہول ہونے کی صورت میں قبول نہیں، بلکہ مدعی کے دعوی کی صحت اور قبولیت کے لیے یہ ضروری ہے کہ مدعی جس چیز کا دعوی کر رہا ہے اس کی جنس اور مقدار کو بھی بیان کر دے، مثلاً یوں کہے کہ فلاں پر میرے ہزار روپے ہیں یا ایک کلو آٹا ہے، کیونکہ مجہول چیز کا دعوی کرنا یہ غیر پر الزام ہے، (و) كذا إذا وكل المكاتب ثم عجز وعاد إلى رقه (أو المأذون) عبداً كان أو صغيراً (فـحـجـر عليه، أو الشريكان فافترقا أى تفاسخا الشركة فهذه الوجوه المذكورة (تبطل الوكالة سواء علم الوكيل) بذلك (أو لم يعلم) لأنه عزل حكمى، لأن بقاء الوكالة يعتمد قياما لأمر، وقد بطل بهذه العوارض.
اللباب فى شرح الكتاب: كتاب الوكالة، ج ۲ ص ۱۴۵)
الهداية كتاب الدعوى، ج ۳ ص ۲۰۸
(83) The imposition (of a claim) regarding an unknown thing is not established. Translation: Imposition is not established regarding an unknown thing. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that it is not correct to impose a thing on someone by claiming an unknown thing, until the claimant states the quantity and type. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, states that a claim that is unknown is not accepted; rather, for the validity and acceptance of the claim, it is necessary that the claimant specifies the type and quantity of the thing he is claiming, for example, he says: 'So-and-so owes me one thousand rupees' or 'one kilo of flour', because claiming an unknown thing is an imposition on another. (And likewise if the mukatab appoints an agent then becomes incapable and returns to slavery, or the ma'dhun—whether a slave or a minor—is placed under restriction, or the two partners separate i.e. dissolve the partnership, these mentioned situations invalidate the agency whether the agent knows or not, because it is a legal withdrawal, because the continuation of agency depends on the continuation of the command, and it is invalidated by these impediments.) (Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Kitab al-Wakalah, vol. 2 p. 145) Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 3 p. 208.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents another Hanafi legal principle: a claim that is unknown in its type and quantity cannot be imposed on another. The author explains that in court, a claimant must specify what he is claiming—both the type (jins) and the amount (miqdar). For example, one must say 'one thousand rupees' or 'one kilo of flour', not just make a vague claim. The reason is that an unknown claim is unjustly imposing a burden on the defendant. The text then repeats an example from the previous lesson about agency, but this appears to be a citation from Al-Lubab to illustrate that legal withdrawal also applies mutatis mutandis. The correct context is Kitab al-Da'wa (Book of Claims) from Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الإلزام (al-ilzam): The imposition of a claim or obligation on another person through a court claim.
المجهول (al-majhul): An unknown thing, unspecified in type and quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this principle, why is a claim for an unknown thing not accepted?
A) Because the court cannot adjudicate without evidence
B) Because it imposes an unjust liability on the defendant
C) Because the claimant must prove the claim immediately
D) Because the defendant has the right to remain silent

Key Takeaway
A claim must specify both type and quantity to be valid; an unknown claim cannot impose liability.

The Principle: A Claim Over an Unknown Thing is Invalid
Definition of a fundamental condition for a valid claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement that a valid claim must specify the type and quantity of the subject matter, and apply this principle to determine whether a given claim is acceptable.

Core Idea
A claim cannot impose an obligation if the subject matter is unknown; specify type and quantity.

This lesson examines a foundational principle from the Hanafi chapter of claims (da'wa). The author of al-Hidayah states that a claim over an unknown item cannot be realized in court. We will unpack the legal reasoning and conditions for a valid claim, emphasizing the necessity of specifying the type (jins) and quantity (qadr) of the claimed item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Read the Arabic principle: 'الْإِلْزَامُ فِى الْمَجُهُولِ لَا يَتَحَقَّقُ'. Understand that it means imposing an obligation regarding an unknown thing is not legally valid.
This is the core of the lesson.
2
Determine the Requirement
The commentary explains that for a claim to be valid, the claimant must state both the type (e.g., rupees, flour) and the quantity (e.g., 1000, one kilo). Without this, the claim is rejected.
This is a condition (shart) for validity.
3
Apply to Examples
Consider the examples: 'he owes me a thousand rupees' is valid; 'he owes me money' is not. Practice distinguishing known from unknown claims.
Both type and quantity must be specified.
4
Note Cross-Madhhab Difference
While the Hanafi school requires explicit type and quantity, other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may accept less precise descriptions if the item can be identified by description. This affects the practical ruling.
Know the Hanafi position for fatwa.

ترجمہ: مجہول چیز میں الزام تحقیق نہیں ہوتا۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ مجہول چیز کا دعوی کر کے کسی پر کوئی چیز لازم کرنا درست نہیں ہے، جب تک اس مدعی کے لئے مقدار اور جنس بیان نہ کرے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ مدعی کا دعوی مجہول ہونے کی صورت میں قبول نہیں، بلکہ مدعی کے دعوی کی صحت اور قبولیت کے لیے یہ ضروری ہے کہ مدعی جس چیز کا دعوی کر رہا ہے اس کی جنس اور مقدار کو بھی بیان کر دے، مثلاً یوں کہے کہ فلاں پر میرے ہزار روپے ہیں یا ایک کلو آٹا ہے، کیونکہ مجہول چیز کا دعوی کرنا یہ غیر پر الزام ہے، (و) كذا إذا وكل المكاتب ثم عجز وعاد إلى رقه (أو المأذون) عبداً كان أو صغيراً (فـحـجـر عليه، أو الشريكان فافترقا أى تفاسخا الشركة فهذه الوجوه المذكورة (تبطل الوكالة سواء علم الوكيل) بذلك (أو لم يعلم) لأنه عزل حكمى، لأن بقاء الوكالة يعتمد قياما لأمر، وقد بطل بهذه العوارض.

Chapter of Claims. (83) Imposition (of a claim) regarding an unknown thing is not realized. Translation: Imposition regarding an unknown thing is not realized. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that it is not valid to impose anything upon someone by claiming an unknown thing, until the claimant states the quantity and type for the claimed item. The author of Hidayah, by mentioning the above principle, is teaching that the claim of the plaintiff is not accepted if it is unknown; rather, for the validity and acceptance of the claim, it is necessary for the plaintiff to state the type and quantity of what he is claiming. For example, he says: 'So-and-so owes me a thousand rupees' or 'one kilo of flour'. Because claiming an unknown thing is an imposition upon another. (And similarly, if a mukatab appoints an agent, then becomes incapacitated and returns to slavery, or the ma'dhun, whether slave or minor, is interdicted, or two partners separate, i.e., dissolve the partnership, then these mentioned causes terminate the agency whether the agent knows or not, because it is constructive dismissal, since the continuance of agency depends on the existence of the matter, and it has been invalidated by these occurrences.) (Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Kitab al-Wakalah, vol. 2, p. 145) (Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 3, p. 208) (Page 128) And imposition regarding an unknown thing is not realized and not proven, because judgment by means of the unknown is not possible, therefore the plaintiff's claim will also not be proven due to the thing being unknown.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents principle (83) from Usool al-Hidayah in the chapter of claims (da'wa). The principle states: 'Imposition (of a claim) regarding an unknown thing is not realized.' This means that a claim must specify the subject matter with enough detail to make it known. The Hanafi school requires that the claimant state both the type (jins) and quantity (qadr) of the item claimed, because the purpose of a claim is to impose a legal obligation (ilzam) on the defendant, and imposing with an unknown item is impossible. The example given: saying 'he owes me a thousand rupees' or 'one kilo of flour' is sufficient. The commentary also cites a relevant ruling from the chapter of agency to illustrate the same concept: agency is terminated by certain events without the agent's knowledge, because the unknown state would make agency invalid. This reinforces the principle that unknown matters cannot be the basis of legal impositions.

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit in which a plaintiff asserts a right against a defendant before a judge.
الإلزام (al-ilzam): Imposition of a legal obligation; the purpose of a claim is to impose a right on the defendant.
مجہول (majhul): Unknown or unspecified in type or quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The claimant must be an adult Muslim.
B) The claim must specify the type and quantity of the subject matter.
C) The claim must be made in Arabic.
D) The defendant must be present.

Key Takeaway
A claim over an unknown item is invalid; the claimant must state both the type and quantity for the claim to be accepted in a Hanafi court.

The Condition of Full Disclosure in Legal Claims
Stating a principle about disclosure as a condition for claim validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi condition for claim validity and apply it by specifying what information about the claimed item must be disclosed.

Core Idea
A claim is only valid if the claimed item is identified to the maximum extent possible.

In Hanafi procedural law, a lawsuit cannot proceed unless the subject matter is clearly identified. This lesson presents the foundational principle that informing the court and opponent about the claimed item to the utmost possible extent is a condition for the claim's validity. You will learn what constitutes sufficient identification and how it plays out in practice, especially when the claimed item is a physical object.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Start by locating the core maxim: 'Al-i'lam bi aqsa ma yumkin shart fi sihhat al-da'wa' (Informing to the utmost possible is a condition for validity of the claim). Understand that this is a procedural condition, not a pillar of the act.
2
Define 'Informing to the Utmost Possible'
Break down what 'utmost possible' means: the claimant must mention the type (jins) and quantity (qadr) of the claimed item. For a specific physical object (ayn), this includes bringing it to court so it can be pointed to.
Type examples: dirhams, dinars, wheat. Quantity examples: number, weight, volume.
3
Connect to the Purpose of a Claim
Understand why this condition exists: the purpose of a claim is to compel the opponent to respond with proof (ilzam). Judgment on an unknown item is impossible, and testimony on an unknown item is inadmissible.
As cited in the passage from al-Ikhtiyar: 'al-da'wa li al-ilzam, wa al-qada' bi al-majhul ghayr mumkin'.
4
Apply to a Specific Scenario: 'Ayn'
If the claimed item is a specific physical object in the defendant's possession, the defendant must bring it to court. The claimant, witnesses, and judge can then point to it during claim, testimony, or oath. This is the most effective form of informing.
Pointing (isharah) is akin to placing a hand on the object.
5
Contrast with a Debt or Unspecified Item
For debts or generic items (e.g., 'I owe you 100 dirhams'), type and quantity suffice because the item is fungible. The physical presence is not required, but specification is still mandatory.
The principle adapts to the nature of the claimed item.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْإِعْلَامُ بِأَقْصَى مَا يُمْكِنُ شَرَطُ فِي صِحَّةِ الدَّعْوَى. ترجمہ: جتنا ممکن ہو سکے اطلاع کرنا دعوی کی صحت کے لئے شرط ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ دعوی کی صحت کے لئے اس مدعی بہ شے کا معلوم کرنا اور اس سے آگاہ کرنا جہاں تک ممکن ہو شرط ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ مدعی علیہ پر مدعی نے جس چیز کا دعوی کیا اگر وہ عین کے قبیل سے ہو تو مدعی علیہ کو مجبور کیا جائے گا کہ وہ اس مدعی بہ (عین) کو قاضی کی مجلس میں حاضر کرے، تا کہ دعوی پیش کرتے وقت یا گواہی دیتے وقت، یا قسم لیتے وقت اس کی طرف اشارہ کیا جا سکے، کیونکہ حتی الامکان مدعی به (عین ) سے آگاہ کرنا ضروری ہے، اور اشارہ آگاہی کی بہترین صورت ہے، اس لیے اسے حاضر کرے تا کہ اشارہ ممکن ہو اور اس مدعی بہ کی آگاہی حاصل ہو سکے۔ اشارہ کرنا اس طرح ہے جیسے کہ اس قَالَ: وَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ تَكُونَ الدَّعْوَى بِشَيْءٍ مَعْلُومِ الْجِنْسِ وَالْقَدْرِ لَّانَّ الدَّعْوَى لِلْإِلْرَامِ، وَالْقَضَاءُ بِالْمَجْهُولِ غَيْرُ مُمْكِنٍ، وَكَذَلِكَ الشَّهَادَةُ بِالْمَجْهُولِ لَا تُقْبَلُ. (الاختيار لتعليل المختار كتاب الدعوى شروط الدعوى وحكمها، ج ۲ ص ۱۱۰) قَالَ (وَلَا تُقْبَلُ الدَّعْوَى حَتَّى يَذْكُرَ شَيْئًا مَعْلُومًا فِي جِنْسِهِ وَقَدْرِهِ إِلَخُ قَدْ ذَكَرْنَا أَنَّ مَعْلُومِيَّةَ الْمُدَّعِى بِهِ شَرُطٌ لِصِحَّةِ الدَّعْوَى فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ ذِكْرِ مَا يُعَيِّنُهُ مِنْ بَيَانِ جِنْسِهِ كَالدَّرَاهِم وَالدَّنَانِيرِ وَالْحِنْطَةِ وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ، وَقَدْرِهِ مِثْلُ كَذَا وَكَذَا دِرُهَمًا أَوْ دِينَارًا أَوْ كُرًّا ، لأَنَّ فَائِدَةَ الدَّعْوَى الْإِلْزَامُ بِإِقَامَةِ الْحُجَّةِ، وَالْإِلْرَامُ فِي الْمَجْهُولِ غَيْرُ مُتَحَقِّقِ. (العناية: كتاب الدعوى، ج۸ ص۱۵۷) الهداية كتاب الدعوى، ج ۳ ص ۲۰۹
Informing to the utmost possible is a condition for the validity of the claim. Translation: Informing as much as possible is a condition for the validity of the claim. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that for the validity of the claim, knowing the claimed thing and informing about it to the extent possible is a condition. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if the claimed item is of the category of a specific physical thing (ayn), then the defendant will be compelled to bring that claimed physical thing to the court of the judge, so that at the time of presenting the claim, or giving testimony, or taking an oath, one can point to it. Because it is necessary to inform about the claimed physical thing as much as possible, and pointing is the best form of informing. Therefore, he should present it so that pointing is possible and information about the claimed item can be obtained. Pointing is like this, as he said: 'And it is necessary that the claim be for something known in type and quantity, because the claim is for enforcement, and judgment on the unknown is not possible. Similarly, testimony on the unknown is not accepted.' (al-Ikhtiyar li-Ta'lil al-Mukhtar, Kitab al-Da'wa, Shurut al-Da'wa wa Hukmuha, vol. 2 p. 110) He said: 'A claim is not accepted until he mentions something known in its type and quantity, etc. We have mentioned that the knownness of the claimed item is a condition for the validity of the claim. So it is necessary to mention what specifies it by stating its type, such as dirhams, dinars, wheat, etc., and its quantity, such as so many dirhams, dinars, or kurr, because the benefit of the claim is enforcement by presenting evidence, and enforcement in the unknown is not actual.' (al-Inayah: Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 8 p. 157) Al-Hidayah Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 3 p. 209.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents and explains a foundational principle of Hanafi procedural law: al-i'lam bi aqsa ma yumkin shart fi sihhat al-da'wa (informing to the utmost possible is a condition for the validity of a claim). The author starts by stating the principle in Arabic, then provides an Urdu translation and commentary. The key idea is that for a legal claim to be valid, the claimant must specify the claimed item as precisely as practically possible. If the item is a specific physical object (ayn) in the defendant's possession, the defendant is required to bring it to court so that it can be pointed to during the claim, testimony, or oath. Pointing is considered the most effective form of identification, akin to placing one's hand on the object. The supporting texts from al-Ikhtiyar and al-Inayah emphasize that the claim must mention the type (jins) and quantity (qadr) of the claimed item, because the purpose of a claim is to compel the opponent to respond with proof, and a judgment cannot be rendered on an unknown item. Similarly, testimony on an unknown item is inadmissible. This principle ensures that the judicial process is based on concrete, identifiable subject matter.

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or assertion made by a claimant (mudda'i) before a judge seeking a remedy.
إعلام (i'lam): Informing or disclosure; the act of making the claimed item known to the court and the opponent.
عين (ayn): A specific, identifiable physical object (as opposed to a debt or generic item).
إشارة (isharah): Pointing; the physical gesture used to identify the claimed item in court during claim, testimony, or oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The claimant must simply state the item's name.
B) The claimant must inform about the item to the utmost possible extent, specifying type and quantity.
C) The claimant must bring two witnesses.
D) The defendant must admit to the claim.

Key Takeaway
No claim is accepted unless the claimed item is identified as precisely as possible, ensuring the subject matter is known to all parties and the court.

Division Negates Partnership and Pointing for Oaths
Ruling with rational proof for oath procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a defendant must bring a movable claimed item to court for pointing during oath-taking and explain the rational proof based on the principle of informing to the utmost possible.

Core Idea
To eliminate ignorance in oath-taking, the defendant must physically produce a movable claimed item for pointing.

This lesson examines a procedural rule in Hanafi fiqh: when a claimant lacks witnesses, the defendant’s oath (yamin) must be performed with maximum clarity. The Hidayah establishes that if the claimed item is a physical movable in the defendant’s possession, he must bring it to court so that the judge, witness, and claimant can point to it. This requirement is derived from the principle that legal proceedings must eliminate ignorance to the fullest extent possible. We will explore the rational proof behind this rule and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Determine that the claimant has no witnesses, and the claimed item is a specific physical object (ayn) in the defendant’s possession and is movable (manqul).
If the item is immovable (e.g., land), the rule differs because it cannot be brought to court.
2
State the Ruling
The defendant is required (wajib) to bring the item to the court for pointing during the claim, testimony, and administration of oath (istihlaf).
Pointing (isharah) is a form of precise identification.
3
Understand the Proof
The condition is ‘al-i‘lam bi aqsa ma yumkin’ (informing to the utmost possible) to negate ignorance. Pointing achieves this best because it is like placing the hand on the item, which is the most effective method for movables.
This is a rational (aqli) proof; no specific Qur’an or hadith is cited.
4
Apply the Principle
For movables, since transportation is possible, pointing is mandatory. For immovables, pointing is done by describing the boundaries.
The rule ensures certainty in the oath procedure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين (۸۵) الْقِسْمَةُ تُنَافِى الشَّرُكَةَ. • ترجمہ تقسیم کرنا شرکت کے منافی ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جہاں تقسیم ہو یعنی ہر آدمی پر الگ الگ حکم ہو تو ان دونوں کا ایک میں شریک ہونا جائز نہیں ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر مدعی کے پاس گواہ نہ ہو تو فَإِنْ كَانَ الْمُدَّعَى بِهِ عَيْنًا فِي يَدِ الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ كُلِّفَ إِحْضَارَهَا إِلَى مَجْلِسِ الْحُكْمِ لِلْإِشَارَةِ إِلَيْهَا فِي الدَّعْوَى وَالشَّهَادَةِ وَالِاسْتِخَلَافِ لِأَنَّ الْإِعْلَامَ بِأَقْصَى مَا يُمْكِنُ شَرْطٌ نَفْيًا لِلْجَهَالَةِ، وَذَلِكَ فِى الْمَنْقُولِ بِالْإِشَارَةِ لأَنَّ النَّقْلَ مُمْكِنٌ، وَالْإِشَارَةُ أَبْلَغُ فِى التَّعْرِيفِ لِكَوْنِهَا بِمَنْزِلَةِ وَضْعِ الْيَدِ عَلَيْهِ. (العناية: كتاب الدعوى، ج ۸ ص ۱۵۸) (فإن كان عينا في يد المدعى عليه كلف إحضارها ليشير إليها بالدعوى) وكذا في الشهادة والاستحلاف حتى يقول الشاهد: إن هذه العين حقه وكذا في الاستحلاف لأن الإعلام بأقصى ما يمكن شرط وذلك بالإشارة فى المنقول لأن النقل ممكن والإشارة أبلغ في التعريف قوله (الجوهرة النيرة : كتاب الدعوى، ج ۲ ص ۲۱۰) أَمْكَنَ لِيُشَارَ إِلَيْهِ فِي الدَّعْوَى وَالشَّهَادَةِ لِأَنَّ الْإِعْلَامَ بِأَقْصَى مَا (وَطَلَبَ) عَطْفٌ عَلَى ذَكَرَ (إِحْضَارَهُ) أَى إِحْضَارَ مَا يَدَّعِيهِ إِنْ يُمْكِنُ شَرْطٌ وَذَا فِي الْمَنْقُولَاتِ بِالْإِشَارَةِ لِأَنَّهَا أَبْلَغُ أَسْبَابِ التَّعْرِيفِ. درر الحكام شرح غرر الحكام: كتاب الدعوى، ج ۲ ص ۳۳۰) الهداية كتاب الدعوى، باب اليمين ج ۳ ص ۲۱۱
Chapter of Oath (85) Division negates partnership. Translation: Division is contrary to partnership. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that where there is division, i.e., each person has a separate ruling, then it is not permissible for both to be partners in one. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if the claimant has no witness, then if the claimed item is a physical thing in the hand of the defendant, he is required to bring it to the court of judgment for pointing to it in the claim, testimony, and administration of oath, because informing to the utmost possible is a condition to negate ignorance, and that is in movables by pointing because moving is possible, and pointing is more effective in identification because it is like placing the hand on it. (al-Inayah: Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 8 p. 158) (If it is a physical thing in the hand of the defendant, he is required to bring it to point to it in the claim) and likewise in testimony and administration of oath so that the witness says: This physical thing is his right, and likewise in the administration of oath because informing to the utmost possible is a condition, and that is by pointing in movables because moving is possible and pointing is more effective in identification, his saying (al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah: Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 2 p. 210) 'it is possible to point to it in the claim and testimony because informing to the utmost' (and he requested) is conjunction on 'he mentioned' (bring it) i.e., bring what he claims if possible is a condition, and that in movables by pointing because it is the most effective means of identification. Durar al-Hukkam Sharh Ghurar al-Ahkam: Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 2 p. 330) Al-Hidayah Kitab al-Da'wa, Chapter of Oath, vol. 3 p. 211.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage covers two related matters. First, it introduces a principle (85): al-qismah tunafi al-shirkah (division negates partnership). The Urdu commentary explains that when a ruling is applied separately to each person, they cannot be considered partners in the same matter. This principle likely sets the stage for the procedure in oaths where the defendant is treated individually. The main legal content then addresses a specific scenario: when the claimant has no witnesses, the defendant may be required to take an oath. If the claimed item is a specific physical object (ayn) in the defendant's possession, the defendant must bring it to court so that it can be pointed to during the claim, testimony, and the administration of the oath (istihlaf). The reason given is the same as before: al-i'lam bi aqsa ma yumkin shart (informing to the utmost possible is a condition) to eliminate ignorance. For movable items (manqulat), pointing is the best method of identification, as it is akin to placing the hand on the item. The supporting texts from al-Inayah, al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah, and Durar al-Hukkam reiterate this rule, emphasizing that the physical presence of the claimed item enables precise pointing, which is the most effective means of identification. This ensures clarity in the oath procedure.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Division; the act of separating a joint matter into distinct parts, each with its own ruling.
يمين (yamin): Oath; a solemn declaration taken from the defendant when the claimant lacks witnesses.
استخلاف (istihlaf): The administration of an oath to the defendant by the judge; the process of requiring the defendant to swear.
منقول (manqul): Movable property; items that can be physically transported to court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, to eliminate ignorance in oath-taking, the defendant must physically produce a movable claimed item for pointing.

Why the Oath Is Not Returned to the Plaintiff: Division Negates Partnership
Explaining why the oath is not returned to the plaintiff using the principle of division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the oath is not returned to the plaintiff and derive it using the principle that division negates partnership and the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him).

Core Idea
The oath belongs solely to the defendant; the plaintiff cannot share it because the Prophet divided roles between them.

This lesson explores a key principle in Hanafi fiqh of judicial procedure: al-qismah tunafi al-shirkah (division negates partnership). We will see how this principle, combined with the famous hadith on evidence and oath, leads to the ruling that the oath is never administered to the plaintiff. Understanding this derivation sharpens your ability to apply hadith-based reasoning in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is taking an oath from the plaintiff in court. The ruling: it is not permissible (batil) to do so.
The ruling is explicitly stated in the Hidayah and its commentaries.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'Al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala al-mudda'alayhi.'
This hadith is recorded in Sunan al-Tirmidhi, hadith no. 1341.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Two arguments: (a) The hadith is a division (qasam) between plaintiff and defendant, and division negates partnership; (b) The word 'al-yamin' is generic (alif-lam jinsi), assigning the whole genus of oath to the defendant, leaving none for the plaintiff.
The generic article indicates the entire category, so no part of the oath can be given to the plaintiff.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
This ruling is agreed upon within the Hanafi school (Sahibayn agree) and is also the majority view; it is muttafaq alayh.
No intra-Hanafi dispute is recorded on this point.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The reliance on the explicit text and the logical principle makes this a clear ruling. The mufta bihi is that the oath is not returned to the plaintiff.
No further tarjih needed; the evidence is decisive.

The plaintiff puts his hand on this.
Chapter of Oath
(85) Division negates partnership.
Translation: Dividing is contrary to partnership.
129
Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that wherever there is division, i.e., each person has a separate ruling, then the two of them sharing in one is not permissible.
The author of Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, is explaining that if the plaintiff has no witness, then: 'If the claimed property is a specific asset in the hand of the defendant, he is required to bring it to the court for pointing it out in the claim, testimony, and oath, because informing to the utmost extent possible is a condition to negate ignorance, and that in movables is by pointing because moving is possible, and pointing is more effective in identification because it is like placing the hand on it.'
(Al-'Inayah: Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 8, p. 158)
(If it is a specific asset in the hand of the defendant, he is required to bring it to point to it in the claim) and similarly in testimony and oath, so that the witness says: 'This specific asset is his right', and similarly in oath, because informing to the utmost extent possible is a condition, and that is by pointing in the case of movables because moving is possible and pointing is more effective in identification – his statement (Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah: Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 2, p. 210) it is possible to point to it in the claim and testimony because informing to the utmost extent is a condition (and he demanded) is wa-'ataf on dhakara (he mentioned) (its bringing) i.e., bringing what he claims if possible is a condition, and this in movables is by pointing because it is the most effective means of identification. (Durar al-Hukkam Sharh Ghurar al-Hukkam: Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 2, p. 330)
Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Da'wa, Bab al-Yamin, vol. 3, p. 211
Usool e Hidayah Volume Three
130
The oath will not be returned to the plaintiff, i.e., an oath will not be taken from the plaintiff, because the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The evidence is on the plaintiff and the oath is on the defendant.' The meaning is that the witness is on the plaintiff and the oath is on the defendant.
The method of deriving evidence from this hadith is that in this hadith there is division: the witness is on the plaintiff and the oath is on the defendant. Now, if we take an oath from the plaintiff, then he would also partner with the defendant in the oath, whereas division is contrary to partnership, therefore an oath will not be taken from the plaintiff. Moreover, in the hadith, the word 'al-yameen' (the oath) is brought with the generic alif-lam, thereby assigning the oath exclusively to the defendant, and nothing remains beyond the genus. So when the genus of oath is reserved for the defendant, then no part of the oath remains for the plaintiff, and when no part of the oath remains for the plaintiff, then the oath will not be applied to the plaintiff either.
Sunan al-Tirmidhi: Abwab al-Ahkam, Bab ma ja'a fi anna al-bayyinata 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yameena 'ala al-mudda'alayhi, hadith no. 1341
He said: (And the oath is not returned to the plaintiff because of his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The evidence is on the plaintiff and the oath is on the defendant.' He made the genus of oath on the defendant, because he mentioned it with the alif-lam, and that negates its return to the plaintiff, and because it is a division, and division negates partnership, so there is no oath for the plaintiff. (Al-Ikhtiyar li Ta'lil al-Mukhtar: Kitab al-Da'wa, vol. 2, p. 111)
And his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The evidence is on the one who claims and the oath is on the one who denies' is a division, and division negates partnership, and he made the genus of oaths on the deniers, and there is nothing beyond the genus. (Al-Ghurar al-Munifah: Kitab Adab al-Qadi, vol. 1, p. 185) And the oath is not returned to the plaintiff because he (peace be upon him) divided between the two litigants so he made...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the principle 'al-qismah tunafi al-shirkah' (division negates partnership) from the Hidayah. The author explains that this principle is used to prove that the oath (yamin) is never returned to the plaintiff in a court case. The passage opens by stating the principle in Arabic and Urdu, then clarifies its meaning: wherever two parties have been assigned distinct roles, they cannot share in the same role. The author then shows how the Hidayah applies this to the case where the plaintiff has no witnesses. The main ruling is that the judge must require the defendant to bring the claimed property to court for pointing, but the crucial point is about the oath. The passage then moves to page 130: 'The oath will not be returned to the plaintiff.' The proof is the well-known hadith: 'The evidence is on the plaintiff and the oath is on the defendant.' This hadith, recorded by Tirmidhi, explicitly divides the functions — the plaintiff brings proof, the defendant takes the oath. The author provides two arguments from this hadith. First, the hadith itself is a division (qasam), and since division precludes partnership (shirkah), the plaintiff cannot share in the oath with the defendant. Second, the word 'al-yamin' in the hadith is used with a generic definite article (alif-lam jinsi), which designates the entire genus of oath to the defendant, leaving no part for the plaintiff. The passage concludes with quotes from authoritative Hanafi works (al-Ikhtiyar, al-Ghurar al-Munifah) reaffirming that the oath is not returned to the plaintiff because the Prophet (peace be upon him) divided between the two litigants.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): The allocation of distinct roles or shares; here the division between plaintiff and defendant in the hadith.
شركة (shirkah): Partnership or sharing in a joint matter; the principle says division and partnership are incompatible.
يمين (yamin): The oath taken in court, required of the defendant to deny the claim.
مدعي (mudda'i): The plaintiff or claimant who brings a case against another.
مدعى عليه (mudda'alayh): The defendant against whom the claim is made.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically witnesses, that the plaintiff must produce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle 'al-qismah tunafi al-shirkah', why is the oath not returned to the plaintiff in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the plaintiff is the one who brings evidence.
B) Because the hadith assigns the oath to the defendant, and division precludes the plaintiff from sharing in it.
C) Because the judge has discretion.
D) Because the plaintiff is always truthful.

Key Takeaway
The oath is exclusively for the defendant because the hadith divides roles and division precludes partnership.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
The Oath is Not Returned to the Plaintiff
Explaining the Hanafi ruling on oath allocation between plaintiff and defendant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oath allocation and explain the proof from the hadith using the concepts of division (qasam) and genus (alif lam li al-jins).

Core Idea
The oath belongs exclusively to the defendant—division precludes sharing.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, the oath (yamin) is a powerful tool assigned solely to the defendant. This lesson unpacks the ruling from a key hadith, showing how linguistic and logical inferences from the Prophet's words create an exclusive division that bars the plaintiff from ever taking the oath. We will explore two angles of reasoning: the division of roles and the definite article for genus.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Begin by articulating the Hanafi position clearly: the oath is never administered to the plaintiff, only to the defendant.
This is the reported position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the primary proof
Locate the hadith in Sunan al-Tirmidhi: 'Al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala al-mudda'a 'alayhi.' Recognize its authority as a hadith ahad.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but qat'i al-dalalah in the Hanafi view.
3
First inference: division precludes partnership
Understand that the hadith imposes a division (qasam) between the two parties. If the plaintiff could also swear, he would share the oath with the defendant, violating the division.
This is logical: division and partnership are mutually exclusive.
4
Second inference: definite article indicates exclusivity
The word al-yamin uses the definite article for the genus (jins). This fixes the entire category of oath to the defendant, leaving nothing for the plaintiff.
This linguistic argument is central to Hanafi usul.
5
Conclude: no return of oath
Combine both inferences to affirm that the oath cannot be returned to the plaintiff under any circumstance.
This settles the ruling as mufta bihi.

سنن الترمذي: أبواب الأحكام، باب ما جاء في أن البينة على المدعي واليمين على المدعى عليه، رقم الحديث: ۱۳۴۱
قَالَ: (وَلَا تُرَدُّ الْيَمِينُ عَلَى الْمُدَّعِى لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ الْبَيِّنَةُ عَلَى الْمُدَّعِي، وَالْيَمِينُ عَلَى الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ جَعَلَ جِئْسَ الْيَمِينِ عَلَى الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ، لِأَنَّهُ ذَكَرَهُ بِالْأَلِفِ وَاللَّامِ وَذَلِكَ يَنْفِي رَدَّهَا عَلَى الْمُدَّعِي، وَلَأَنَّهُ قَسُمٌ وَالْقِسْمَةُ تُنَافِي الشَّرِكَةَ، فَلا يَكُونُ لِلْمُدَّعِي يَمِينٌ . الاختيار لتعليل المختار: كتاب الدعوى، ج ۲ ص ١١١)
وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم البينة على من ادعى واليمين على من أنكر قسم والقسمة تنافي الشركة وجعل جنس الأيمان على المنكرين وليس وراء الجنس شيء. الغرة المنيفة : كتاب أدب القاضي، ج ۱ ص ۱۸۵) وَلَا تُرَدُّ الْيَمِينُ عَلَى الْمُدَّعِى لِأَنَّهُ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ قَسَمَ بَيْنَ الْخَصْمَيْنِ فَجَعَلَ
Principles of Hidayah, Vol. 3, 130. The oath will not be returned to the plaintiff, meaning the plaintiff will not be asked to take an oath, because the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'The proof is upon the plaintiff and the oath is upon the defendant.' The meaning is that witnesses are upon the plaintiff and the oath is upon the defendant. The method of inference from this hadith is that in this hadith there is a division: witnesses are for the plaintiff and the oath is for the defendant. Now if the plaintiff were to take an oath, he would also share in the oath with the defendant, while division precludes sharing. Hence the oath will not be taken from the plaintiff. Also, in the hadith, 'al-yamin' is mentioned with the definite article for genus, thereby fixing the oath exclusively to the defendant, and there is nothing beyond the genus. Therefore, when the genus of oath is specific to the defendant, no portion of the oath remains for the plaintiff, and when no portion of the oath remains for the plaintiff, the oath will not be administered to the plaintiff. Sunan al-Tirmidhi: Book of Judgments, Chapter on the hadith that proof is on the plaintiff and the oath on the defendant, Hadith number 1341. He said: (The oath is not returned to the plaintiff because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'The proof is upon the plaintiff and the oath is upon the defendant.' He made the genus of oath upon the defendant because he mentioned it with the definite article, and that negates returning it to the plaintiff, and because it is a division, and division precludes partnership, so there is no oath for the plaintiff. Al-Ikhtiyar li Ta'lil al-Mukhtar: Book of Claims, vol. 2, p. 111). And his statement, peace be upon him: 'The proof is on the one who claims and the oath is on the one who denies' is a division, and division precludes partnership, and he made the genus of oaths upon the deniers, and there is nothing beyond the genus. Al-Ghurra al-Munifa: Book of Judge's Etiquette, vol. 1, p. 185). And the oath is not returned to the plaintiff because he, peace be upon him, divided between the two litigants and made...
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the allocation of the oath in judicial proceedings. The Hanafi school rules that the oath (yamin) is never to be administered to the plaintiff (mudda'i); it is exclusively the defendant's (mudda'a 'alayhi) responsibility. The proof is the well-known hadith from Sunan al-Tirmidhi: 'The proof is upon the plaintiff and the oath is upon the defendant.' The commentary explains two angles of inference. First, the hadith establishes a division (qasam) between the two parties: the plaintiff provides evidence (bayyinah), and the defendant swears an oath. Division inherently precludes partnership (shirkah). If the plaintiff were also allowed to swear, he would be sharing in what is assigned to the defendant, undermining the division. Second, the word al-yamin is used with the definite article (alif lam) indicating the genus (jins). This makes the oath a category exclusively for the defendant — there is no portion left for the plaintiff. Therefore, the oath cannot be 'returned' to the plaintiff under any circumstance. This ruling is established in the Zahir al-Riwayah based on authentic hadith and is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
یمین (yamin): The oath administered in court to the defendant when the plaintiff lacks evidence.
مدعی (mudda'i): The plaintiff or claimant who brings a case and must provide evidence.
مدعی علیہ (mudda'a 'alayhi): The defendant who is being sued and may be required to take an oath.
قسم (qasam): The division of roles between plaintiff and defendant as established by the hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the oath not administered to the plaintiff in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the plaintiff is always truthful.
B) Because the hadith divides roles: the plaintiff brings evidence, the defendant swears, and division precludes sharing.
C) Because the defendant may be lying.
D) Because the plaintiff can choose to swear if he wants.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'al-bayyinah ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin ala al-mudda'a alayhi' establishes an exclusive division, so the oath cannot be returned to the plaintiff.

The Statement Belongs to the Possessor: A Key Procedural Principle
Stating general principle for prima facie evidence in disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle 'al-qawl li-sahib al-yad', identify the possessor in a dispute, and apply the principle to determine whose statement is accepted in property and contract disputes.

Core Idea
In a dispute, the possessor's statement prevails with an oath – possession is prima facie evidence.

When two parties dispute over property or a debt, and neither has clear evidence, how does a judge decide? Hanafi fiqh provides a clear procedural rule: the one who currently possesses the object (sahib al-yad) is given credence in his statement, provided he takes an oath. This lesson unpacks that principle and illustrates it with a classical example from salam contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Read the principle 'al-qawl li-sahib al-yad' and understand its translation: 'The statement is for the possessor.' The possessor is the person currently holding the disputed item.
This principle applies when the claimant lacks proof and the possessor's statement is plausible.
2
Identify the possessor in a scenario
Given a dispute, determine who physically controls the object or has the upper hand over the debt. That party is sahib al-yad. Their statement is accepted with an oath.
In the salam example, the seller (muslim ilayh) has the capital (dirhams) physically after iqala, so he is the possessor.
3
Apply to the salam contract example
Work through the example: A buyer advanced dirhams for a specified quantity of wheat. They mutually annulled (iqala). Then they disputed the amount of the capital. The seller (muslim ilayh) now holds the dirhams. According to the principle, his statement about the amount is accepted (with oath), and there is no mutual oath (tahaluf) because iqala in salam does not allow rescission, and the discharged debt does not revive.
The Hanafi texts emphasize that iqala is a dropping (isqat) of the debt, so once dropped, it cannot return.
4
Explain the rationale
Understand why the possessor is favored: possession provides a strong indication of a right; shifting the burden to the claimant is consistent with the rule 'al-bayyinatu 'ala man idda'a wa al-yaminu 'ala man ankara' (evidence is on the claimant, oath on the denier). The possessor is like the defendant, so he only needs an oath.
This principle combines practical fairness with legal consistency.

Source Text

تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ دعوؤں میں اختلاف کے وقت صاحب ید یعنی قَالَ (وَمَنْ أَسْلَمَ عَشَرَةَ دَرَاهِمَ إِلَخْ وَمَنْ أَسْلَمَ عَشَرَةَ دَرَاهِمَ فِي كُرِّ حِنْطَةٌ ثُمَّ تَقَايَلا ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفَا فِي الثَّمَنِ لَا يَتَحَالَفَانِ وَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْمُسْلَمِ إِلَيْهِ وَلَا يَعُودُ السَّلَمُ لأَنَّ فَائِدَةَ التَّحَالُفِ الْفَسْخُ وَالْإِقَالَةُ فِى بَابِ السَّلَمِ لَا تَحْتَمِلُهُ لِكَوْنِهِ فِيهِ إِسْقَاطُ الْمُسْلَمِ فِيهِ وَهُوَ دَيْنٌ وَالدَّيْنُ السَّاقِطُ لَا يَعُودُ.

Principle 92: 'The statement in claims belongs to the possessor.' Translation: In disputes, the statement of the one in possession (sahib al-yad) is considered authoritative. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that at the time of difference in claims, the one in possession is [as the quote says]: 'And whoever advanced ten dirhams for a kurr of wheat, then they annulled, then they disputed over the price: they do not perform mutual oath, and the statement is of the muslim ilayh, and the salam does not return, because the benefit of mutual oath is nullification, and iqala in the chapter of salam does not permit it because it is dropping the muslam fihi, and it is a debt, and the dropped debt does not return.' (from al-Inayah, Kitab al-Da'wa, Bab al-Tahalf, vol. 8 p. 227). And also: 'When they dispute – i.e., rabb al-salam and muslim ilayh – after iqala of the salam contract about the amount of the capital, they do not perform mutual oath, and the statement is of the muslim ilayh with his oath, and the salam does not return, because iqala in salam does not permit reversal, because it is a dropping, so it does not return.' (from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Kitab al-Da'wa, Bab al-Tahalf). al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Da'wa, Bab al-Tahalf, vol. 3 p. 222.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents principle 92: 'The statement in claims is for the possessor.' This is a fundamental principle in Hanafi procedural law. In any dispute over property or debt, the party who physically possesses the object in question is given credence in their statement regarding it, provided they take an oath. The commentary illustrates this with the salam example: after iqala, the seller (muslim ilayh) has possession of the capital (the dirhams) because he received them? Actually, the seller received the capital at the time of contract, but after iqala, he should return it. However, the dispute is about the amount he needs to return. Because he physically has the dirhams in his possession, he is sahib al-yad, so his statement is accepted. The quotes from al-Inayah and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq reaffirm that there is no mutual oath (tahaluf) in this case, and the salam does not return, consistent with the earlier principle.

Key Terms
صاحب اليد (sahib al-yad): The person who currently has physical possession or control of the object in dispute.
الدعوى (al-da'wa): A claim or lawsuit; a party's assertion of a right in court.
القول قول (al-qawl qawl): The statement is accepted (with oath) of a particular party.

C) The person in possession (sahib al-yad).
D) The defendant (mudda'a alayh) regardless of possession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when evidence is lacking, the possessor's statement with an oath determines the dispute—a fundamental application of the maxim 'al-qawl li-sahib al-yad'.

The Word of the Possessor is Accepted in Claims
Defines and expands the Hanafi maxim of possession-based presumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim regarding the possessor's statement in claims and apply it to disputes over property, including the spouse-specific exception.

Core Idea
The possessor's word is preferred in claims, but neutral items in a marriage go to the husband.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi procedural principle: that the person in possession of an item is legally presumed to be its owner unless proven otherwise. We will examine the maxim, its application in a Salam contract scenario, and its crucial exception in marital property disputes.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rationale
Recall the Arabic maxim: القول في الدعاوى لصاحب اليد. Understand that physical possession (yad) creates a presumption of ownership in legal disputes, giving the possessor the benefit of the doubt.
This is a procedural rule for judges.
2
Examine the Salam contract example
Look at the case where a buyer and seller disagree after iqala (rescission) over the price. The principle is applied to the seller as the possessor of the subject matter (the Salam capital), preventing mutual oaths and preserving the rescission.
The ruling that the seller's word is accepted prevents the return of the Salam.
3
Apply to the husband-wife dispute
Apply the principle to household items: gender-specific items go to the spouse they suit, neutral items go to the husband because his hand is stronger.
This shows the principle is not absolute—it yields to the husband's stronger possession.
4
Identify the exception
Note that items clearly specific to the wife (like jewelry) are excluded from the husband's possession presumption. This exception is based on custom and the nature of the items.
The exception prevents injustice.
5
Understand the underlying rationale
The principle rests on the idea that possession is apparent evidence of ownership and that the possessor's word is taken to avoid perjury and maintain order in claims.
Stronger evidence can overturn the presumption.

(92) The word in claims is for the possessor. Translation: In claims, the word of the possessor is accepted. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that when there is a dispute in claims, the possessor, meaning he said (And whoever advances ten dirhams... and whoever advances ten dirhams for a kurr of wheat, then they perform iqala, then they dispute over the price, they do not take mutual oaths, and the word is that of the seller, and the Salam does not return because the benefit of mutual oaths is annulment, and iqala in the chapter of Salam does not permit that because it involves dropping the subject matter which is a debt, and a dropped debt does not return. The word of the possessor will be accepted because the possession of the possessor can serve as apparent evidence of ownership. Therefore, when witnesses are presented by both sides, the word of the possessor gets preference due to possession. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a husband and wife dispute over household items, then items suitable for the wife belong to her, like jewelry; items suitable for the husband belong to him, like weapons, hats, etc.; and items usable by both, like utensils, belong to the husband because the wife and whatever is in her hand belong to the husband, and in claims the word of the possessor is accepted. Thus, in the mentioned case, the husband's word regarding the items is accepted, unlike items specific to the wife. Chapter: What two men claim. (Al-'Inayah, Kitab al-Da'wa, Bab al-Tahalf, vol.8 p.236) Or what is suitable for them, like utensils, gold, silver, goods, and real estate, it belongs to the man because the wife and what is in her hand belong to the husband, and the word in claims is for the possessor, unlike what is specific to her because the hand of the husband is stronger than hers. Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a definition and expansion of a key Hanafi procedural principle. It first states the maxim: in disputes, the word of the possessor is accepted. This means the person in physical possession of an item is given the legal benefit of the doubt. The commentary then provides a specific example from the Hidayah regarding Salam contracts and iqala, illustrating that the seller remains the possessor of the capital claim. It then applies the principle to a common marital property dispute: if spouses argue over household items, items are classified by gender suitability. Items fitting the wife are hers, fitting the husband are his, and neutral items belong to the husband because the wife and her possessions are under the husband's control. This application shows that possession determines the default assignment, with an exception for items clearly specific to the wife.

Key Terms
صاحب ید (sahib al-yad): The person in physical possession of an item; the possessor.
دعوی (da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit; a dispute over rights or property.

Possession (Yad) and the Husband's Priority in Spousal Property Disputes
Ruling from Hidayah on possession-based priority in spousal property disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of possession (yad) to determine ownership of common household items in a spousal dispute.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the husband's possession (yad) covers the wife — so common items are his.

When spouses dispute ownership of household items, Hanafi jurisprudence applies a possession-based presumption. The passage from Hidayah lays down a three-category classification, with common items going to the husband because his yad encompasses the wife's. This lesson unpacks the principle and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of items
The passage divides items into three types: those specific to the wife (e.g., jewelry), those specific to the husband (e.g., weapons, hat), and those common to both (e.g., vessels, gold, silver, goods, real estate).
The classification is based on usage and nature.
2
Understand the underlying principle: qawlu li-sahib al-yad
The general rule in claims is that the statement of the possessor is accepted because possession is prima facie evidence of ownership. This is a key Hanafi legal maxim.
Yad (possession) creates a presumption of ownership (zahir al-milk).
3
Apply the husband's extended yad
The passage states that the wife and what she holds are in the hand of the husband. Thus, for common items, the husband is considered the possessor, and his statement prevails.
This extension is unique to Hanafi fiqh; the husband's yad is treated as stronger.
4
Recognize the exception for wife-specific items
Items that are exclusively for the wife (like jewelry) are not covered by the husband's yad. Here, the wife's possession is not overridden because the item's specificity opposes the apparent ownership of the husband.
This exception prevents injustice; the wife retains items that are inherently hers.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi ruling is based on the husband's yad being stronger. The Shafi'i school may not extend the husband's yad over the wife to the same degree, leading to different outcomes for common items.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is in the distribution of common items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول ہدایہ ثالث
۱۳۸
قابض کا قول معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ صاحب ید کا قبضہ بظاہر ملک کی دلیل بن سکتا ہے، لہذا جب جانبین سے گواہ پیش ہوں تو قابض کے قول کو بسبب قبضہ ترجیح حاصل ہوگی۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر میاں بیوی کا گھر کے سامان میں اختلاف ہو گیا تو جو سامان عورت کے لائق ہے وہ عورت کا ہوگا ، جیسے زیور وغیرہ اور جو مرد کے لائق ہے وہ مرد کا ہوگا، جیسے اسلحہ ٹوپی ، چڑی وغیرہ، اور جو دونوں کے لئے قابل استعمال ہو ، جیسے برتن وغیرہ تو وہ مرد کے لئے ہوں گے، کیونکہ عورت اور عورت کے قبضہ میں جو کچھ ہے وہ شوہر کا ہے اور دعووں میں قابض کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی سامان میں شوہر کا قول معتبر ہوگا، بر خلاف ان اشیاء کے جو بیوی کے ساتھ خاص ہوں۔
(وَمَا يَصْلُحُ لَهُمَا كَالْآنِيَةِ) وَالذَّهَبِ وَالْفِضَّةِ وَالْأمْتِعَةِ وَالْعَقَارِ فَهُوَ لِلرَّجُلِ لِأَنَّ الْمَرْأَةَ وَمَا فِي يَدِهَا فِي يَدِ الزَّوْجِ، وَالْقَوْلُ فِي الدَّعَاوَى لِصَاحِبِ الْيَدِ) بِخِلَافِ مَا يَخْتَصُّ بِهَا لِأَنَّهُ يُعَارِضُ ظَاهِرَ الزَّوْجِ بِالْيَدِ.
(العناية كتاب الدعوى باب التحالف، ج۸ ص ۲۳۶)
أَوْ مَا يَصْلُحُ لَهُمْ كَالْآنِيَةِ وَالذَّهَبِ وَالْفِضَّةِ وَالْأمْتِعَةِ وَالْعَقَارِ، فَهُوَ لِلرَّجُلِ لِأَنَّ الْمَرْأَةَ وَمَا فِي يَدِهَا لِلزَّوْجِ، وَالْقَوْلُ فِي الدَّعَاوَى لِصَاحِبِ الْيَدِ، بِخِلَافِ مَا يَخْتَصُّ بِهَا لأَنَّهُ عَارِضٌ يَدُ الزَّوْجِ أَقْوَى مِنْهَا .
(رد المحتار مع الدر المختار كتاب الدعوى باب التحالف، ج ۵ ص ۵۶۴)
الهداية كتاب الدعوى، باب ما يدعيه الرجلان، ج ۳ ص ۲۲۵
The statement of the possessor will be accepted because the possession of the one in control can be evidence of ownership prima facie. Hence, when witnesses are presented by both sides, the statement of the possessor will have preference due to possession. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, is explaining that if a husband and wife have a dispute over household items, then an item suitable for the wife is the wife's, like jewelry etc., and an item suitable for the husband is the husband's, like weapons, hat, [chadar?], etc., and what is usable for both, like vessels etc., will be for the husband, because the wife and what is in her hand are in the husband's hand, and in claims the statement is for the possessor. So in this case too, the husband's statement regarding the items will be accepted, except for items specific to the wife. (What is suitable for them both, like vessels, gold, silver, goods, and real estate, it is for the man, because the woman and what is in her hand are in the hand of the husband, and the statement in claims is for the possessor) contrary to what is specific to her, because that opposes the apparent [ownership] of the husband with possession. (al-'Inayah, Kitab al-Da'wa, Bab al-Tahalf, vol. 8, p. 236) Or what is suitable for them, like vessels, gold, silver, goods, and real estate, it is for the man, because the woman and what is in her hand are for the husband, and the statement in claims is for the possessor, contrary to what is specific to her, because that opposes the husband's possession, which is stronger. (Radd al-Muhtar with al-Durr al-Mukhtar, Kitab al-Da'wa, Bab al-Tahalf, vol. 5, p. 564) (al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Da'wa, Bab Ma Yadda'ihi al-Rajulan, vol. 3, p. 225)
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hidayah, explained by the author of Usool-e-Hidayah. It establishes the principle of possession (yad) in property disputes. The general rule is that the statement of the possessor is accepted because possession is a prima facie evidence of ownership. The specific application here is a dispute between spouses over household items. The Hanafi ruling categorizes items into three types: (1) items specific to women (like jewelry) go to the wife, (2) items specific to men (like weapons) go to the husband, and (3) common items (like vessels) go to the husband. The reasoning is that the wife herself and what she holds are considered in the hand of her husband, so the husband's possession prevails. This is an application of the legal maxim al-qawlu li-sahib al-yad (the statement is for the possessor). The Arabic quotes from 'Inayah and Radd al-Muhtar reinforce the same ruling, emphasizing that the husband's hand is stronger.

Key Terms
قابض (qābiḍ): The person who physically holds the property; the possessor.
صاحب الید (ṣāḥib al-yad): The one in possession; the person whose hand controls the property.
ید (yad): Possession or control over property, often synonymous with ownership in disputes.
القول لصاحب اليد (al-qawlu li-sāḥib al-yad): The statement is for the possessor; a legal maxim giving evidentiary priority to the possessor in property disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule on spousal property disputes, who gets the household utensils (vessels) if both claim them?
A) The wife
B) The husband
C) Divided equally
D) Given to the spouse who possesses them at the time of dispute

Key Takeaway
In spousal property disputes, Hanafi law uses possession (yad) as a decisive factor, with the husband's yad covering the wife's, giving him common items.
